Is now to be strictly enforced. No more Sunday trading, no more sales of any kind—bar
tram tickets,, one can purchase them as usual. The law can prevent smokers from buying
but it cannot prevent them from puffing
CAMEO CIGARETTES.
So don’t be caught napping. Make. your purchases on Saturday night, or you will put in a

THE BED PAGE.
Richard Le Gallienne, the clever, conceited,
long-haired English minor-poet who cycles in
silk stockings and satin knickerbockers, with
lace ruffles at his wrists, and cultivates his
most intimate emotions for their spot-cash value,
has sold to an enterprising American magazine-
personanew version of Omar
or part thereof. Who invites comparison with
Edward Fitzgerald is a bold man, not to say a
rash one; but a modern minor-poet dares any-
thing—-and Hamer and Shakespeare are probably
trembling on their pinnacles. Le Gallienne’s idea
is not altogether new. Fitzgerald’s quatrains
have attracted many imitators, and a New York
writer, Edward Bryant, only the other day
printed some which might easily be mistaken for
Omar’s. Such as—
What if there be no Heaven of the Blest—
If death be nothing hut an endless rest?
Yet life is sweet in living, and the grapes
Of life into a golden oup are pressed.
And tho’ we may not see the Vintner’s hand
We fain must drink. And if the feast were planned.
Or if chance filled the oup, what matters it?
Must we go thirsting till we understand ?
For He who sets the wine-cup to our lip
Pities, perchance, our evil fate, that sip ;
And when the wine is bitter, in His grace
He sweetens it with pleasant fellowship.
But we, who may not toss the cup aside
Until at least its flavor we have tried,
If it be sweet or hitter, who shall say?
If't were best drink or spill it, who decide ?
The wine of life is drawn. Why curse the oup?
At worst’t is emptied at a single sup.
And since it may be never filled again
The taste is soon forgotten. Drink it up 1
And if the Drawer hath more wine fo-r three,
It shall not be a poorer quality.
Why, man,*t is he who bids thee to the feast l
And dost thou doubt his hospitality ?
* * * *
Hitherto imitators have frankly acknowledged
themselves imitators, with a proper humility
in presence of the great original. But Le
Gallienne hints no apologies to Fitzgerald;
and his editor hints that there is no need in re-
spect of work which will assure to its author ‘ a
leading place among living English poets.’ Com-
parison, rivalry, is invited instead of being
shunned ! And comparison is curiously difficult.
To a modern mind, the magic of Fitzgerald’s
quatrains is such that they are infiltrated and
absorbed by the mindstuff—join wi.th it in a
chemical, not a mechanical union. Critics lose a
first essential of criticism—the capacity of mental
aloofness, of making a clear distinction between
cause and effeot, action and re-action. Omar is
all-pervading as Brahma —
If the red slayer think he slays,
Or if the slain think he is slain,
They know not well the subtle ways
I keep, and pass, and turn agate . , .
They reokon ill who leave me out;
When me they fly, I am the wings ;
I am the doubter and the doubt,
And I the hymn the Brahmin sings . . ,
To successfully fight against prepossessions, in
fact, is to become a snake which swallows itself
entirely and remains the same snake. So most
people run away at the first encounter. The
revised version 'of the Bible is practically a dead
letter—because pious people prefer familiar error
to strange truth. There is no place like home, no
pio like mother used to make—because strange
places and new pies cannot win a verdict in a
prejudged case. As was explained a little while
ago, the brain-molecules which hoard impressions
arrange themselves in definite series, and after a
time- it is as physically difficult or impossible to
alter the series to suit a new impression as it is to
make water run uphill.
* * * *
And a prepossession breeds its own justification,
or its further justification. The moment any
object is_ differentiated by praise or blame from
other objects, that moment ordinary minds, by
dint of focussing attention, begin to see much
more to praise or blame (.than in reality exists.
Many of the women who adored, Mrs. Brown-
Potter were themselves far more deserving of
adoration ; but the public mind had not been
limelighted to their beauties. Much of Shake-
speare is rubbish which a modern writer
would be hooted for producing ; but Shake-
speare has been canonised by critical cen-
turies, and the average reader swallows
bad and good with equal rapture. To this day
students of English style are led by Macaulay’s
erroneous dictum to give their days and nights to
the imitation of Addison, regardless of the fact
that Addison’s style is habitually artificial and
monotonous, and that dozens of better models
are writing in the daily papers. And so
on to infinity. Of the boasted beauties of
classic masters in every branch of art, perhaps
half are real the remainder are factitious
and imaginary, but they pass for real because of
their classical environment. Thousands of the
beauties extolled in Homer and Virgil would by
the same commentators be counted commonplace
in Jones or Robinson. Thousands of the beauties
passed over in Smith are in Dante called divine,
because a reader of Dante is on the watch for the
divine, and a reader of Smith is not. Fame is a
rolling snowball, says the adage. So the pub-
lic, having been taught to admire Kipling,
reasons from the part to the whole with
illogical certainty, and professes to find even
Kipling’s nonsense glorious ; or having often
heard it repeated that ‘ Robbery Under Arms ’ is
one of the best Australian stories, buys ‘The
Sealskin Cloak ’ or ‘My Run Home ’ and con-
scientiously reads them under the impression that
it is enjoying itself. And, if it starts with a
sufficiently strong prepossession, probably it is
enjoying itself.
« • * *
But the moral of all this is that admirers of Le
Gallienne’s ‘ Omar ’ may say with justice that
those who prefer Fitzgerald’s are probably biased ;
or that, even if unbiased, the contemplation of
years has led them to prize in Fitzgerald beauties
of a class which they cannot see at all in Le
Gallicnne, because their spirits are nob attentive.
Nevertheless, the judgment is here ventured that,
so far as his performance is published, Le Gal-
lienne ranks only as an inferior imitator. One
might even call him a parodist; since he knows
nothing of Persian, and, though he has several
translations to sift from, and some three hundred
quatrains to choose from besides Fitzgerald’s, his
best stanzas are those in which he comes closest
to his model. After all, can one conquer a
strongly-fortified classic with mere knack of
pictorial phrasing ? Le Gallienne brings little
else to the assault. And the essence of Fitz-
gerald’s Omar is not the phrasing, but the philo-
sophy, which Le Gallienne lacks manhood
to appreciate and develop. For he is essen-
tially a feminine writer—a tinkler, prattler, em-
broiderer—and Hafiz’ lute would suit him better
than Omar’s sturdy pipe. There is a touch of
rugged maleness in Omar which looks at home
in armour, and ill at ease in silk attire.
‘Only two things matter in a man,’ says the
bride’s aunt in Gustave Droz’s ‘ Papa, Mamma,
and Baby ’ —‘ the look and the leg. George has the
look : I am sure he has the leg. You will tell
me, won’t you ? ’ Omar, one feels ssire, had the
look and the leg; and, judging from his letters,
Fitzgerald also. But one searches vainly for
either in the works and portraits of Le Gallienne.
• * • •
A few verses for comparison, choosing Le Gal-
lienne’s best. Here he is—
Spring, with the cuckoo sob deep in his throat,
O’er all the land his thrilling whispers float;
Old earth believes his ancient lies once more,
And runs to meet him in a golden ooat.
And many a lovely girl that long hath lain
Beneath the grass, out in the sun and rain,
Lifts up a daisied head to hear him sing,
Hearkens a little, smiles, and sleeps again.
Yea, love, this very ground you lightly tread,
Who knows I is pillow to some fair one’H head.
Ah ! tread upon it lightly, lest you wake
The sacred slumber of the happy dead.
And here Fitzgerald—
I sometimes think that never blows so red
The Rose as where some buried Csesar bled ;
That every Hyacinth the Garden wears
Dropt in her lap from some once lovely Head.
And this reviving Herb whose tender Green
Fledges the River-Lip on which we lean—
Ah, lean upon it lightly 1 for who knows
From what once lovely Lip it springs unseen.
Eliminate bias, as far as possible; forget that
Fitzgerald came first, and knew the Persian, and
translated for himself, and that Le Gallienne
comes second, and does not know Persian, and
borrows his translations; and say, is not Fitzgerald
incomparably stronger ? Concede Le Galdienne’s
feminine prettiness—for his verses are pretty ; but
is not his thought thinner, his line padded, and his
phrase sweetened tocloying? And, for all his para-
phrasing, has he reached Fitzgerald’s verbal charm
—the magic apposition and opposition—the deli-
cate wrapping of the message, fold on fold of mean-
ing ? No, he has not—this Richard the usurper.
* • • •
Or again, Le Gallienne—
The stream of life runs, ah 1 so swiftly by,
A gleaming race 'twixt bank and bank—we fly ;
Faces alight and little trailing songs ; -
Then plunge into the gulf, and so good-bye.
Of all my seeking this is all my gain—
No agony of any mortal beam
ShaH wrest the secret of the life of man ;
The Search has taught me that the Search is vain.
Yet sometimes on a sudden all seems clear—
Hush 1 hush ! my soul, the Secnet draweth near;
Make silence ready for the speech divine— ’
If Heaven should speak, and there be none to heari
Yea 1 sometimes on the instant all seems plain,
The simple sun could tell us, or the rain ;
The World, caught dreaming with a look of heaven
Seems on a sudden tip-toe to explain.
These again are pretty verses—particularly the
hrst and last—and worth treasuring ; but thev
do not carry the weight of Fitzgerald's nearest
analogues. And, despite his talent, Le Galli-
enne s workmanship is often weak and flawed.
Faces alight and little trailing songs ;
Then plunge into the gulf, and so good-bye—
is good ; but the preceding line—
A gleaming race 'twixt bank and bank—we fly—
is an awkward rhyme-eker. And the verse which
follows on the last quoted—
Like to a maid who exquisitely turns
A promising face to him who, waiting, burns
In hell to hear her answer—so the World
Tricks all, and hints what no man ever learns.
drops into very stale minor-poetrv. Fancy Fitz-
gerald passing a discordant line like
A promising face to him who, waiting, burns—
or the cheap exaggeration of “burns in hell”!
And so on, and so on. Conclusion : Icarus was
a promising young fellow, but his friends should
never have let him fly so near the sun.
* * * »
Intbe July ‘Cosmopolitan’ Prof. Peck has an
excellent article on ‘Modern Education.’ He
speaks of the ‘elegant sterility’ of English uni-
versities a century ago ; and says that as handed
down in America, until the German-led revival,
English scholarship wholly lost its elegance and
intensified its sterility. Unluckily we have had
in Australia no German-led revival, and there
could be no better examples of inelegant
sterility than Australian universities to-day.
It is with our universities, in fact, as with
our education departments : ignorance and
bigotry are rampant, and red tape easily
throttles any attempt at reform. Every year
bright minds are brought to the stagnant
educational pools which we miscall founts of
learning, and are remorselessly quenched by mulish
conservatism and official sloth. Occasionally, when
a mind brighter than usual disappears, a univer-
sity bemoans it as the Sydney University be-
moaned the late John Kinloch. “ Jack ” Kinloch
was a graduate of Sydney University, a scholar of
fair general attainments, and the best mathemati-
cian that the University has turned out. His talent
for mathematics, indeed, was quite exceptional,
and would have won him credit anywhere. When
Kinloch completed his course and received his
M.A. degree he was a candidate for University
employment as leoturer or assistant professor.
Here was a chance for the University to show
itself worthy of the affectionate title of alma mater,
and to give its brilliant son opportunity to further
distinguish himself and Australia. The Sydney
University, which habitually imports its professors
from Britain, and rarely encourages Australian
learning by promoting it to offices of profit and
trust, nobly refused the chance. Kinloch-became
a tutor ; for a time did well, and then did badly.
He tried general teaching ; but his speculations
failed—the very quality of mind which made him
such a promising scholar opposing his success in
business. He asked the University for a teacher-
ship or lectureship —to which his talents well
entitled him ; but the University was overstocked
with highly-paid foreigners, and could not make
room for one of its own sons, however deserving.
At the doors of member after member of the Uni-
versity Senate Kinloch knocked. Erom one he
got rebuffs; from another advice; from none
assistance. Yet Kinloch’s character was tho-
roughly good: he did not drink; he had no
vices. At last the University gave him the
trumpery office of esquire bedell. And the
the other. day Jaok Kinloch died, a broken-
hearted failure, and the Sydney University by
the mouth of its Chancellor grieved loudly for
the loss of one of its most brilliant sons ; while
Bernhard Wise extolled the inestimable value of
the University as a place for making acquaint-
anceships which are of lasting profit to a gradu-
ate (like Kinloch) through all his days. And that
is the disgraceful story of Sydney University and
John Kinloch, Master of Arts, mathematician,
and esquire bedell.
* * * *
Writes ‘ Paperknife ’—
Almost every dabbler in writing keeps, or has kept, a
kind of commonplace book, or collection of material,
and it seems to me that all are dissatisfied in that line
except myself. My idea is that it is best to do without
any memoranda at all, and next best with as little as
possible. Nothing is easier than to drift into the position
of George Eliot’sCasaubon, in ‘ Middlemarch,’ accumulating
materials for a great work, never begun. We have always
to remember we are not antediluvians. A Shakespeare,
with the Midas touch: even a Dickens or a Kipling: finds
all his time employed in making ‘copy.’ Yet a few
great workers have been slow, for our encouragement.
The celebrated mountains of Chas. Reade’s memoranda
were a dead weight on him. Much that Sala wrote proved
that he overdid the same line, uncongenial to his genius.
You get too much soaffolding, too little building.
My system has grown up in twenty years. Simply jot-
ting down one significant word, nevermore, keeping all in
alphabetical order, re-writing a bit every day continu-
ously. For ten years past Ido not think the mass has
increased at all, except immeasureably in value. On re-
vision, the mems. not on the nail are struck out. I never
forget what the word stands for. My useful mental pos-

Ishmael:• ‘But did you not notice that Wratislaw
adopts reading suggested for the envoy to Villon's
Ballad of the Gibbet”? His line, “Have naught to say
to them that with us vie ” translates “Ne soyez dono
de nostre confrairie.” “Them that with us vie’’— i.e.,
those who live (vivre—vie) with us—are of our company.
The meaning is strained, certainly; but that is the
meaning,*
The pick of the books recently sold from Sydney Pubtio
Library have been snapped up privately; the remainder
w’ere re-sold at auction last week, and realised, in the
absence of competition, very low prices. Barrington's
‘ History ’ brought 245., Wentworth’s ‘ Description,' 205,.
Labillardi&re’s * Voyage,' 205.; and so on. If these values
bear any near relation to those received by the Library,
Mie folly of the trustees must be evident even to them-
selves ; since they probably paid several times as much
for suoh books in the first instance, and the copies are
worth to New South Wales several times as much for
preservation to posterity.

STRONG AS DEATH.
O Death, when thou shalt come to me
From out thy dark, where she is new.
Come not with graveyard smell on thee
Or withered roses on thy brow.
Come not, O Death, with hollow tone.
And soundless step, and clammy hand—
See, I am now no less alone
Than in thy desolate, doubtful land ;
But with that sweet and subtle scent
That ever clung about her (such
As with all things she brushed was blent) ;
And with her quick and tender touch.
With the dim gold that lit her hair.
Crown thyself, Death ; let fall thy tread
So light that I may dream her there
And turn upon my dying bed.
And through my chilling veins shall flame
My love, as though beneath her breath ;
And in her voice but call my name,
And I will follow thee, O Death.
H. C. Bunner.
BALLADE OF OLD SONGS.
Where is each dim and vanished air—
Brave tunes of youth and of yesterday >
Catches that once seemed wondrous fair,
Yet ,like the years have fleeted away :
Where now does Annie Laurie stray,
Or Annie Rooney see*; her Jb,
Ellen Adair, and Auld Robin Gray ?
And where are the songs of long ago?
Where are that jovial, dashing pair,
The Bold Gendarmes of Brabant gay?
And where sweet Alice, the maiden rare
Whom ‘ Trilby ’ wanned from her lifeless clay?
Do Babies on the Block still play?
For Champagne Charlie vintage fl >w ?
But where are the Gallants of Eng and, say ?
And where are the songs of long ago ?
The Heart Bowed Down with grief and care
With Sally of Paradise Alley may
Part Strangers Yet, Upon the Stair,
While Coster Joe does his little lay.
At Fimiegan’s Wake the Vicar of Bray
May hear the Hen Convention crow
At Judas turning to save his pay
And where are the songs of long ago ?
ENVOI.
People, ask not of them I pray ;
Fdr song or singer, maid or beau,
So few survive old Time's affray—
And where are the songs of long ago?

Bards of the Backblochs.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
LOST LOUISE.
Quarranyarran is a peaceful
Township sleeping in the west;
Its inhabitants an easeful
Folk whose aim in life is—Rest.
Pride of heart, which sets a task for
Ardent souls, they do not know ;
Rest alone is all they ask for
In this vale of tears below.
They are neither worn nor weary,
They have merely work eschewed ;
Living comatosely-cheery
Lives of sunny lassitude.
Foolishly, in ant-like fashion,
Other folk may rush around,
Twist their threads of life in passion,
Seeking—what is never found.
That is—howsoever barren
Some might think the life led there—
Not their style in Quarranyarran,
Not their manner debonair.
Bruit of war, and news of battle,
If they hear —in their profound
Interests in sheep and cattle
Quietly the news is drowned.
Very quiet and pacific
Is the pub wherein the wise
Ancients meet, and soporific
Stories tell, and lazy lies.
Yarns of how at Ginginarum
Station, in the days of old,
Squatter Morgan kept a harem,
Black but comely to behold.
And the tale of Mrs. Grainger,
Who—it was a famous freak—
Dressed, and kept Jim Flood, bushranger,
As her “ cousin ” for a week.
Jim was young, with face unhairy,
And a girlish sort of look,
So that when she called him “ Mary ”
Everyone his sex mistook.
Then, to heights of fiction soaring,
Would the tale rise, and describe
How “Miss Mary ” had adoring
Suitors after her—a tribe ;
And how Sub-Inspector Carey,
On the hunt tor Jimmy Flood,
Told his mission to “Miss Mary,”
Vowing he would have Jim's blood.
Also, how some late carousers,
Dropping in at Grainger’s, saw
Sweet “ Miss Mary ’’—clad in trousers !
Feet on table, pipe in jaw !
Yarns of all sorts—mostly barren—
Yet, a jewel amongst these
Once I heard at Quarranyarran—
’Twas the tale of Lost Louise.
Long ago, ere engines fuming
Took the place of coaches bravo,
Lou lived there, in beauty blooming,
Like a rose upon a grave.
Suitors came with love-professions
From all corners of the land,
Swore they’d give their whole possessions
Just to kiss (and keep) her hand.
Squatters, doctors, bankers, lawyers,
They were down upon their knees,
All the country-side top-sawyers
At the feet of fair Louise.
All in vain : her heart, though tender,
Unto them was hard as stone,
Yet was ready to surrender
To some Picturesque Unknown
To some Fairy Prince ideal
Plumed and crested riding by.
“lam tired of rough and real
Back-blocks lovers ! ” was her cry.
“ They offend my sense aesthetic,
Evermore in vain I pine
For a soul superb, poetic,
Fearless—fit to mate with mine.”
Came a circus celebrated
Unto Quarranyarran town,
And a great furore created—
Worked up largely by the Clown.
Yet the merest rank outsider
Knew the Combination’s star
Was the famous bare-back rider,
Don Alonzo de Bivar.
Swarthy, supple, with dark-streaming
Hair, the proud Equestrian King,
In a suit of spangles gleaming,
Like a whirlwind scoured the Ring.
Three nights passed, and—then a wonder—
Lovers, riding as you please,
Yelling, cursing “ H- 1 and thunder !
Where the blazes is Louise ? ”
She was gone—like mists of morning
When the sun springs up to view—
She was gone, sans word of warning,
And a large portmanteau, too.
Thus the yarn—and it was well borne
Out by facts, for, if you please,
Whom should I meet last in Melbourne
But Alonzo and Louise !
Yes, Alonzo—first-class fellow
I discovered him to be —
And Louise—as ripe and mellow
As a pear dropped from a tree.
Both informed me what the game was—
Don Alonzo was a myth,
Lou said that her actual name was
Mrs. W. Bilger Smith.
I have been of late no courier
Of the.night, yet—Lord knows why—
We had supper—oysters, porter,
Bilger Smith, Louise, and I.
And she told me, “ If you dare to
(While I saw the oysters wince)
You can tell them if you care to
I have found my Fairy Prince.”

SATURDAY, JULY 31, 1897.
Price, (sd.
A number of clergymen, ladies, and laymen
had meantime assembled in the Executive cham-
ber. This was the most formidable deputation
of the three. Mr. BrUnker knew most of them.
He was cheerd when he entered the room. In /
response he shook several of the gentlemen by, '
the hand.
The
speakers dwelt on the importance of Sunday as a
day of rest, and attributed the greatness of Eng-
land to It.
Mr. Brunker expressed his entire sympathy
with the deputation. If the working classes
were deprived of their day of rest, it was hard
to predict what the result would be. He hoped
that this country would yet become as great as
the dear old land. And it could do so onlf by a
Close observance of the Sunday. He hope# that
the rising generation would always be taught to
observe not only the laws of their counttfaF but
tho laws of God. Vt

By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty
Shillings sterling—with postage added where required—

you can have The Bulletin posted regularly to you every
■week for a year.

to he addressed to William Macleod, Managing Director.

Archibald.
Special Notice.
The whole contents of this newspaper are copyright by
the publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
‘ Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted—
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding tir i columns in
length, or, say, SOW words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. ill
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission
To Amateurs in Black and White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original humorous or
other topical drawings in line or wash, of sufficient merit
and interest and adapted for direct reproduction, or any
interesting photograph illustrative of a current event,
will be paid for same on acceptance. Any picture con-
taining a good idea, but unfitted for direct reproduc-
tion—i.e., any picture ivhich has to be re-drawn —will be
paid for as a suggestion only. The conditions under
which such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

Notice to Subscribers in Victoria, S. Australia,
Westralia and Jlaoriland.
In consequence of the imposition of One Halfpenny
postage on Interprovincial Newspapers, the annual sub-
scription outside N.S. Wales (except Queensland) is now
£l, with 3s. 3d. added for postage. England £l, with
6s. 6d. added for postage.
No newspaper can be sent unless the subscription is
prepaid. There will be no exception to this rule.
To Subscribers in Queensland.
Owing to the imposition of l\d. postage, the subscription
to. The Bulletin to Queensland will be £1 6s. 6d. per
annum, payable in advance.
To ease off in some degree the burden of the unreasonable
postage— on a Bulletin of ordinary weight—now
exacted by the Queensland Post Office, a special Thynne-
paper edition of The Bulletin is now sent to Queensland.

SATURDAY, JULY 31, 1897.
“The Abundance which God Provides.”
Mr. G. H. Reid, in opening- the bridge at Sunlight, de-
clared that he had staked his political fortunes in in-
ducing New South Wales to emulate the policy which had
made England great. It would be suicidal for England,
asserts Mr. Reid, to imitate nations who were so mad as
to try and exclude the abundance which God provides.—
Cablegram.
Premier Reid, as a fragment of the God-
fearing Ministry, is developing an appro-
priate tendency to work off sentiments with
a streak of mild snuffle about the Deity in
them. The Deity, in New South Wales is,
and long has been, the particular property of
the Calico-J immy party A few years ago,
when the sectarian drum was wallopped more
furiously than it is to-day, and when the
Kiama spectre still survived, the party
organs were wont to publish articles setting
forth that Christ was a Freetrader, and that
Jehovah was especially wroth at Protective
duties, and that the Almighty, on His ever-
lasting throne somewhere beyond the
Magellan Clouds, took a personal interest in
the ephemeral ants that brawled down York-
street way over small matters relating to
Shoddy. This phase of the fiscal question
has partially died away of late years, but in
a general sense it is still understood that
God belongs especially to the great Foreign-
trade party, and that Brunker and He look
after things jointly. A tendency to tag
on the name of the Creator to little
party phrases is ingrained in the in-
telligence of the Reid following ; it in-
herited the Creator from Parkes, whose
political stock-in-trade He used to be. The
beautiful remark quoted above is a whiff of
the old party atmosphere as it was in the
good old days before Brunker and Garrard
brought their little minds to bear on the
religious aspect of politics, and created a
general idea that Providence is something
about the size of a hat-box, and that its chief
mission is to go round looking for people
who sell bananas on Sunday.
Yet Premier Reid, as a pious phrase
maker, is not a success. Freetrade he detines
. the free admission (if “the abundance
which God provides.” Protection is a mad-
tess which consists in an impious attempt to
shut out the abundance which God provides.
And the Reid practice doesn’t square with
the Reid theory worth a cent God made
abundance of wheat and sugar and tea and
corn and other things, therefore Reid lets
some of them in free and others he does not.
He also made abundance of Chinamen, and
Reid levies a hideous protective tax of £‘loo
on each divinely-created Chow to keep him
out. He made abundance of niggers, and
Reid gammons that his mission to England
is to get the Imperial sanction for their
exclusion He made abundance of microbes,
and under the auspices of the Reid Ministry
a rigid system of quarantine is enforced
whenever it seems that there is any chance of
this equally Divine product getting access to
New South Wales. The politician whose
fiscal creed is based on the theory that what
God created it is impiety for mere man to
shut out, and who yet tries to rigidly exclude
the nigger, and the Chinaman, and the germ,
and who restricts the free admission of the
dog, and tries to shut out the rabbit and
many other animals, is a poor, weak creature,
without the courage of his convictions. His
theory may seem pious enough, but his prac-
tice is sinful and wholly lamentable.
* * * *
Further, even the Reid Government can
hardly allege that the Lord made calico, and
shoddy, and blankets which profess to be
wool but are not, yet its policy is to admit
these freely ; while tobacco, even if it ap-
pears in its raw state, just as it came from
the hand of the Creator, is not allowed free
access, but is heavily taxed. Again, it is an
acknowledged fact that imports have to be
paid for by exports, therefore the free admis-
sion of other people’s goods, eve* from the
most pious motives, involves a free exporta-
tion of our own in exchange, and “the
abundauce which God provides ” has to be
shovelled out of the country in immense
quantities as a natural consequence of
the other arrangement. Between refus-
ing to let the Divine blessings into the
country and sending the same bless-
ings out of the country, the difference,
from a religious point of view, is hardly ap>-
preciable. The principal boast of the Reid
Government is that last year £20,000,000
worth of the products which a beneficent
Providence bestowed on New South Wales
were packed up tightly in bales by a par-
ticularly blasphemous section of the com-
munity, and laboriously humped on board
ship, and sent away in exchange for filthy
lucre, and rum, and bananas that are sold on
Sunday by Sabbath-breaking shopkeepers,
and tickets in Tattersall’s sweep, and other
gratifications of the flesh. And the things
which were not packed up in bales were
mostly dug out of the ground, and heaped
into the coffin-ship which mysteriously com-
busts on the road to Callao and is no more
heard of. If this callous chucking - out of
the abundance which God provides squares
with Premier Reid’s pious philosophy, then
it must be a very accommodating philosophy
indeed.
* * * *
The Bulletin respectfully suggests that
the Deity be altogether left out of the fiscal
question as an unknown quantity, and that
the matter be relegated to the community to
settle the best way it can, according to the
common-sense with which it has not been too
abundantly provided. The bald assertion
that if God were on earth He would be a
Freetrader, or any other brand of purely local
politician, settles nothing, and the spectacle
of any small, casual individual making politics
for his God is a very poor species of drivel.
Almost any political faith can be supported
with equal facility by some variety of pious
snuffle or other. To levy a protective tax
on the abundance which God created looks
impious enough when the statement is
delivered with a serious countenance, and
uttered impressively through the nose by a
man in a black coat and a tall, murky hat.
To’ shut out the Chinaman and the other
Asiatics whom He created is equally horrible;
if the Bill which Georue Reid is asking the
British Government to endorse becomes law,
then George Reid’s Asiatic Redeemer,
should He come back to this earth as the
same Syrian apostle He was aforetime, would
be refused admission to New South Wales as
an alien To tax the land which Jehovah
created is equally impious , likewise to tax
the man whom He created. And when the
man whom He created—the bulgy little man
with an eyeglass and a snigger and a deficit—
becomes so proudly cocksure about what his
Deity intended should be done with the
abundance aforesaid, there are certain draw-
backs even about that. In any case, the
difference between a nation shutting out the
said abundance because it can’t afford to pay
for it, and an individual doing likewise, is
imperceptible. When G. Reid bought and
ate sixpenn’orth of confectionery out of a
shop his conduct in not buying all the con-
fectionery on the premises doesn’t seem
any more justifiable than the action of the
community which doesn’t buy a million tons
of wheat from some other community. There
is an equally good or equally bad reason in
either case.
* * * *
This paper objects to self : styled God-
fearing Ministries for a number of reasons.
One is that it objects to the theory that such
slow, dull, ordinary persons as Garrard,
Gould, Brunker and Co. are the chosen in-
struments of any Deity. Another is that it
has a certain wholly unorthodox religion of
its own, and the locally-manufactured Deity
who is constructed as a reason why M'MiLLAN
should get his calico in free of duty is too
small for its liking. Still another is that
there is nothing on this earth, good, in-
different, bad, or utterly horrible, that can’t
be supported and endorsed and justified by
some plausible snuffle or other, and iO
amount of orthodox piety can make Free-
trade any better or worse or any more ir
less logical than it was before. The case
which is supported by many Scriptural quo-
tations, as Freetrade is in New South Wales,
isn’t necessarily a bad case on that account,
but it is a case that is in very suspicious
company. It associates with the case for
the burning of heretics, and the one for
the damnation of unbaptised infants, and
the one for war and pious fraud and the sale
of rum to the heathen and other things of the
kind, and none of these can be classed as
elevating society.
The Bogan Bridge at Monkey.
During the year ended 30th June, the N.S.W.
Government got through .£1,548,105 of borrowed
money. This was nominally expended on
“permanent and reproductive works,” that
being the only kind of works which are sup-
posed to be paid for out of loans. Works
which yield no revenue, and which are so tem-
porary that only the present generation will
get the benefit of them, it is considered a fair
thing that the present generation should pay
for ; to hand down the bill but not the assets
to posterity is manifestly mere swindling and
confidence trick. In New South Wales, how-
ever, the terms “ permanent and reproductive ”
are very elastic, and they are growing a trifle
more elastic every year. At the present rate
of progress, the time ought to come shortly
when pensions, and sick-pay, and the wages of
the hangman, and the civil servant’s salary, and
the Jubilee illuminations, and the official screw
of the Premier will all be called permanent and
reproductive works, and be handed down as a
permanent and unreproductive liability to that
patient, comprehensive ass, posterity.
♦ * *
The following are some of the items which
the Reid Government included among the
“permanent and reproductive” undertakings
for the past year, and paid out of loan funds
accordingly :
Improving entrance Nambucca River ... ... £2,716
Warlike Stores ... ... ... ... 336
Improving the navigation of Darling and Mur-
rumbidgee Rivers ... ... ... ... 642
Wood-paving portion of Sydney tramway-lines ... 2,549
Road to Wombeyan Oaves ... ... ... 114
Military magazines ... ... ... ... 2,709
Submarine mines ... ... ... ... 46
Fortifications ... ... ... ... ... 1,233
Snagging streams flowing into Richmond River 1,332
Naval stations . ... ... ... ... 5,720
Dredging, &c., Manning River... ... ... 10,803
Defence works, armaments, etc. ... ... 1,054
Clearing scrub... ... ... ... ... 36,678
Dredging, etc., Richmond River ... ... 19,529
Do. Nambucca do. ... ... 497
Do. lieilinger do. ... ... 578
Do. Tweed do. ... ... 1,506
Do. Cook’s do. ... ... 37,436
Straightening Styx and Throsby Creeks ... 6,633
New lighter ... ... ... ... ... 564
Altering old dredges ... ... ... ... 2,201
Repairing old bridge, Windsor ... ... ... 1,630
Do. do. Guudagai ... ... 6,148
Wood-blocking Western Road, Glebe ... ... 2,370
Compensation for cancellation of lease ... ... 13
Thinning-out forests ... ... ... ... 11,001
Dredging, etc., Macleay River ... ... ... 10,829
Landing silt from dredges ... ... ... 6,953
Repairing and extending wharf ... ... 4,700
New roof for Museum . ... ... ... 642
Repairing Government House ... ... ... 1,000
Wood-blocking in King-street, Newtown ... 3
Alterations to old dredges ... ... ... 13,180
Towards Governor Phillip’s Statue ... ... 260
* * * *
These are a few samples taken at random
from a very long list. None of them are repro-
ductive ; there is not among them all one penny
of direct revenue to be obtained. None of
them are permanent ; as a matter of fact, no
public works ever constructed were really per-
manent, but those to which the term can fairly
be applied are such as the railways, which pay
for their renewal and repair out of earnings, so
that when once constructed they are self-sup-
porting. But the wood-blocks will have to be
replaced in a dozen years or less; and the
streams that are snagged will need to be
snagged over and over again ; and the rivers
that are dredged will want to keep on being
dredged permanently, else they will silt up
again , and the forests that were thinned will
want to be thinned again and again ; and the
guns will presently wear out, and the powder
explode, and the old bridge that is repaired
will want to be repaired some more, and the
ancient Museum will require another roof
by-and-bye. None of these pay anything
at all towards their own maintenance. And
even if the “ scrub - clearing, £36,378,”
does increase the value of the lands by
that amount, the added value, when they
are sold, will go into revenue. Barring Go-
vernor Phillip’s statue, which can hardly
come round again, almost every item is as much
a recurring item of unreproductive expenditure
as the civil servant’s monthly salary or the office
gas-bill.
* * • *
These, as already mentioned, are only a few
Casual items. The great bulk of the balance
consists of roads, bridges, gaols, lockups, court-
houses, asylums, wharves, alterations to
wharves, &c., &c., which are also wholly unre-
productive and many of them only permanent
in a very limited sense. The Government will
need to go on building bridges at the present
rate for another century before the requirements
of the province are met, and long before the end
of that time, in addition to paying the interest
on the original cost, it will need to start build-
ing them all over again, because the earlieron.es
will be worn out, and before they are all re-
placed another rebuilding will be due, and so
on ad infinitum. Therefore, the bridge item is
also as much a recurrent item as the civil ser-
vant’s salary, and most of the others are in a
very similar position. Out of the £1,548,105
loan moneys expended on alleged “ permanent
and reproductive works,” only about £735,000
can, by the most liberal interpretation, be called
reproductive. The rest are plain, unvarnished
swindle, devised that the Government may
placate the community by keeping down taxa-
tion, and yet profess to make ends meet when
they don’t.
* * * *
The amount of the public works expenditure
which is defrayed out of revenue gets smaller
every year as the habit of charging to pos-
terity the forest-thinning and scrub-cutting and
sand-shifting, and the cost of the Bogan bridge
at Monkey, and the repairs and other sundries,
glow’s stronger. In 1891 £1,486,000 of the
New South Wales Works expenditure was
paid out of revenue ; in 1896 only £978,000 ;
in 1897 only £845,000. The whole story of
tiie economy which is alleged to have been in-
troduced into the finances of late years is repre-
sented by the difference. The plea on which
the borrowing policy mostly rests is that this
is still a young country, and is too poor and
too thinly peopled to stand the cost of construct-
ing works out of revetiue. The said plea
would be a much better argument for a policy
of absolute self-dependence. New South Wales
is still young, therefore it hasn’t had time to
dissipate its public estate, and from this source
alone it derives, without any exertion, a
magnificent revenue of £2,000,000 a year—more
than enough to build all its public w'orks without
borrowing or inflicting any burden on the
people whatever. If it was old and had already
fooled away this splendid asset there might be
some excuse for cadging after foreign capital ;
as matters are there is none. Being young and
out of the track of the world’s bloodshed and
political riot, it has got through so far with a
very small military and naval expenditure. It
has never been dragged into war and seen fifty
millions disappear in a few months’ battle-
smoke. It has never had to rebuild its bom-
barded cities, or to compensate inhabitants who
were ruined by invasion, or to pay twenty
millions or so as an indemnity to buy off a suc-
cessful invader. All these things will probably
come in course of time they come to
every nation in course of time. But
up to the present the Australians, if
they only knew it, have been living in
a halcyon period, and have been enjoying
about the greatest, richest era of prosperity
that any nation was ever blessed with. And
if they can’t liquidate the miserable little daily
bill for roads and bridges and river dredging,
and must needs go hat in hand to the foreign
money-lender, as things are now, for the where-
withal to patch the roof of the old museum and
to repair a wharf and straighten some obscure
creek, their future prospects are an interesting
subject for speculation. The amazing luck
which Australia has enjoyed so far will hardly
last for ever. No country’s luck lasts for ever.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Casefor the Little Cornstalk Party.
Attorney-General Jack Want, the lone,
loud-mouthed survivor of the old “ cabbage-
tree mob,” announced himself definitely last
week as the leader of the anti-Federal party in
N.S. W. Council. Apparently the provincial
element is largely in the majority in the House
of Sleep, which is one solid evidence, at all
events, that Federation means progress and
liberalism and everything else that a demo-
cratic community should strive after. Attorney-
General Want stated the case for the Little
Cornstalk party at great length, but either it
was a very poor case or else it had a very poor
advocate. Apart from a few figures of speech,
it was all contained in these 10 assertions : (1)
The greatest intellects in N.S.W. (no names
given) had always been against Federation ;
(2) Governor Dennison, an obscure, forgotten
person who was out here 40 years ago, was said
to be against it. (Statement not backed up
by any evidence whatever) ; (3) No one had
dared to call a public meeting in Sydney in
favor of Federation. (There have been a dozen
or more, but King Jack was probably away
fishing at the time) ; (4) The “ object of Federa-
tion was separation from England ” ; (5) The
question had only once, since 1858, been sub-
mitted to the people of N.S.W., and that,
according to Want, was when poor old
Walter Bradley, who was an anti-Federalist
among other things, got in by a narrow
majority for East Sydney. (Attorney-General
Want thus actually denies that there was ever
a Federal Convention election, at which Barton
got 98,000 votes and the Little Cornstalk party
was licked out of sight and couldn’t get within
gunshot of securing one seat. The whole
Federal problem, in Jack Want’s idea, turned
on that one election, years and years ago, when
Bradley got in) ; (0) Federation would cost
N.S. W. .£400,000 or £500,000 a year, and “ in-
stead of taxation being only £1 per head it
would be £2.” (£500,000 per annum is only
7s. Bd. per head of N.S. W. population,
not £1 ; and the present taxation isn’t £1
per head, but very near £2 ; and Want ad-
vances nothing but his own bald, splashy
assertion to show that Federation will cost
N.S.W. £400,000 or £500,000 per annum,
or anything at all per annum. Also, the awful
ignorance of arithmetic revealed in his state-
ment is good evidence that he knows nothing
at all about the matter) ; (7) Railway Commis-
sioner Mathieson, of Victoria, “said that the
condition should be made absolute that the
whole of our railways should be handed over ”
(what Mathieson really did say was that the
railways should either be solely under Federal
or solely under provincial control) ; (8) That
the late Commissioner Eddy said N.S. W. should
on no account part with its railways. (This
also rests on no evidence, and Want’s bare
assertion is the first that has been heard of it);
(9) That the people would lose the blessed privi-
lege of being dragged 16,000 miles to have
their law-suits finally decided in England,
and instead there would be a degrading, soul-
blasting Court of Appeal to give them justice
on the spot. Also (10) that Botany Bay, with
its gaol and convict and hangman traditions,
has always been the “ freest of the free,” but
now the constitoosh’n is in danger and the
liberties of the people are imperilled. Attor-
ney-General Want is supposed to be one of the
brightest intellects in the arid wastes of the
N.S. W. Council, and if the case for the Little
Cornstalk party looks so utterly abject when b-
delivers it, what would it have looked like suy


osing the giant intellect of, say, Hoskins had
jjeen on the job ?

ofler all he might not pass, in order that he might get

in dav’s work, even if he got there. Better to keep the

cinrclv one of the most foolish decisions ever
aniiouneed in * con,.. Where is .he •'; sound

The Cyanide Patent.
h j ” replies to Mr. Arthur Griffith’s letter
in last issue :
Dear Bulletin,— Mr. Griffith’s letter is misleading—
unintentionally so, no doubt. He says “the cyanide people
calmlv claimed what they did not invent, what has been
known for 30 years, viz., the mode of extraction of gold
from powdered ore by any solution containing cyanogen.
Now, that is exactly what was not known. It has been
known for years that cyanide of potassium is a solvent of
gold, but it was not known that it could be applied to re-
lease gold “ from powdered ore.” And it was M'Arthur
and Forrest who discovered, after long and ex-
haustive experiments, that a dilute solution of cyanogen
will not only free the gold from the ore, but will leave the
base metals in the residue. Even such eminent scientists
as Prof. Dewar, Prof. Crookes, and Sir Henry Roscoe
have admitted that they were ignorant of this fact till the
discovery in question. And it is this discovery to which
the representatives of M‘ Arthur and Forrest seek to con-
fine their N.S.W. patent. Mr. Griffith is also astray in
saying that the English Court of Appeal ruled this claim
to be bad. This reluctant adverse ruling was confined to
the original claim for the use of solutions of cyanide of alt
strength, which was a “ bad ” claim by reason of its being
too comprehensive and impracticable, but the ruling had
no reference whatever to the claim for a dilute solu-
tion. Throughout their very exhaustive judgment
their Lordships state, in the clearest language, that
they consider the discoverers of the dilute solution as
justly entitled to the merit and profit arising out of their
process. A technical slip was made in including in their
specifications the “bad” claim already referred to, and
the purpose of the proposed amendment is to rectify this
slip, and thus assure to the representatives of the dis-
coverers the benefit of their discovery. It is the gold-
miners who “ calmly claim what they did not invent,
and for two Ministers of the Crown to take sides with
them is not a very creditable piece of business. The
Registrar of Patents will need to be a man of grit to
withstand the weight of this extraordinary combination,
and the public will watch with interest the future pro-
ceedings in this matter. It is not fair to any official to
subject him to such influence. — J.
Gone Under.
The Federation Enabling Bill came to grief in
Queensland Assembly for the second time under
rather confused circumstances. The House was
divided into four parties. There was, first,
the Tozer crowd, which favored Federation
(but wasn’t very anxious about it) provided
Queensland sent 10 reliable Tory nominees to
the Convention, but which would rather give
it up for ever than have the province repre-
sented by popularly-elected delegates. Next,
the GIaSSEY crowd favored Federation (but
wasn’t very anxious about it) provided Queens-
land was represented by 10 elected delegates,
but preferred, on the whole, to let it slide
rather than leave the matter to Tozer’s
nominees. The third party was the one which
opposed Federation under any and all circum-
stances ; and the fourth was the very small
handful which believed in Federation straight
out, and regarded the end as more important
than the means. Tozer introduced the Enabl-
ing Bill with the proviso that Parliament
should appoint the 10 representatives. The
Glassey party moved an amendment with a
view to providing for the election of representa-
tives by the people, and the anti-Federationists
rallied round Glassey in order to create a dead-
lock ; therefore the amendment was carried by a
large majority. Then the badly-defeated and
disgusted Tozer party moved that the bill be
dropped altogether, and the anti-Federationists
rallied round it by way of variety, and that
proposition was carried by a still larger
majority. The Federatiouists form the larger
part of Queensland Assembly, but being sub-
divided into irreconcileable Tory Federationists
and irreconcileable Democratic ditto, the Pro-
vincialists, by voting with each party turn
about, naturally produced chaos. If either
brand of Federationists had been in dead
earnest some better results might possibly have
been arrived at, but both Tozer and Glassey
are very half-hearted apostles. Anyhow, it is
a great deal better that Queensland should
stand out than that it should send ten old,
crusted Conservatives to the next Convention.
The amount of good Tuzer’s nominees can do
to the Federal constitution by not helping to
make it is incalculable.

“God” in the Federal Constitution.
The admitted purpose of some who lead in the
movement to have God recognised in the Com-
monwealth Constitution Bill is to follow on
with further petitions to have the proceedings
of the Federal Parliament, when such a stage
of fruition has been reached, opened each sit-
ting with prayer. The latter is, indeed, a
natural corollary of the first. If there be no
God in the “Constitution” a Parliamentary
chaplain will be a solecism ; if God be made the
basis, the non-admission of the chaplain when
actual governing work is to be done will make
the original recognition construable as the
most pharisaic of lip-services. It is conse-
quently allowable to argue against the admis-
sion of God into the Constitution by showing
the indecency of admitting a chaplain —the
legitimate corollary—into the proceedings of
the Federal Parliament. That Parliament will
be, practically, an enlarged photograph of the
composite provincial Parliaments. Religion
and irreligion, as expressed in words, will exist
there in larger body. Theism and atheism will
co-exist, each with wider influence. If the
theist is to have his chaplain, why not the
atheist—the constitutional counterpart? The
chaplain, as an officer of Parliament, will neces-
sarily demand payment for his services. To
impose the chaplain of Faith on Lhe consciences
and.pockets of the holders of Unfaith would be
to introduce into a country now' free from all
religious tests an incubus identical in character
with that of the Established State Church
which for generations turned the blood of Ire-
land into gall, or that of the millions-grabbing
Episcopacy which, by tithe-bludgeons and other
legitimatised burglars’ jemmies, at present robs
the Nonconformists and non-religionists of Eng-
land. Forty years ago N.S. W. was less liberal,
religiously considered —or, properly, less just—
than it is now. The dominant creed and the
dominant civil power were often found arm-in-
arm. But when, at that period, Dr. Lang
introduced a Bill to have Parliamentary pro-
ceedings opened by a chaplain’s prayer,
it was rejected by 19 votes to 11 ; and
when, later on, “Slippery” Charles Cowper
moved in the same direction, his resolution was
rejected by 14 to 8. The world of Australia
has rolled sunward leagues since then. Atheist
and Theist stand on the same platform ; Jew
and Christian are equal in the eyes of the law.
Is Australia now to be “ petitioned” backward
to a time antedating the birth of three-
fourths of the population? But, if the
active, operating chaplain be an impos-
sibility in Parliamentary proceedings, the
chaplain’s ostensible and self-described raison
d’etre is equally impossible as a basis of these
proceedings. If the logical corollary be matter
out of place, the postulate is matter or spirit
similarly astray.
An Australian Peerage.
At the Premiers’ Conference, held at Hobart
last Feb., it was resolved, by four votes to
three :
That the proposal of the Secretary of State for con-
ferring the permanent title of Honorable after ten years’
service in the Legislative Council be not accepted, and
that the Secretary of State be respectfully requested to
allow the previously existing rule to continue in its uni-
form application to all British colonies.
But Chamberlain “gets there just the same.”
That magnificent person informs an Australian
Governor, who has just put in a word on behalf
of the “ Honorables ” udio desire to retain the
prefix in his province, that Her Gracious has
already approved of the proposal in the case of
Cape Colony. Furthermore, Natal, Newfound-
land, Queensland and Maoriland, have
asked for the privilege, and as it is
“apparent that had all the Responsible Go-
vernments of the Empire been represented on
that occasion (Hobart Conference), there would
have been a small majority in favor of the pro-
posal, I have felt that I should not be justified
in advising Her Majesty, in deference to the
resolution of the majority of the representa-
tives of the Australian Governments, to aban-
don the proposal in the case of any colonies
whose Governments may be still in favor of its
adoption.” Therefore the London Gazette
notifies :
Downing-street, April 10, 1597.
The Queen has been graciously pleased to signify Her
approval that members of the Legislative Councils in cer-
tain colonies possessing Responsible Government may,
on retirement or resignation after a continuous service in
such Councils of notdess than ten years, be eligible to be
recommended by the Governors of such colonies for Her
Majesty’s permission to retain the title of “ Honorable.”
But
the arrangement is merely permissive ; and it will there-
fore rest with the Government of any particular Colony
to recommend or not any qualified person for the privi-
lege as it may think fit.
The arrangement is “ merely permissive,’ says
Chamberlain. Exactly 1 And, therefore, it
looks like a covert stab at Australian republi-
canism which should be promptly resented. It
really amounts to giving Ministers power to
confer a title on any member of the House of
Landlords who has not died or been deposed
during 10 years ; and, as the majority of Aus-
tralian Ministers are title bearers, or expect to
be such, the chances are that the necessary
recommendation to the Governor will never
be withheld. The principle, once ad-
mitted in any province, practically applies
to all Australia. The question is: Does
Australia approve? That the imported foxes
who have already lost their tails in the
Imperial trap may approve, goes without saying.
But, though these provinces carelessly allow
the stump-tailed crowd to show their agility as
Premiers and things like that, it is quite a
different matter when they want to import a
cargo of foreign fooleries for which Australians
have no manner of use whatever,

Verbatim report from the Northern Courier
(Fernmount, N.S.W.) of July 13: —
On Sunday morning last information was given to Con-
stable Allman, at Fernmount, that an old man was lying-
near the ferry approach on the north side of the river, ap-
parently dead. Constable Allman at onee went over, and
found the information to be only too true. The old man
was evidently a swagman, and though he was not dead, he
exhibited signs of most brutal ill-usage. His face and
head were battered fearfully, while his clothing was
saturated with blood. Traces of blood were plentiful
down to the ferry, and even the ferry deck was smeared,
as though it had just been used for pig slaughter. On
enquiries made to the ferryman, who had first given the
alarm, it was elicited that the old fellow, whose name is
Jambs Flett, came on to the ferry about midnight on
Saturday, in the company of Robert Campbell,
jun., who has been living with his father and sister
on the north bank. According to the ferryman
(a foreigner), the two were no sooner on the ferry than
Campbell began the assault. From the account given,
he threw the inoffensive old fellow down on the ferry
deck, and began booting him about. the head and body.
Young Ussher, a mere lad, who was on the ferry at the
time, interfered, and received a blow in the face from
Campbell for his trouble. When the other side of the
river was reached, Campbell caught his helpless victim by
the legs and drew him, head downwards, off the ferry, up
the approach, and left him, to all appearances, dead. It
was after midnight, and the ferryman and young Ussiier
both excuse themselves for not giving ail alarm by saying
“ everybody was in bed.” When found in the morning
Flett presented a ghastly sight. He was promptly taken
to the police-station, and Mr. W. B. Hicks called in. Ilis
wounds were attended to, and later in the day Constables
Jordan and Allman arrested Campbell at bis fathers
place. No provocation appears to have been given for
the brutal usage the harmless old man received ; indeed a
man of his weight and years and temper could scarce!y do
anything to justify hai - sh treatment. It appears Camp-
bell had been remonstrated with for knocking the old
man about early in the evening, and at one time came
near summary treatment at the hands of one of the
Barrieton quarrymen for his actions. Campbell probably
resented this, and wreaked his vengeance on the poor old
man. lie appeared before the Bellingen Bench yesterday,
in custody, and was charged with assault, occasioning
actual bodily harm, and was fined £5, or, in default, three
months in Keinpsey gaol. The fine was paid.
The class of people who howl for the lash as a
means of suppressing brutal larrikinism do so
on the plea that the existing laws are in-
adequate to effect its suppression. The Bul-
letin’s theory is that no one knows whether
the existing faws are adequate or not,
because a callous, stupid and hog-like magis-
tracy never enforces them, and a hog-like
Government never inquires why they are not
enforced, nor sacks the worst specimens of the
present magistracy, nor amends the law so as
to limit their discretion in letting off inhuman
ruffians like Campbell with a ,£5 penalty. A
third cause is that there is no decent public
opinion in N.S. Wales to make the Govern-
ment or the magistracy do its duty ; nor even
enough to shift the brute abovementioned out
of the district as a person whom no white
inhabitant would associate with. There is
plenty of law in N.S. W. to get Campbell or
any savage like him five years in gaol, and he
was only fined £5 11 ! If the possibility of the
lash was added to the law as it stands he would
have been fined £5 just the same, unless the
intelligence of the local Bench was materially
improved. It is not the law, hut the adminis-
tration of it, which wants reforming. The law
is strong enough. The administration of it, in
cases of brutal violence, is miserably weak ; in
cases of injury to property of the value of
threepence or more it is amazingly strong.
Only the other day Judge Gibson sent up a man
for "three years for stealing ninepenn’orth of
baking-powder.
A petition against “ The proposal of the
churches to secure the formal recognition of
God in the Constitution of the Australian
Commonwealth ” is being pushed around Mel-
bourne, not by Jos. Symes or the Anarchist
Club, or any disreputable Unbelieving body,
but by—The Australian Tract Society 1 The
Nonconformist Conscience is, in this matter,
astoundingly common-sensical, and its argu-
ments are briefly set forth on a tractlet:
The recognition of God is an act of faith.
A statement of that recognition is a declaration of faith.
To incorporate in the Constitution of a civil govern-
ment a recognition of God, or a declaration of faith, is to
insert a religious clause.
And so on. A religious clause necessarily
tends towards interference with Man’s secular
right to believe, or disbelieve, anything he
chooses, therefore let us keep God’s name
out of the blessed Constitution, says the
Tract Society. It is quite interesting to find
citizens of ordinarily modern snufflebustious
aspect walking apologetically into city offices
for the purpose of explaining that State
recognitipn of Cod is inconsistent with
original Christianity. Fat merchants, trad-
ing as pillars of their particular churches,
glare at the petition-monger with scorn.
The Fat Man’s shepherd advocates the drag-
ging of God’s name into every kind of document
relating to civil government, and although his
flock have heard the holy gabbler quote “ Ren-
der unto (Lesar,” &c., a hundred times, the
meaning of that historical text has never dawned
upon them. In fact, they feel pretty sure that
Christ wanted to introduce sectarianism into
State school-teaching, and was in favor of repre-
senting V.R. as a queen “ by the grace of God,”
in which case, of course, He would have voted
for establishing the Australian Constitution on
a religious basis. And now, to he told by a
missionary-looking visitor that any mention of
God in an Act of Parliament is irreconcileable
with Christian principles—well, they consider
it blasphemy, and regard the strange person,
with his tracts in one hand and a petition in the
other, as nothing better than “a socialist!”
“A dangerous socialist 1 ”
The gilded Barr-Smith, of S.A., has agreed to
pay all expenses for the construction of meteoro-
logist Wragge’s observatory on Mt. Kosciusko
on condition that the structure be called Barr-
Smith Observatory. The oiler should be firmly
declined, and, if already accepted by Wragge
or others, should be officially repudiated by
Govt. Apart from the fact that, if such a
station be necessary for Australian meteorologi-
cal science, it is the duty of Govt, to erect the
building as well as allot the land, the public
should be protected from the monstrum horren-
dum of a Kosciusko with a Smith on top.
Astronomers in concert wisely interdicted the
application of every-day names to the heavenly
bodies, and Wragge should rather have waited
a while for Government acceptance of his hobby
than have departed from the spirit of such a
tradition.

The following item, from Sydney Telegraph,
is reproduced in all its naked and unadorned
loveliness: —
It is well-known that there exist in Sydney and suburbs
a number of bogus “registry” offices, and latterly so
many complaints have been made to the police authori-
ties hy persons who have been victimised by them that it
has been deemed necessary to make strict investigations
into the methods employed by the proprietors and pro-
prietresses of these offices. The result has proved that
bogus offices exist in the metropolis to an alarming
extent, and almost every day a number of men and
women are swindled out of their scanty earnings. Yester-
day Detectives Roche and M'Lean arrested a man at
North Sydney on charges of obtaining money under false
pretences, and he will appear at the Water Police Court
on Thursday.
The bogus registry-office swindle has been going
on with impunity for years. It has been ex-
posed over and over again. There are at least
20 or 30 individuals who have been running,
this particularly disgraceful fraud every day,
and their premises could be identified at long
range by the moral stench which they exhale.
The demand has been made t nes without
number that some GoD-fearing Government
should take a hand in the business by requiring
the registry-office person to take out a license
which would be cancelled in case of misconduct,
by fixing the scale of fees, and by making it a
heavily punishable offence to run an unlicensed
registry, but no Government has interfered.
Next it appears that “ latterly many complaints
have been made to the police authorities by
persons who have been victimised,” and appar-
ently the said authorities took no special
notice, for nothing in particular ensued. But,
finally, after years of waiting, the force rose up
and arrested one man. And the Telegraph
heads its statement, in large letters, “Deter-
mined action of the Police.” The humor of it!
What The Bulletin desires to ask is : If it
takes such solid determination on the part of
the police force to arrest one individual, how
much fewtr individuals could they have arrested
supposing they had been a shade less deter-
mined ? Also, how soon will this great, whole-
sale swindle be put down at the present rate of
progress —the arrest of one alleged offender in
ten years or so ?
Murderer Butler’s pathetic, parsonically-
conveyed allusions to Sunday-school and drink
are accepted by the orthodox community gener-
ally as satisfactory evidence that the Mountain
monster died penitent, and is now, according to
the theological Cocker, among the happy
mortals. The jump-to-glory travesty is reminis-
cent of the dying speech of murderer Doolan
in the condemned cell in Glasgow. Doolan,
addressing the officer of the prison, said :
I often think of the many narrow escapes I have made
in Ireland and England when I have seen a fellow-work-
man sent into eternity by an accident, which might have
happened to me as well as to him. Had I died then I
would have been called into eternity without a moment’s
preparation, and my immortal soul would have been lost.
But I have reason to thank God that He has given me a
long day to prepare for the change that awaits me, and I
hope I have improved mv time and will go straight to His
blessed arms.
In effect Mr. Doolan reasoned that had he died
by an accident while at work he would have
gone to hell, but that God was good to him, and
by allowing him to brutally murder the fore-
man of his gang secured for him some weeks in
gaol, where he was able to properly prepare
himself for death. The Butler theory is the
same. Had that worthy fallen overboard 15
years ago he would have been damned—because
he did not slip gracefully through a parson’s
hands. Now, although a murderer, he is in
heaven. The parson let him in.

Pang Goon, a Chinese laundryman, was sum-
moned to the Hawthorn (Melb.) police-court,
the other day, to show cause why he shouldn’t
go to prison for non-payment of a .£5 fine, said
fine having been incurred by his open defiance
of the Factories Act. Pang, it seems, toils in
his insanitary shop 18 hours a day, or there-
abouts, which helps to make life harder than it
need be for workers in general. The Factory
Act was intended to protect Pang against him-
self in the interests of civilisation. Since it is
not good for a man, not even a Chinaman,
in his individual character, or as a member
of the laboring community, to work 18 hours
a day, one would naturally expect the ser-
vants of the Lord to pin the Factories
Act to the Ten Commandments and de-
nounce Pang Goon as something little
better than a Sabbatli-breaker. That’s where
one would naturally make a mistake. When
the case of the defaulting laundryman came up
for hearing, “ one of the guardians of St.
Columb’s (fashionable Anglican) church in-
formed the Bench that if an adjournment were
granted the fine would be paid by some mem-
bers of the church, the defendant being one of
its Chinese converts.” This is a lesson in
Clistianity which requires to be carefully
studied in all its bearings upon the advance*
ment of mankind, and the diffusion of sweet-
ness and light among law-abiding citizens.
Woman has lost another of her privileges in
Maoriland. In that province a male person
has been iirrested and is out on heavy bail upon
a charge of administering noxious drugs with
intent to procure abortion, which, so far, is
only part of the ordinary course of things.
But the startling novelty is that the lady is
also out on bail, chai’ged with being an accom-
plice and with taking the drugs aforesaid. This
looks like quite a new departure. Hitherto,
when a woman has gone to an abortionist and
incited him or her to do an unlawful act, and-
has paid for it being done, the law has never
held her responsible. She might inform on the
abortionist, and give evidence against him, and
get him a long term in gaol, without her in-
criminating statements entailing any conse-
quences whatever upon herself. Not very long
ago a Maoriland chemist was convicted solely
on the evidence of a woman upon whom he
operated, and when the defence raised the plea
that she was not a competent witness, being an
accomplice in the act, the Court decided,
without the slightest hesitation, that she
was not. How she could be anything
else than an accomplice was not at
all clear to the lay intelligence, but
the Court adhered to its decision. Apparently,
the law is now going to try a new reading of
the case, and the new reading has logic on its
side. Whatever people may think of the re-
lative guilt of the woman who is driven to
desperate measures by the dread of disgrace
and exposure, and the man or woman who
makes money out of her fears, there is no doubt
that they are accomplices, The new reading
may make the path of the abortionist a i rifle
easier by furnishing his clients with an addi-
tional reason why they should not give him
away, but that is a different matter.

“The Sign of the Cross.”
H 8 Rickards’ Variety Co.
The “Friend from India,” staged on
oaturday night at Her Majesty’s, has so many
advantages as compared with the “Night Out,”
that it should certainly have been put on at the
beginning instead of at the sudden tail end of the
brief season. The motive of the previous comedy
was as old as the days when the elderly cave-man
hrst gave way to temptation and induced the
young cave-woman to come out on the primitive
razzle-dazzle in her husband’s absence, and have
bear s meat with him behind a tree in a ferny vale
aD j idok at the stars, while she sat on her hair
and he maundered into her large ear. The present
one is built on a rather novel idea, and the idea is
well worked out. Paulton’s little barber, who is
driven by force of circumstances to pass as a
Mahatma with a counterpane around him, and his
mouth full of abracadabra, is a really interesting
character. Also, he is a wild satire on Theosophy
and on creeds in general. When he remarks in a
deep monotone that time is nought, and space is a
phantasm, and the thing which is now is merely
the shadow of what used to be, and that eggs are
bd. a dozen, and the reason why a blue rhinoceros
can t walk on the ceiling is the same which causes
butter to have hairs on it in Galilee, it sounds
quite as convincing as most other things in the
way of dogma ! Then, when he strikes a tre-
®™ons attitude and asks why there are cold
shudders in Brahmapootra, and what evidence
there is that Pharaoh was ever drunk and had a
black eye beside the Mediterranean, or words to
that effect, the statement seems absolutely conclu-
v®-. -Almost anything in the way of loose whoop,
if it is delivered in a deep, slow monotone by a
man who holds his hand up in the air, is good evi-
dence in support of almost any kind of creed.
After hearing Paulton on the subject, this journal
feels nearer being a convert to Theosophy than it
ever was before or is ever likely to be again.
r * * * *
That old favorite, Lauri, as the small,
fat millionaire who hires the Mahatma to have
mysteries and convulsions in his drawing-room
before a fashionable audience, is another tower of
strength, and these two take up so much room that
there is comparatively little space for anybody
else. Miss Alma Stanley is much better than she
was in the last production, inasmuch as she only
wears one utterly insane hat, whereas last time
she wore an unspeakable hat all the time. Paulton
rises consideraHy in The Bulletin's estimation
in the Friend from India.” Among the minor
people, Miss Minna Phillips, Pred. Cambourne,
and Albert Whelan (who puts a lot of rich
humor into a very casual policeman) leave
the largest footprints on this paper’s soul,
taken all round, however, the co. does its work
well and the “Friend from India” gives it
abundant scope. It contains no plot in particular ;
young Chignell simply wakes up in the marble
halls of old Chignell after a fearful drunk, and
can t remember how he got home, or why he
brought with him the complete stranger who is
sleeping in his room. All he can recall distinctly
through a haze of wine and song and broken win-
dows and smashed hats, is that the old man has
given him notice that his next drunk will lead to
final ejectment, therefore he wildly introduces the
stranger as an eminent Mahatma to avoid intro-
ducing him as the benevolent fellow-drunk who
carried him home. Thereafter the comedy runs
riot on no special system, and only ends when it
is too much exhausted to go on any longer.

Juliet Wray, John Coleman, Lauri, Ernest Shand,
and a multitude of other people in it, is to follow
on Saturday.
** * *
“ The Pauper,” which has taken the place
©f “ Mrs. Quinn’s Twins ” at Sydney Royal
deserves honorable mention as about the worst-
named drama on record. The name suggests a
toothless old person, named Crump, living on
gruel in the workhouse, and Maggie Moore, as it
proves, never was in a workhouse at all. She is
merely a young lady of limited means, who lives
on the penny she gets occasionally to stop singing
and go away. Circumstances throw her into an
unaccountable family, consisting of a rich flour
merchant, his male cousin, who has just married
the flour merchant’s sister in order to get her
money, and his she-cousin, who is a lady with
long hair and a horrible past, and with murder in
her soul. This lady is followed by her husband
whom she supposes to be dead ; he is effectually
disguised as a red-headed Jew, and he lends
money to her to make recognition absolutely im-
possible, and the name on his card is Azariah.
Azariah “ blows ” much good red gold in discount-
mg his faithless wife’s unsecured promissory-note
and lurks considerably behind hedges. The bene-
volent flour-merchant falls in love with the sing-
ing girl, and the lady with the past poisons a
harmless professor in mistake for her; and the
male cousin fails to get his wife’s money after all
and leaves her, but afterwards repents. In be-
tween times a Count looks in and makes weepful
remarks about his lost daughter who was stolen
from It-al-ie. Naturally, the singing-girl marries
the flour-merchant and proves to be the Count’s
daughter, and at the psychological moment
Azariah falls on the lady with the dreadful past,
and she is arrested in hysterics. The play ends
with a general impression that the lady is ulti.
mately hanged in a beautiful empire costume of
pale-blue silk with merveilleux trimmings, and
with a diamond aigrette in her hair and a spray of
roses in her oorsage.
* * * *
None of these people—not even the lady
who is arrested in hysterics—look particularly
real; the play is a dream-like thing laid in the
land of nowhere in particular, and it is quite im-
possible to take Azariah seriously. The obvious
purpose of it is to enable Maggie Moore to sing as
often as possible, and she fulfils her mission admir-
ably- Also, she wears clothes which are a vision
of Paradise, and in her black costume in the last
act she looks young and beautiful to a surprising
degree. The Bulletin would like to break the
news gently to Miss Moore that when she attires
herself in black she ceases to look tall cross-
wise (which she sometimes does under other
circumstances), and appears almost as young
as she did in the early days of “Struck
Oil. But this is a delicate matter which is best
explained to a lady by anonymous post-card.
Harry Roberts’ clever acting lifts the rich flour-
man on to an undeserved eminence, and Max
Maxwell, Johnson Weir, E. B. Russell, and Miss
h itzmauriee Gill do good service and help to keep
a rather slipshod drama from falling over its own
feet. Miss Gill is the lady with the dreadful re-
cord, and the business-like manner in which she
supplies the corpse on very short notice calls for a
word of approval. The writer knows where the
raw material for a really excellent corpse is walk-
ing about at present, and he views Miss Gill’s per-
formance with a personal interest.

Bristol’s educated horses and mules, under
the management of our hitherto awful friend,
Professor Kennedy, commence a season at Sydney
Criterion on Saturday. In the present case, this
paper is pleased to state, the Professor doesn’t
mesmerise anybody and then make him drink
kerosene, or put him in a rigid state across two
c ; lal js and prance on his stomach, so he is
alluded to above as our hitherto awful acquaint-
ance by way of giving him the benefit of the
doubt. Possibly, his desire not to outrage the
feelings of the comic mule may keep the Professor
from falling into his old practices this time. The
said comic mule came upstairs and paid this office
Vmt on Tuesday, offering to temporarily take
the place of any scribe who wanted a holiday ; he
is the most cultivated and gentlemanly mule the
staff has yet met.

A benefit is being engineered for Miss
Elsie Adair, the clever little lady whose recent
show came to an untimely end at Sydney Cri-
terion. Miss Maggie Moore lends the Theatre
Royal for the occasion, and this effort to help a
nice-looking fellow-creature on her path is fixed
for August 4.
