** * *
. Sydney Town Hall is to burst into a par-
ticularly brilliant spell of melody on Saturday
night by way of a benefit to Pechotsch, junior,
who is departing for Europe, with a view to carry-
ing on his musical studies. Pechotsch, junior,
whose name isn’t nearly so difficult to pronounce
as it looks, comes of a musical family, and has
already an excellent reputation of his own, there-
fore the best musical talent in Sydney has rallied
round to give him the good send-off which he
deserves.
** * *
The Farley opera season fell to pieces on
Friday night and dispersed in various directions
and that none-too-prosperous show is now ad-
journed indefinitely. The co. thinned out con-
siderably in its last few nights, and the only
reason why the audience didn’t do likewise was
that it was already in a lamentable state of
attenuation. Considering all things, The Bulle-
tin reckons that a benefit for the members of the
deceased company would be a judicious piece of
benevolence.
** * *
At Melbourne Royal, on Saturday night
a vast audience tried earnestly to find astounding
features in the “Flying Jordans and their Famous
Organisation,” but the desire for a new tftrill from
the new ’Murkan visitors was not gratified. In-
asmuch as the fleeting Royal show challenges com-
parison with the entertainment run by Rickards
at somewhat lower prices, all the year round the
present writer feels unusually grateful to Rickards,
and it is only fair to express this gratitude in a
public manner. What would his adopted country
say nowadays if ’Arry were to produce Miss Lily
Jrost, as a Queen of Song, for the purpose of sing-
mg something which Florence Esdaile, a while
ago, warbled much more sweetly and fluently in
the ordinary Opera House first-part ? Also, what
would an Opera House “ god ” say to a Miss Post
under these painful circumstances ?
* * * *
The few people who appear on the Royal
stage are satisfactory, in some cases, until they
take an encore, and even the encore seems desir-
able in the case of John J. Burke, who is a bright
comedian, with a large, handsome lady attached.
I here are other nice-looking girls attached to other
comedians, but these simply serve as excuses for
the men to stand round and disgorge aged jests
whereas the massive vision aforesaid is required
to assist J ohn Burke in a boxing-match. The rest
of the stage items, including horizontal - bar
exercises, a comic bicyclist, and two gro-
tesque Oil fans, range from fair to nasty.
Ihe two principal items happen in mid-air. Miss
Virginia Aragon, the wire-walker, and the five
Jordan trapezists are excellent performers. But
the incidental rope-fixing and net-spreading is
tiresome, and for two sections of a theatre audi-
ence Miss Virginia and the Jordans are awkwardly
situated. Those in the stalls have to gaze up-
wards, whilst the back row of the dress-circle also
gets a stiff neck in reaching for a view. Taking
one consideration with another, the show at the
Koyal doesn’t appear likely to be patronised be-
yond its deserts.
** * *
Meantime, the Opera House is going
strong. The Selbinis have revealed further cause
* or their popularity, on bicycles and otherwise,
and their rapid mode of procedure grows faster
than ever. Tom Wootwell, the leg-shaker from
oydney, was received with thanks on Saturday,
when Milford Haven dropped his respectable
voice into the gap left by Phil Newbury, and a
number of well-beloved turns came to pass as
before.
** * *
“ The Sign of the Cross ” continues to
display Belief in a pale blue limelight at Mel-
bourne Princess’s, whilst a rosy and rapturous
light streams down upon Pagan vice. Marcus and
Mercia, handicapped by severe colds at the outset,
have quite recovered from their respective bron-
chitises, and all is well with the Firm’s imposing
spectacle. It promises to run for several more
weeks to the satisfaction of hundreds of sincere
church-goers, whose new respect for the theatre
will probably justify a taste for comic-opera later
on 'j • here fore, The Bulletin derives comfort
and joy from “The Sign of the Cross.” If the
nnancial success of this piping-hot love-story
should lead to an increased expenditure on
frivolous shows hereafter, and if the recent con-
vcrts don’t backslide into their former gloom, “ The
Sign will have done a good deal for humanity,
anyway. W. and M._ must deal carefully with
their„ converts. The piece that follows “The
Sign must be as giddy a thing as one can find
in irreligious dramatic works, otherwise these re-
cruits from the ranks of the godly will certainly
backslide, and devote their evenings to gentle
Christian dissipation as of yore. They have seen
a raging Marcus chase his heart’s delight under
exceptionally-improper circumstances, also they
have heard the girl afterwards confess that she
loved the rakish Roman all the time; furthermore,
they have listened to the furious expression of
.berenis s long-pent-up feelings, and so the next
time they cross the threshold of a playhouse they
will expect cayenne pepper with their oysters.
** * *
Charley s Aunt ” has come to stay at the
Bijou for an indefinite period. Apart from the
reduction in the tariff, Frank Thornton’s fourth
revival of good old Donna Lucia has a strong
point in its favor. Last time he came to Mel-
bourne, he played “ Miss Brown ” through a
seven-weeks’ season, and the “ Aunt” shines bril-
liantly by comparison with the other bogus female
who was perhaps intended to prepare the way for
her prototype’s re-appearance. “Miss Brown”
having served a managerial turn, may consider
herself dead, or nearly so ; but her relative from
iuJ! mt f is immortal. Which reminds one
that Charley s Aunt ” has spread a knowledge of
Brazil nuts among the Australian race, and the
race, here and there, has paid for its wisdom with
awful pains inside.
** * *
Mark Hambourg, and brother Jakoff,
assisted by John Lemmone and a lady vocalist
(who is subject to alteration), opened their concert-
season at Melbourne Town Hall on Thursday last,
the first concert drew a multitude, and a Ham-
bourg Boom is now progressing at the rate of three
explosions per week. Jakoff, in his 17th year is
a remarkable violinist, but the multitude’s adora-
tion is for the youth rather than for the early
sometimes unripe, fruits of his genius. Jakoff
is wondrous, considering his age. Mark Ham-
bourg, at 18, may be reckoned wonderful
a l*)t)ng pianists of all ages. Two years ago he was
chiefly a slogger, and though Beethoven and other
composers couldn’t bowl the boy out with their
curliest deliveries, his batting showed room for
improvement. Mark has returned to Australia a
great player. He drives the music to the off, or
pulls it round to leg, or gets a single through the
slips as feelingly as he sends it flying over the
boundary. There is pleasure in hearing Mark
rattle up a score on the Erard piano, fitted with
somebody s patent resonator. W hen a strikerof his
phenomenal ability has learned to infuse soul into
his smites, the world rightly regards him as a
noble object-lesson for the girl who worries the
piano next door, and we pray that the spinster in
question may profit by Mark’s example, or" lapse
into a despairing silence. John Lemmone puts
himself quite m the background at these concerts,
as though he were an artist of no importance.
Nevertheless he is the flautist of old, and his per-
formance delights everybody.
* fK * *
The Melbourne audience at the first of the
Hambourg concerts was mixed in the proportions
of about women to 1 man. Of. all musical dis-
plays, women are most fetched by piano playing
They worship Paderewski and dote on little Mark
Hambourg. Not the sounds extracted from the
box of harmony but the method of extraction it is
that appeals to their hysterical souls. They have
no ears for the sounds ; all their attention is con-
centrated upon the sounder, and when he isn’t
hitting out furiously at the instrument, their
interest grows cold. Average women, it is evi-
dent, don t half appreciate a beautifully modulated
rendering of a gentle composition, such as Chopin’s
Berceuse. Tms delightful music has charms
for men who are not themselves players.
All the women at a concert are addicted to the
piano, and their delight is in astonishment at the
technical skill of the performer’s hands. The per-
former must be of the male sex, too. A woman
key-puncher cannot excite them to madness. One
hears them confessing at their little musical even-
ings—“ Ido so love to see (not hear) a man at the
piano.”
At Poverty Point.
Theorbo : “Heard Max Hambourg for the first time,
on the premifere of this Melb. season, and had a remark-
ably good place, quite close to him, on the front seat down
from the organ. Neither he nor Jakoff seem to have the
faintest enthusiasm or sentiment. They are as cool as
cucumbers, directly they have done playing-, though both
very high-strung at their work, especially Max. Trilby
was present to my thought watching him. Before each
selection, he passes his hand over his brow, as if to recol-
lect, for he plays without music. You would think his
inside was all piano-wire. His trembling is like that of
the steam man I once saw, walking round and round.
The inspired marionette is suggested, everything is mere
faultless technique, and he almost staggered off when his
night’s work was done, after a finale by Rubinstein, the
most terrific flogging fight with a piano imaginable.
His actions may be classified. First and favorite,
a smiting of the piano, as thrashing with a
u-i Sometimes one hand scrabbles and scribbles,
while the other is poised in air, and down it swoops to
join in the torrent of sound. Then he clutches at the
keys as if they were harp-strings. Most lovely, in
Chopin’s Berceuse, was the fluttering of his hands, as a
pair of doves gently flapping. It made me think of a
passage in Dante’s Pare.diso, where two loving souls are
represented as circling round each other, murmur-
mg, cooing their affection. At times his fingers went
so fast that you could no more distinguish them than
bicycle spokes doing a scorcher. Again, they re-
minded me of the flickering spirit hands, showed by the
tneks of the mediums. Jakoff is a great violinist,
with the majesty of genius almost as distinct as
Max s. None of the constraint visible in all the
eminent ones I have seen, beginning with Camilla
Urso. There is neither modesty nor assurance. He is
simply regal. The bow, indeed, seems to guide the
hand, and work of itself, flying about at all angles. All
soloists but he appear to labor, as I have indicated. The
audience, with me, enjoyed him a little more than Max,
who enforces such awful respect. With Melbourne’s best
quality stalled immediately before me, I fancied Max
could not help thinking them drab-looking, as compared
with a block of his patrons out of London or Paris.
There was not the air of luxuriant wealth. Not the fresh'
bloom, gloss duvet on the costumes. Nor the sheen of
diamonds and jewellery at large. An evidence that Melb.
is not quite financially convalescent.”
Melbourne’s musical culchaw was fully represented, one
may suppose, at Marshall Hall’s orchestral concert, in the
Town Hall, t’other Saturday. A patient, cold-footed
audience filled the uncomfortable building, and listened
admiringly to an excellent performance of three principal
pieces a Wagnerian idyll, a Beethoven symphonj 7 , and a
concerto for violincello and orchestra. The concerto,
with Hattenbaeh as soloist, was evidently the most popu-
lar item on the programme. It gave the greatest pleasure
to the greatest number, judging from the applause when
Marshall Hall (who is the most modest and unassuming of
conductors, anyway) brought Hattenbaeh back to the
platform to take his “recall.” Therefore, the cant of
musical criticism was very severe upon this particular
success. The Argus said, “ The one novelty, Volkmann’s
concerto, is a composition which might, without any
serious loss—so far as the value of the music is concerned
have been omitted from the programme. It practically
consists of only a first movement, and scarcely merits ad-
mission at a classical concert.” The Age said : “ Herr
Louis Hattenbaeh brought forward a concerto by Volk-
mann for violincello and orchestra. Beyond a very
melodious second subject, and a cadenza of almost appal-
ling difficulty, there is not much about the work that
appears either striking or particularly interesting.” The
moral of these newspaper notices appears striking enough.
Musical criticism is intended to warn the public against
appreciating any instrumental music that is too melodious
and easy to comprehend.

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
The Commercial Banking Co. of Sydney has
had a very successful half-year, though the regular 8 per
Uont dividend is paid with a microscopic margin.
The ’six months’ profits are £40,119 against £40,067 last
half and £40,303 the one previous. Of this £40,000 goes
in nav an 8 per cent, dividend, leaving a margin of only
m fn East half the margin was £67, and at June,’96,
there was a surplus of £337. For a weak institution this
would be sailing pretty close to the wind, but the Com-
mercial of Sydney, with £1,000,000 paid-up capital,
£1 010 000 reserve-fund, and £3,082,000 of cash and
Government securities, has no urgent necessity to accumu-
late further reserves just now. Deposits (£9,490,000) show
a steady improvement, and the Commercial follows the
wise course of piling up a large holding of Government
securities, the amount now held (£712,000) being £300,000
more than that of six months back. Altogether it is a
balance-sheet which fully justifies the public confidence
in the institution as about the most solid thing of its kind
in Australasia. * * *
Vic. State Land Bank has now advanced about
£3OO 000 to farmers on freehold security. This means a
saving of about £7500 a year in interest to the borrowers
—a decent-sized load already lifted off the hard-driven
agriculturist. * * *
The City Bank of Sydney states its profit for
the first half of ’97 at £BO4B, as compared with £BO7O and
£BlO7 for two previous half-years. This just enables it to
pay its little 4 per cent, dividend (£8000) and leaves £4B
over. The deadly accuracy with which the City Bank
just earns enough to cover its dividend with a nominal
balance over is shown underneath:—
Profits Dividend Surplus
December, 1893 ... £10,736 £10,418 £3lB
Deficit
June, 1894 11,468 11,613 145
Surplus
December, 1894 ... 10,004 10,000 4
June, 1895 10,030 10.000 30
December, 1895 ... 10,020 10,000 20
June, 1896 8,108 8,000 108
December, 1896 ... 8,071 8,000 71
June, 1897 8,048 8,000 48
This means that the City Bank has accumulated only
£454 in four years—it has written £139,132 off its reserves
in the same period to cover losses—and the accuracy with
which it just earns its 5 per cent, or 4 per cent, dividend,
as the case may be, is really marvellous. During these
four years the item “ Bank premises and furniture ” has
increased by £BO6O. It rose steadily till December, 1895,
and since then it has remained absolutely stationary ;
apparently the City Bank doesn’t believe in writing-off
anything for depreciation of the tables and chairs. With
such small margins as the above, it couldn’t write-off
much without breaking into the dividend.
The balance-sheet contains the least amount of
information possible. Assets appear under three head-
ings only : “Coin, bullion, and cash balances,” “Premises
and furniture,” and “ Bills discounted and all other Debts
due to the Bank.” The management doesn’t even
particularise such things as notes of other banks,
Government or other debentures, balances due by
other banks, &c. The only real news to be dug
out of this very reticent sheet is that deposits and
advances have both increased slightly, and that the cash
(£269,021) is equal to nearly 24 per cent, on the liabilities
to the public. If the Vic. banking law, which requires it
to be stated whether property is put in at cost, or, if not,
how much is written off or written up, and which
demands an explanation of many other particulars, were
in force in N.S.W., it should evoke quite a lot of informa-
tion. The Citv Bank’s shares (£8 paid up) are still selling
at about £3 165., and until they get to par, of which there
seems not the remotest hope at present, there is little
prospect of selling that £1,000,000 worth of new shares
which it W'as decided to issue in 1893. Apparently the
idea of making the City Bank a massive, live institution
will have to be hung up for a good many years. Its
deposits are not much more than half what they were in
its best year (1886), and the gaudy 12J per cent, dividends
it paid in its good times have left it with not very much
in the way of reserves to fall back upon.
* * * *
New South Wales railways and trains yielded
£149,920 more net revenue for the year ended June, ’97,
than for the one previous. The previous year’s railway
deficit was £321,690, so this year the shortage should be
down to about £172,000.
* » * »
The Commercial Bank of Australia (Melbourne)
has finished its second half-year under the new recon-
struction with remarkably good results. The profits are
£52,529 as compared with £41,636 last half-year. A year
ago the Commercial got a reduction of interest from 41 to
3 per cent, (having then just made a loss of £24,000
for the six months ended June, ’96, the need for some
such relief was sufficiently evident). According to the
figures of the present balance sheet, however, things have
since so far improved that the Commercial could now pay
the w'hole 4£ per cent, if necessary, and still have a
small b ilauee of profit. The stipulated 2 per cent,
dividend to preference shareholders absorbs £21,170 and
£33,000 is added to the special reserve account to cover
possible further losses in realising the assets of the old
bank. This makes £53,000 accumulated for this purpose
in the past year. How much will be wanted is, of course,
an unknown quantity dependent entirely on the property
market and other things. In the meantime the business
seems to be improving ; the profits show a very solid in-
crease ; and the writing-off of £1,200,000 lost capital, and
the deduction of the same amount from the inflated
values on the other side of the balance-sheet, should make
things reasonably sound. At present the Commercial is
earning about 3J per cent, on its capital, which is pro-
bably about as much as any Melbourne bank can expect
just now.
The dead or comatose assets of the old Commer-
cial are now kept in a separate “ Trust ” as special
security for £3,600,000 odd of the old deposits, and one
quaint proposal in the last reconstruction scheme is the
formation of a “ separate company ” to take these over.
This idea had much better he dropped ; it is the same one
which the B.N.Z. tried to such very little purpose with
its famous Estates Co. The thing may he called a new
co., but it must consist of the same old shareholders and
management and assets and liabilities as it does now
when it is merely a department of the bank, and the bank
will he responsible for any shortage just the same, and
the only difference will be a new name and some useless
expense for stamp-duty and stationery. During the last
half -year the Assets Trust has extinguished £lOO,OOO of its
liabilities, so steady progress is being effected towards
wiping off the mess that was made in ’93. The bank’s
own reconstructed deposits (redeemable at fixed dates)
are now worth 18s. 6d. in the £, and those of the Assets
Trust (redeemable only as cash becomes available through
the realisation of properties) are worth 15s. 9d., which
figures show that; public faith in the Commercial is dis-
tinctly reviving. The bank now holds £48,000 of the
Assets Trust deposits (£12,000 more than it did six months
ago), and, presuming it bought them at market-value,
that should account for £2400 of the profit, anyhow.
* • * *
Goldsbrough, Mort, and Co. (Ld.) is another
big reconstructed institution which is in trouble with its
“B ” debentures. It owes £1,491,400 at 4 per cent, to
“ A ” debenture-holders and £1,234,350 at 3 per cent, to
the “ B ” crowd, and it contrived to pay the former in
full for the year just ended, while the latter got 15s. per
cent., or only a quarter of the amount agreed upon. The
co. has had two re-arrangements, and has got a large re-
duction of interest, but should things get a little worse—
if they possibly can get worse —the urgent need for a
third re-arrangement seems possible. G.M. and Co.,
like many other institutions, is loaded down with too
much “A ” and “B ” debenture. It runs its huge busi-
ness with £923,000 capital and £2,800,000 borrowed
money, and the arrangement is a tfop h^avy.

N.S.W. Mont de Piete reckons its half-year's
profit at £4225, as compared with £4284 and £4699 for
two previous half-years, so it appears that the financial
blight has even struck, to a limited extent, the thriving
joint-stock “ uncle ”in Castlereagh-street, Sydney. The
usual 10 per cent, dividend takes £3672, and the balance
goes to various reserves which now amount altogether to
£19,564. With this reasonably solid accumulation on
hand the co. is in a position to consider applications for
the hypothecation of the flat-iron in a liberal spirit.

The Civil Service Building Society (Sydney)
puts a good face on a report that is none too cheerful.
The profits for the year are £359, whereas last year they
were £453, and £435 the one previous. A dividend of 2)4
percent, for the year (the society calls it a dividend of 5
per cent, for the half-year, but apparently it follows the
quaint practice of declaring a half-year’s dividend once a
year) absorbs £379, so the 120 deficiency is made up out of
the balance on hand. Another £3OO is added to reserve
fund, also out of the balance on hand. Said reserve is
now just £BOO. It takes more than half the microscopic
income to pay workingexpenses(income, £1213 ; expenses
and taxes, £686). Also, the society owes £1933 of deposits
from the public, which cost it £ll6 in interest, and then
it deposits £2OOO in the City Bank, for which it gets only
£lOl interest, which is a truly brilliant arrangement.

The Newcastle and Hunter River Steamship Co.
(N.S.W.) still pursues its career of giddy finance. For the
half-year ended June, 1896, it earned £2285, and spent
£3OOO in paying a 6 per cent, dividend. For the half
ended Dec., ’96, it earned £3106 and paid £3OOO in divi-
dends. For the present half it earned £2876 and pays
£3OOO dividend. The balance to credit of profit and loss
has diminished in 1)4 years from £766 to £33 ; and, as the
N. and H.R. S.S. Co., despite its £lOO,OOO capital, has a re-
serve of only £5OO, the situation is interesting. If things
don’t improve, the next attempt at paying a 6 per cent,
dividend should break something.

* * * *
The rains in the Northern agricultural districts
of South Australia have come too late to do much good to
the wheat-crops, but the yield of hay of generally poor
quality will probably he very heavy owing to the rapid
growth of “self-sown” stuff—wild-oats, &c.—and chaff
promises to be as cheap next season as this year it has
been dear.
* * * *
A knock-down blow to some of the weaker
trustee companies will probably have been dealt by the
recent decision of Vic. Full Court, confirming the opinion
of the Judge below, to the effect that a trustee co. is no
more entitled than any other trustee to place the
money of its clients on fixed deposit in banks. There was
a proviso in the Act relating to trustee co’s which
appeared to suggest that they had such power, but the
court very properly held that if such an extraordinary
power was intended to be given, it would have been in
express words. The decision of the Full Court is in
opposition to an opinion given to the trustee co’s by the
English Q.C. Cozens-Hardy, but is in accord with opinions
given by Cussen and Woinarski, of Vic. bar. The banks
in this particular case were the City and the English,
Scottish and Australian.
* * * *
Judge Murray, sitting as a Royal Commission
to inquire into the prevalence of accidents at Broken Hill,
has been confronted with evidence diametrically opposite
on most points, managers standing solidly on one side
and men pretty solidly on the other. All, how'ever, are
more or less in agreement as to the incompetency or care-
lessness of workmen causing most of the accidents ; and,
in that connection, general manager Stewart, of the B.H.
Proprietary, gave evidence to the effect that in the 12
months to June 30 there had been discharged from that
mine alone 84 men for offences (such as lighting fires
underground) that might have led to accidents, and 461
for incompetency ; scores of others, after working a shift or
two, saw that they could not do the work,and left of their
own accord. Managers and men agree that a proper system
of apprenticing ought to be introduced —and this is likely
to be the most permanent benefit to follow the Commis-
sion. The apprentice system would have been adopted
long ago but for the hostility of the Miners’ Association to
any but full-pay men going into the mines ; the body is
more reasonable now, though, and will only grumble over
the conditions of engagement.

* * * ♦
Practically every time the N.S.W. mining regu-
lations come into a court of law they are snorted at scorn-
fully and thrown out. The last pitiful exhibition made
of them was at Tarrawingee, the flux quarries outside
Broken Hill, where, two workmen having been killed
through using an iron bar in a partly-charged quarry-
hole, the manager and ganger were prosecuted. Held
that the regulation was ultra vires, section 64 of the
Mining Act not conferring power to regulate the use of
explosives.
* * * *
The price of copper has a big bearing on mining
for that metal in N.S.W. If it is going to sta.vforsome years
in the neighborhood of £5O a ton, thousands of men will be
employed in copper-mining, and ere long the production
of copper from N.S.W. will exceed that hitherto sent from
the whole of Australia—about 11,000 tons a year. Says
the Mechanical World :—“ For the past two or three
years the supply of copper from America has kept up the
stocks and kept down the prices, but the revival of
general trade in America has led to an increased con-
sumption of copper, with the result that there is nothing
left for this country. Although we are not likely to see
a repetition of the iniquitous copper-ring of several years
ago, there is bound to be a rise in the price. The present
price is practically £5l a ton, and it need surprise no one
to see it up to £7O. Beyond this price aluminium
may be able to compete.” Against this prediction the
latest development in the art of casting copper without
alloys is quoted. The Electrical Review says the new
metal is known as M.B. copper, possesses an additional
tensile strength of 33)4 per cent., and as the new copper
is said to carry the same amount of electrical current
with one-third the weight of metal, it will cause a distinct
change in the quantity used in electrical manufacture.
It is understood that Edison is much interested in the
new development.

A new Sydney mining tipster prints this : —“ I
look upon the Great Gobar as an A 1 concern, having such
a great body of solid oreasfew mines possess. It hasalso the
enormous advantage of carrying in its copper a great per-
centage of gold, so that no matter what the price of the
metal, copper, may be, it will always pay to work the mine
owing to its gold contents.” The Great Cobar is indeed
a splendid mine- -but the fact remains that given a fall in
copper like that which happened in silver a few years
hack, the Cobar, yea, even the Great Cobar mine, would
shut up like an oyster.

At White Cliffs, notwithstanding five or six
years’ experience, opal mining, or rather fossicking, is
still little better than a lottery. One Bonnor bought a
semi-deserted claim a month or two back for £l5. To the
beginning of July he had picked out gem which had
realised £lll3.

A.C. : The Bulletin takes little interest in
market-mining, but prefers to see mining regarded as a
solid industry, like sheep-raising or dairying. There are
not a few Eastern mines the shares in which, if treated as
investments, yield from 15 to 25 per cent, on market
prices year after year ; and are likely to do so long after
they have repaid their original capital—which some of
them have returned over and over again.

A Brisbane sharebroker, giving evidence before
the Mining Commission, opinioned that it was absolutely
criminal for a mining sec. to traffic as a broker in the
shares of his co. Of course ; and yet it has for years been
openly practised in the North, and is likely to continue
until the law sits down hard on those who thusly rake in
ths shekels.

* * * *
The attempt to “boom” Wadnaminga, in the
N.E. of S.A., has fizzled suddenly. At the beginning of
July only one claim was being worked, and by the middle
of the month that, too, was deserted.
* * * *
The Bulletin said a year ago, on the authority
of a famous mining-ma.i who, being no boomster, was
well detested when in Westralia, that the next big gold-
boom would be in British Columbia. The boom, it is
cabled, has come. “ Phenomenally rich ” —“ richest spot
on earth ” —and other real boom talk ; the Canadian com-
missioner in Sydney is besieged by people who want to
know how to get there ; Canadians are clamoring to con-
fine the working of the new camp to British subjects,
and the chances are that within the next few weeks
hundreds of men will be getting away from Australia,
more particularly Westralia, where the fortunes made in
mining by cable are keenly remembered and envied. The
Bulletin, though not the boomsters’ paper, at last even
finds itself in accord with W.A. papers in trying to stop
men and money from going out there. Says one : “It is
saddening to hear that close on three millions of English
capital has during the last ten months been sunk
in the gold - mines of British Columbia. It is
very saddening to Westralians, inasmuch as we
are convinced that this large sum of money
would have come to us, had we kept faith with the
British speculator. ‘Wild Cats’ have been the cause of
robbing us of the three millions which have gone. It is
sad to contemplate, and we wish a law existed which
would put behind the bars of justice those who, have
caused the ‘ slump’ we are at present experiencing.’’ The
writer mingles his tears with these, and thinks so too.
He never thought or said otherwise, and made heaps of
enemies by it. Latest sent to The Bulletin—“ lam now
on my way hack to the Alaska and British Columbia
country, wiaere I expect to remain all the summer. This
great gold-mining country is going to show up very well.
Take my word for it the good mines are more numerous
and visible than the}' were in Westralia w'hen we travelled
over it, though that is not saying much. It is a terrible
country to prospect; the winters are so severe. For six
and eight months in the year it is difficult to work at all,
unless you have prepared for the snow, and most of the
country is covered with a heavy moss.”
♦ * * »
W.A. Govt, has just passed some additional
regulations, to come into force Aug. 1, the effect of which
will he to do away almost entirely with any necessity to
comply with labor-conditions. By these new regulations
no application for forfeiture of a lease can be entertained
until applicant has first deposited £25 as security for costs
and expenses; and the warden has the power, on refusing
to recommend forfeiture, to award expenses and £5O
damages against complainant. This would not he so bad
if the wardens were always just and competent men ; but,
as matters stand, a man with a good case hasalmost an equal
chance with a man with a bad one. Only at present he can-
not be heavily cast in expenses if the warden chooses to
decide against evidence, or to draw his decision out of a
hat, as he often seems to do. Few' persons will, in future,
take the threatened risk ; and the result will be that
leaseholders will generally do as they please about keep-
ing on men. The Bulletin abhors “ jumping,” i.e., it
derides the corrupt and unbusinesslike idea that a valu-
able mine, w'hen forfeited, should become the property of
the pimps, sneaks and perjurers by whom the forfeiture
is so often brought about, instead of reverting to the State
and being publicly auctioned for the benefit of the con-
solidated revenue. But there is a happy medium—and
the W.A. Govt, hasn’t found that medium.

• * * *
A party of miners, who could not get work in
the mines, took up an abandoned lease in the Mt.
Margaret district containing a peculiar formation not
unlike soapstone, and heavily charged with mundic. A
small crushing gave a return of over 2oz. per ton, a
similar amount showing in the tailings. The future suc-
cess of the Coolgardie goldfields will depend on the
number of payable mines having formations other than
quartz.
* * * *
Up to less than a year ago, in Westralia, neither
the prominent mining-men nor the newspapers outside
the Norseman dared to say anything for the building of
the Esperance-Coolgardie railway—a very offensive sub-
ject to the Forrest Govt, and the Six Families of Perth.
Nearly all in a boom want to be loyal to the Govt., and
say pleasant things to everybody, and to lie generally
about everything. That line is now clamored for all
round, and, as the writer declared three years ago, the
Forrest crowd will be forced to build it—“ to shoot or
give up the gun. ” Modest Maryanski—and he is no small
beer now—says : “ We must have the railway to Esperance
Bay. It is absolutely necessary that we should have cheap
timber and fuel for the mines here. If you get good
mining-laws, cheap timber and lumber for the mines, and
cheap freights to and from the seaboard, this colony will
beat the whole world as a gold-producing eountry.” It is
the fate of the Govt, to build that line, in spite of itself.

“The new find at Mt. Remarkable, near

* * •
A little news about Bayley’s: “ The work of
prospecting from the main shaft has been resumed. The
shaft is 466 ft. deep.” And around that shaft the writer
regrets to say there are tens of thousands of tons of
quartz as white as a boar’s tusk and almost as barren in
gold. Occasional pockets may be found in it, perhaps
costing more to find than they are worth, but the great
bulk is too poor for anything, a fact known to every miner
working in the mine three years ago, but concealed from
public belief chiefly by the aid of that degraded institu-
tion—the Sup-Press.

OCIETY
“The Australasian troops have been here
just long enough to realise that the ‘ crimson
thread of kinship ’ only exists between ordinary
British military officialdom and ‘ Britain’s sons
across the sea,’ just so long as it is not used as a
ferny-rope to guide the ‘Greater Britons’ home,”
writes The Bulletin’s London correspondent.
“ Chamberlain may do his hospitable damnedest
to ‘gather up the strings of the Empire’ and
make the Australians feel at home, but
the padded, pompous B.M. officer’s priggish
treatment of the visiting troops would burst
up the best scheme of a ‘ united empire.’ The
N.S.W. Mounted Rifles are lodged on the plains
of Hounslow, in an uninviting white-washed barn
where they were nearly corpsed by the sharp, un-
seasonable weather. Like Buffalo Bills, Red
Indians, or dancing Hottentots, they were com-
pelled—by some agreement with the War Office—
to ‘ show ’ at. the Islington Tournament twice a
day in the side-splitting farce of ‘ capturing a
bushranger ’ —veritably working their passage. [lt
is now stated that they were paid .£5OO for the
show.] If the Premiers wero ‘guests’ of the
military, doubtless Reid would be asked to do
some ‘ tumbling,’ Turner to play the bones, whilst
Seddon boxed the Zoo goat, and Kingston stump-
speeched on ‘ Querulous Matrons and Cocky Doc-
tors ’ —and ‘ quaite good enough for the blawsted
kerloneyul kon vicks.’ ‘Faw-em faw-ers. Raight
turn. Marrreh !’ ”
“ A.ll the colonial troops at Chelsea Barracks
were in a state of mild mutiny within a week of
landing at their contemptuous treatment. The
V. and S. A. M. R., and W. A. troops per Orotava
were marched on foot for six miles from St. Pan-
eras Station to Chelsea on their arrival behind a
straggling band of the Scots Greys. The absurdity
of marching mounted men, after six weeks’ ship-
board, through the rough-cobbled or asphalted
•streets was exemplified in the result, as many
IN DROUGHT-TIME.
Trying to raise a fallen beast—by twisting its tail and shaking a hat in front to make the
animal attempt a charge.
were footsore tor days afterwards. The crowning
insult arose over the inspection-day, when the
tickets issued to the individual members of the
troop for their friends’ use bore a stamped injunc-
tion on the back : ‘ Admission by hack gate only.’
The front gate was reserved for the ladies of the
‘ Hinglish hossifers, bless yer ’eart, ’ and the
colonials’ pals could go to blazes or come in through
the dirty slums. The Daily Mail, backed by the
other papers, made such a storm over this that
Salisbury interested himself, and things are very
different since.”
• • • *
“Jimmie Pannikin ” to The Bulletin :
A new thing in addresses. The passengers of the R.M.S.
Ballaarat presented Lieut. Chick Cox (officer command-
ing) with an address complimenting him upon the good
behavior of the N.S.W. Lancers on their voyage to Eng-
land. I wish to make a few remarks. I have been re-
markless for a long time. Why shouldn’t the N.S.W.
Lancers (who are not professional soldiers, mind you) be-
have just as well as, say, the second-class passengers ? All
the Lancers are respectable young fellows. Some of them
have very good social and professional positions. I can
quite understand how the Lancers’ gorge rose at the in-
sulting patronage of the passengers. If I had been one of
the Lancers, I’d have retaliated in kind by presenting the
captain with an address congratulating him upon the
really admirable behavior of the second-class passengers,
and also upon the exceedingly creditable conduct of the
first-class passengers. A sensible crowd would have taken
the good conduct of such young men as the Lancers as a
matter of course ; but the Ballaarat crowd professed to
deem the Lancers’ normal behavior a matter for surprise,
and, therefore, worthy of special recognition. Of course,
the address was weli-meant. The insults of ignorance
are often very well-meant, but d annoying all the
same. — Yours-agin-all-addresses, Jimmie Pannikin.
* * • *
Pointed out by Rev. P. G. Smith at Goul-
burn Anglican Synod, that Australian parsons
have no business with any style but “ minister ” ;
whereupon the degraded Smith was promptly sat
upon by all the self-dubbed “ rectahs ” and
“ vicahs,’ who would scorn to share with common
dissenting persons a degrading appellation which
actually means “ servant! ” The Anglican parson
who wrote, “ Without black velvet breeches—
what is Man! ” probably intended no joke after all!

Sydney’s old -friend, Dr. Jefferis, of the
strident voice and monkey shave, was the chief
figure in the memorial services at Adelaide for the
dead explorers, Wells and Jones, and the further
advt. of the modest Calvert. One point in his
reverence’s address had relation to the alleged fact
that Wells, asked if he would like to take a flask
of brandy on his flying trip with Jones, replied
that he preferred to take a prayer-book. “We
can thank the Lord,” said Pastor Jefferis, “ that
this young man preferred the book of Common
Prayer to a flask of brandy. ” But why not take
both ?

They had been five solid weeks at sea, and
were nearing the Australian coast when the com-
mittee got up some sports for the children.
Amongst the other amusements was ducking in a
tub of water for apples, but the tub in use was too
deep for the youngsters, so the captain turned to
one of a number of young ecclesiastics who were on
board and said, “ This tub is too big, you’d better
have a smaller one fetched from the bathroom.”
“ Ah, sure, sir,” cried his reverence, innocently,
“ and where’s the bathroom ? ” In such cases
celibacy is surely a virtue.

A NEW METHOD.
ARTLESS LITTLE INNOCENT ( to leader of German hand, present-
ing him with a copper) : “ Please, Mr. Musician, don't
play any more. We can't afford it—mother says this is
all the money she’s got."—(And they didn’t.)
A Yic. anti-vaccinationist “got” 40s. or
three days a few weeks back, and in default of the
£2 a warrant of commitment was issued. It was
then pointed out that the man lived at the top of
an almost inaccessible perch in the midst of
almost impenetrable scrub in furthest Croajinga-
long, and that, assuming the trooper succeeded in
finding him at home, it would occupy more than
the three days to convey him to the nearest lock-
up —so that he would have served his sentence
before completing his journey. The warrant was
withdrawn.

* * * *
X.Z. : “You remark on a Sydney doc-
tor’s objection to seeing the statute-book stained
with a law to force people into a hospital, and
mention consumptives particularly. The objec-
tion is most reasonable as hospitals are now built.
Would he himself, if consumptive, go gladly into
a huge barrack, whose only occupants were within,
say, a year to a week of death from phthisis?
Would he, if a typhoid patient knowing himself
to be on the cliff-edge of death, gladly go into a
typhoid ward in Westralia and aid in adding to
and swallowing up the foulness of the air ? The
cottage system—best of all, a tent system in a dry
air like Hay or Deniliquin—is what is called for.
To place a hundred tuberculous patients in one big
barrack is plain murder.”

The Argus was too much shocked to record,
as the Age did, the fact of Johanna M'Mahon’s
successful upsetting of the Holy Bible in the mud at
the Brunswick procession. The “ Grane ” generally
regards this particular performance as the test of
the moral victory of the occasion, and it would not
be surprising to hear of Mrs. Johanna M‘Mahon
being canonised at the proper time.

* * *
The bogus tea-merchant
dodge has of late been going
strong in some N.S.W. country
districts. It is a poor, clumsy
device, but a swindle needs to be
very poor and clumsy before it
can be called an absolute failure.
A seedy man, who looks as if he
had seen better days, offers lib.
packets of tea at 25., with a prize
m each packet. The buyer, by
special request, empties out the
tea to make sure the prize is
there. It is. Prize consists of a
huge ring, apparently gold, but
presumably brass, that would be
a loose fit on the lady’s big toe.
As she obviously can’t wear it,
the agent offers to get it exchanged
for a smaller one, 18-carat, with an •
opal in it, and takes it away for
the purpose. Almost every house-
wife in one big district has now
got 3d. worth of tea-dust for 25.,
and is waiting for the return of
the agent driving a van loaded
with jewellery and silver tea-sets
for distribution. But the seedy
man in the dusty soft hat never
comes back.

Filthy Perth: Slab from “remarkable
revelations ” in a recent municipal report:—
Residence, St. George’s-terrace. A fowl-house, 23ft. x
9ft. 6in., in the yard of these premises has been recently
converted into a dwelling-place for a man, his wife, and
three children, and for which a rental of 7s. 6d. per week
is charged, the only structural alterations being the sub-
stitution of galvanised iron for wire-netting. The sleep-

TRUE LOVE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
True Love, like the blue rolling ocean,
Hath seasons of fitful unrest; .
But unchanged is the depth of devotion
Which lies ’neath its storm-ruffled crest.
It may ebb as the tide when receding,
And seem as though gone evermore,
But returning, with fervor exceeding,
Flows again like the tide to its shore.
W.A.
J.M.
SUSPENSE.
I have written my darling a letter,
A letter of passionate stress ;
Would my powers were stronger and better
To tell all I strive to express ;
For the weight of the fate that is lying
On the chance of her “ No ” or her “ Yes ”
Will deal me disaster undying,
Or a life one eternal caress.
Her answer is lying before me
Untouched ! for swift-coursing chills press
On my limbs, like the ice on the stormy
Sea tracts, holding all in duress,
Till I move—ah !so slowly—to take it,
Faint and fearful, not daring to guess.
And the seal —it costs hours just to break it
But the answer, the answer is——“ Yes ! ”
YARRSS.

“One noticeable matter at Kalgoorlie,”
writes a Bulletin correspondent, “is the super-
abundance of johnnies, all exactly alike. They
turn up the bottoms of their trousahs, call water
‘ watah,’ laugh inordinately amongst themselves
about nothing in particular, have the same reced-
ing foreheads and retreating chins, and the one
typical look of helpless inanity. At t e recent
record-reign corroboree they displayed an aggres-
sive loyalty to the Crown, insisted ostentatiously
on taking off their hats when ‘ Gorsave ’ was
being howled, and felt called upon to loudly pro-
claim that they nevaw, nevaw should be slaves.
On th - other hand they are plucky and orderly,
and generally work for their living. Some do
fencing or rough-carpentering, or carry round the
‘ papaws ’ for sale. Nearly every English
mining-co. lias its inevitable attendant jackaroo
johnnie, who is cook, clerk, or washerman—per-
haps all three combined. One amusing phase is
that those who have been a year or so on the fields
are palpably ashamed of their johnnydom, for
they turn down the bottoms of their trousahs, go
without collahs, and effect other shallow attempts
at disguise. They don’t seem to have acquired
the knack of accumulating money, and it’s not
easy to forecast what will become of them when
they grow old, ‘ stranded on a foreign soil.’
Perhaps by that time Government will have seen
its way to provide an asylum for ‘ poor and dis-
tressed johnnies-’ ”

From the S.A. Church News (Adelaide) :
Quiet Day for Women. —At the request of several
ladies, Canon Green proposes to conduct a quiet day for
women in St. Peter's, Glenelg, on August 25th. The ser-
vices will begin on the evening of the 24th. Full particu-
lars will be given later on. Names of those who may
wish to attend may be sent to the Sisters of the Church
or to Mrs. Green, the Rectory, Glenelg, who will answer
any enquiries. The probabie cost to each visitor will be
about 7s. 6d.
How moderate ! And yet the parsons wonder
why this wicked world laughs at them !

It will be interesting to note the machinery
provisions, the qualifications of the officers and
general administrative paraphernalia, provided
by the Kansas (U.S.) legislature for the en-
forcement of its proposed new statute, “Thou
shalt not covet thy neighbor’s wife.” One bad
thought that this had dropped out of the Ten
Commandments as being obsolete, but there s
consolation. A penal statute is strictly construed,
and, accordingly, the prohibition against coveting
will be limited to the case of one’s next-door
neighbor. Hence it is advisable to live next-door
to a bachelor in Kansas.
Here is a knotty point for Kansas lawyers under
the Ten Commandments statute ! Isn’t the
coveted wife in every case a voluntary or, occasion-
ally, an involuntary, accomplice in the coveting
process? Doesn’t she want to be coveted, if no
more? And doesn't her husband, in most cases,
want the same, but no more ? The point is :
shouldn’t their evidence be excluded as being the
evidence of accomplices ? The canonists of Kan-
sas, no doubt, have anticipated the difficulty.

Australians probably export the best beef
in creation, and undoubtedly eat some of the
worst. Said a Northern pastoralist and butcher,
boastfuliv : “We don’t slaughter a single waster
for sale. "No—we feed the pigs with them. O yes,
we sell the pork ! ” He looked upon the pig as a
sort of patent purifier for the conversion of dis-
eased beef into healthy bacon.

The Himperial Hinstitute “authorities”
have at last done something to deserve Australia’s
gratitude. They gave a Diamond Jubilee dinner,
whereat “ colonial journalists were refused the
customary facilities afforded at public functions
for reporting the proceedings.” The authorities,
perhaps, wanted to save us from the customary
dose of cable drivel.
Minister Gould’s new N.S.W. Licensing
Bill provides that a married woman who has
obtained a protection-order, or judicial separation
or divorce, may hold a publican’s license or obtain
a. new one. But only a few months ago Gould,
Cook, Garrard, and the revered Brunker were
unanimously agreed that barmaids should be ex-
cluded from hotel service. What is the difference
between a divorced etcetera woman obtaining a
license and a barmaid retailing? And, indeed,
are not many barmaids now serving divorced
women etcetera ?

WHEN, WHERE, HOW, WHY, &c.
Hug her neatly, in the dancing, shimmering moonshine,
Swear for her you’d die . . . and other lies ;
pop it sweetly when the glancing, simmering spoonshine—
Escaping from a sigh—glows in her eyes.
V,hen she says “Yes!” haste ! ! wed her while she’s
willing ;
Rush on ! there’s stacks of nuptial joy ahead,
The gentle heart you’ve won likes cooing, billing.
She’ll make . . . the shops all bill you once you’re wed.
Alone.
DEAD.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
She flashes on the inner sight,
In other guise by memory given,
Unto her former self as bright
As heaven is to one’s dreams of heaven.
Death came. She knew no cold embrace.
She felt no dart; she drank no cup,
’Twas but God’s shadow on her face,
As low He stooped to lift her up.
Dunedin, M.L.
Constance Clyde.

Bidding farewell to dear old England
Premier Reid said he had staked his political for-
tunes on inducing N.S. Wales to emulate the
policy which had made England great, It now
transpires that Napoleon’s defeat at Waterloo was
du ®> popularly and erroneously assumed
—to Wellington and Blucher, but to a valiant
bagman called Cobden. Pulsford, not Nelson
deserves the glory of Trafalgar. ’
Majah J. 0. Neild, the other day, put 147
questions on the N.S.W. Assembly notice-paper
about civil service matters. Under the Super-
annuation swindle a public servant had to con-
tribute to the Fund whether he would or not. If
he reached the age of 60 and was still in the
service, he was entitled to a pension. If he left or
was sacked even a day before the statutory age he
* OS ii “is pension and had merely a claim, not too
well-defined to a gratuity. And it is alleged—
this is what Neild is asking 147 questions about
that a number of civil servants who were just on
the verge of 60 were summarily sacked by the new
Board, thus missing their pensions by a few
months or weeks or even days, after many years
of enforced contribution to the fund. And in four
cases—concerning which Neild asks 46 questions—
it is even alleg-ed that the gratuity was refused also.

A WOMA N'S LETTER.
~ „ Sydney, July 27, 1897.
Mt Dkab Myee—
Speaking generally, things in Sydney are no
cheaper than they are in Melbourne. There are
vast quantities of eheap-and-nasty importations
here, and I constantly encounter enthusiastic
visitors from the South rushing off to buy “such
beautiful shoes and gloves,’ at cataclysmal prices;
a fortnight later their ardor is damped, for by
that time the earthly career of the inexpensive
purchases is closed for ever. There is less of the
phenomenally cheap production in Melb., and less
consequent shabbiness, but Sydney seems to enjoy
being made the dumping-ground for a world’s
shoddy.
The highly-decorated stewards at the com-
mercial travellers’ ball, on Triday evening, found
that, contrary to usual custom, there were not
‘ la “, enough girls to go round. Disconsolate males,
puffing cigars, stood about in groups in the corri-
dors all the evening, entirely abandoning all hope
of capturing one of the few women present and
leading her forth to gyrate in the almost
empty ball-room. Only when supper-time ap-
proached did these gloomy bands of partnerless
men show any enthusiasm or interest in the pro-
ceedings. The wet evening or the high price of
the admission cards had the effect of making a
crowd of doubtless-fascinating maidens stay away
with zeal from this brilliantly-arranged dance.
Those who did turn up wore their prettiest frocks
and were rewarded by having hosts of partners
and a pleasant evening altogether. The debutante
of the evening, a dainty-looking girl in white
brocade, carried the only bouquet in the room.
Several girls, obedient to fashion’s latest dictates,
wore sleeves : one or two made up for the abun-
dance of material they had lavished on their arms
by economising in the matter of shoulder-and-busb
drapings. A flame-colored satin gown made a
vivid streak of color and showed up well against
the palm]- branch decorations; but probably the
most noted and most admired gown of the lot was
a black satin finished with copper sequins and
worn by a foreign-looking brunette with alabaster
neck and arms.
During a gilt-edged wedding ceremony, last
week, pickpockets were busy among the crowd of
well-dressed guests waiting at the door for the
bridal procession to depart. Two accounts of
vanished purses were reported to the police next
day. The man who willingly walks into a church
to see a wedding for the amusement of the thing
is such a rare bird that a male pickpocket would
be at once detected; the bridal purse-abstractor
must be a woman. It has not been needful yet to
chain down the cheques and other portable articles
of vertu among the wedding-presents displayed at
swell marriage festivals, because a detective is
supposed to lurk eagle-eyed among the throng.
But if the new industry continues to thrive, the
plain-clothes man will have to be introduced into
the ceremony itself, perhaps concealed behind the
assisting curate’s surplice, or disguised in the
floral buttonhole and long frock-coat of the best
man.
At the Engineering and Electrical Exhibition
at Prince Alfred Park, visitors who want to see
the wheels go round are most pleased with the
exhibits in active eruption, such as the inter-
mittently-gleaming eyes of the fountain lions, and
the cookery practitioner who turns out batches
of edible cakes and realistic puff pastry under the
spectators’ eyes. An attractive show is the stall
of the clever glass-engraver, some of whose work
is preserved in the National Gallery—upon his
glassware at the Exhibition Mr. Webb has cut
several hundreds of different designs. A contri-
vance that draws an absorbed-looking crowd of
men on all occasions is a patent beer-pull, which I
can’t presume to understand, but which always
keeps the beer fresh, and is intended to supersede
the bar “ beer-pump.” Afternoon and evening
concerts happen at the Exhibition; also the
strains of De Groen’s band mingle with the
puffing of gas-engines and the whirr of the
machinery.
The beer-pump is a mystery to every girl who
isn t a barmaid. A party of girls from the city
were staying at a quiet country hotel for their
holidays, and used to pass the bar on the way to
their rooms. It had the fascination of mystery,
and one damsel determined to be an amateur bar-
maid the first chance she had. Seeing a solitary
man enter one day she glided quietly behind the
counter and asked if he wanted anything to drink.
The customer looked surprised at the naive query,
and mentioned beer. So the amateur made wild
pulls at the handles on the counter (without turn-
ing the taps), but was baffled at the start, and
made no headway until the customer, observing
confidentially, “You don’t seem to know how to
work them pulls, Miss,” walked round and helped
himself, while giving instruction in the art of beer-
serving to the flushed and embarrassed Hebe. All
the rest of the day that bar did a roaring trade,
for the customer spread such reports of the style
of “ the new girl at Blank’s ’’ that the whole town-
ship strolled in to see her, and had to worship dis-
contentedly at an empty shrine.

From Sydney D.T. :
RETURN THANKS.
Mrs. and family sincerely thank their many
friends for kind cards, letters, telegrams, floral
tributes, &c. (Floral tributes supplied by Miss ,
and Son.)
But why leave out in the cold the chemist, the
nurse, the parson, the driver of the funeral casket,
and the grave-digger ? Were they not too “in at
the death ” ?
Vice-regal unpunctuality in Brisbane is be-
coming well-known as a failing of the present
dynasty. A recent instance of it was at a dinner-
party at Govt. House, where a famished group had
to wait an hour beyond the named feeding-time
for the arrival of the host and hostess. The hostess
didn’t turn up at all, and after a long delay H.E.
appeared in the drawing-room and announced that
her ladyship would not dine that evening, and the
party filed hungrily in without her.
After years of silence, remorse has caused a
N.S.W. railway refreshment-room-keeper to con-
fess to a bluff which he once worked-off on Gov.
Jersey while His ex-Ex. was travelling “ordinary.”
Lord J. demanded luncheon—something simple,
* ‘a slice of cold sirloin, butter, and a head of
celery.” A minute afterwards the caterer rushed
the aide aside and whispered in tones of agony
“no celery.” No matter, horseradish would do.
Unluckily, horseradish also failed to be on hand,
and the prestige of that station refreshment-room
was at stake. But ingenuity conquered, and,
before the train moved out, His Ex. owned to
having enjoyed “ the impromptu little meal, par-
ticularly the sauce.” The resourceful lessee had
got over the horseradish difficulty by hastily
whittling a wooden butter-pat with a piece of
broken glass, and mixing with the mass of soft,
curly, white wood the yolk of an egg, olive oil,
mustard, and a suspicion of vinegar.
Mark Hainbourg’s arrival will be hailed with
rapturous thrills by many Sydney women, who
gushed over the boyful musician on his last visit,
and gave a water-party (between 20 and 30
women attended), at which the pianist was the
only man present. Mark, at 16, was catholic
in tastes; he invited, or rather permitted, adora-
tion from a worshipping bevy of all ages. And
yet he was eclectic, and always chose the same
favored maiden to accompany him in his cab to all
his concerts, calling for heron the way, “as her
presence stimulated him.” In other cities, it was
a different cab and a different girl—but the same
Mark.
The Charity Carnival, which will begin in the
Town Hall on the 13th Sept, and remain open for
a fortnight, will contain all kinds of devices for
raising the wind for various benevolent objects.
Several firms have sent and promised expensive
gifts, to be drawn as prizes, and the doll-show will
include dolls (to be dressed competitively) of every
country and rank in life. The most original
new-woman” doll will get a prize; also wax
models of characters from well-known plays.
Elavia and Marcus ought to make gorgeous dolls.
At a Mel b. doll-show, held during the “Trilby”
run, Zou Zou was the most reduplicated charac-
ter ; the little Zouave’s picturesque red and blue
looked very showy in miniature. The boy-doll is
a much more interesting object to dress than the
beauteous, characterless blonde beloved of babies.
At the children’s fancy-dress ball, the other
night, in Sydney Town Hall, several women in
the gallery quietly slipped off their cloaks (they
were in evening dress), and, going downstairs,
mingled with the dancers. The fee for the body
of the hall was 55., for the gallery 2s. ; they thus
got a night’s dancing at a saving of 3s. As the
ball was given for a charity, this about takes the
cake for meanness.
The Suva (Fiji) people are great on balls and
dances, no matter what the season of the year.
At one fancy-dress affair a prominent town coun-
cillor was going to make a big impression, and
kept his make-up a great secret —only his wife
knew of it. He was going as “ Ally Sloper,” and
had modelled himself a beautiful artificial nose
out of plaster. On the eventful night, after he
had donned the well-known seedy “ duds,” and
was reaching out for his nose, he found, to his
great sorrow, that the wretched cockroaches had
eaten this ornament “ almost to the bone.” Cock-
roaches are very fond of plaster.
. Fiji cockroaches will devour anything. There
is not a house in the group with wall-papered
rooms, because these beetles eat the paste and the
pattern off the paper. You cannot hang a picture
unless it has a glass front and a hardwood or metal
frame They even attack a sleeper’s toenails
should they accidentally stick out beyond the
boundary of the mosquito-net.
The tea-room was shown up in a recent Sydney
matrimonial-case as a possible hatching-ground of
mischief. It is a cheap and easy meeting-place,
and must fill a long-felt want in the social scheme
of things ;in proof, witness its popularity. The
secret of its success as a rendezvous is that the ap-
pointment to meet at a tea-room can be made by
a girl; she feels that her request does not involve
expenditure beyond sixpence on the man’s part,
and he is more likely to come, and at the same
time it is just as compromising, and Mrs. Grundy’s
frown is cheap at a tanner. Many gilded youths
are caught by the buttered bait of buns and tea.
Etiquette-guides now recognise the tea-room as a
social factor, and give hints and aids to unwilling
men to extricate themselves gracefully from awk-
ward appointments into which they may be
dragged, and bid them beware of the note-writing
damsel. The more secluded resorts, not known
generally, are the favorite nooks.
Madame Henry’s “Cercle Fran9aise ;
met on Wednesday evening last in her smart new
room in Tattersall’s Chambers. The hostess had
provided a capital programme, every item of
which was so genuinely French that one solitary
German-named piano-piece looked quite alien and
exiled on it. Mrs. Alfred Brown, a four months’
pupil of madame’s, made the hit of the evening in
a brilliant recitation and a beautifully-cut pink
brocade gown. Two girls, with uncertain gesture
but charming natural accent, went through a
small comedy which occupied a very pleasant ten
minutes. A solid slab of information about the
history of French literature was given by the
hostess herself, and the evening was pleasantly
rounded-off with songs and some dainty refresh-
ments. At the next meeting of the “ Cercle,”
which takes place on Ist Sept., the Consul-General
of France will be the guest of honor on the
occasion.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ A London correspondent tells me that Premier
Turner stepped out of the Dover train, on his
arrival, ‘in a thin, creased sac suit, a mouldy
“bowler,” and a genial boyish smile. What
recked he of the three Royal carriages and tall,
champing steeds, controlled and attended by the
scarlet claw - hammered, flour - haired flunkeys,
all wooden dignity and brassy braid? His
attire and demeanor hinted a saunter along
the Esplanade, with his hands intertwined behind
his back. He was brown and rosy with health,
and the newly-shaven chin disclosed the flowing
curve of a duplicate his dearest enemy would not
have previously suspected. Looking jaunty and
jolly, and several years younger, he pleaded a
sundowner’s sundown tiredness. Contrariwise,
Lady and Miss Premier were millinerised and
costumi&red to meet the House of Peers and
Peeresses, and, with some dignity, accepted their
greetings from the official and political representa-
tives. There was no big drum, no rainbow testi-
monial, no guard of honor, and George I. didn’t
know or care whether there was or not. He
wanted bed, and lots of it.’
“Tenor Napoleon Boffard was the lion of the
afternoon (19th) when the smartest-looking
‘ Madame ’ in the club was hostess at the Austral
Salon. Before the plump visitor began to sing,
women who had not sampled his voice at previous
concerts pronounced him ‘ absurdly fat and
Frenchy ; ’ but as soon as he opened his mouth it
was evident that, fat or not, he ‘had away with
him.’ Even in French, which might as well have
been Greek to most of his hearers, he made an
impression, and when he went on to warble
‘ Island of Dreams ’ with its longings and sighings
in the vernacular, every she present felt thrills
down her spine and wanted to run and nestle her
head on the singer’s G. H. Reidish waistcoat.
Meantime the sprinkling of husbands on the pre-
mises looked on rather blankly while their wives
winked tears off their eyelashes and gazed yearn-
ingly at the vocalist as if anxious to accompany
him to the ‘lsland of Dreams,’ or any other
lovers’ paradise he chose to mention. Picturesque
Mabel Mattingley, in a wine-colored frock and
creamy lace fichu, trilled cheerfully of ‘ The Work-
a-day World,’ and helped to dispel the glamor of
the sleek wizard’s sorcery.
“ The accident-curse apparently still hovers over
Australian vice-regalities. Surgeon Fitzgerald,
by this time quite an expert in gubernatorial
anatomy, is at present engaged in extensive re-
pairs on the lacerated ribs, irritated lungs, bruises
and general shock of Lady Brassey, who emerged
badly from a collision between her landau and a
run-away butcher’s cart.
“A certain musical genius, for all his cleverness,
showed up at a recent Chamber concert as an un-
licked cub. He talked all the time, and whenever
an instrumentalist deviated by a hairbreadth from
time or tune, exclaimed ostentatiously ‘ flat! ’ or
‘ dragged!’ just as if he were a pianoforte evangel
sent forth to unveil the dark places of Australian
musical heathendom. But musical prodigies are
seldom harmonious except at the finger-tips.
“ One of our own Australian specimens is a case
in point. At an early age he was taken in hand
and presented with a first-class foreign education
by music-loving enthusiasts. Lately, when he re-
visited these shores and toured round the country
districts with a concert co., a reverend sympa-
thiser, hoping to shield the youngster from the
snares of a Bohemian life, &c., bespoke for him
gratuitous board and lodging at most parsonages
cn route. To this day his hostesses, careworn
Marthas one and all, wax eloquent as they dilate
upon the sullen boorishness and lack of common
gratitude displayed by the fledgling artist, for
whose benefit they had turned out their nurseries
and laid in small luxuries usually foreign to their
larders.
Mayoress Strong is fixing the civic ball for the
middle of next month, the early date being picked
on in the interests of her medical son, Dr. Harry
Strong, and the warrior, sweet William (of the
Vic. Rifle team), who arrive from ’Ome about that
time. Mrs. Strong’s tall, good-looking quartette
of boys have been a help to the mater in doing
her rounds, and a pleasant relief to the Nicest,
who generally find mayoral family connections a
severe trial.
“ At Prahran mayoral shivoo, last Friday, there
appeared in the debutantes’ set Miss Bluebell
Staples, Miss Violet Meudell, and Miss Lily Arm-
strong. In due course Melbourne may expect a
ballroom crop of Dandelions and Daisies, the
result of having a Field Flowers Goe in the
episcopal gaiters.
“ Lady Holled-Smith, perhaps out of a thirst
for novelty, dispensed with dance programmes at
her recent big dance, and the less desirable male
creatures suffered severely from the restriction to
verbal agreements. One callow youth, engaging
an utterly unprincipled angel for a waltz, pleaded
hard ‘ Can’t you put it in writing ? ’ but she was
not willing to sign a contract. When the turn
came he found her turning round with another
fellow.
“ Miss Eva Woolcott, one of the five pretty
daughters of Mr. J. S. Woolcott, of Balaclava
was married on Wednesday last at All Saints’, St.
Kilda, to Mr. W. Grant, whose family is well-
known in Victoria. The bridegroom’s father was
the late E. C. Grant, of Stawell, one of the
developers of quartz-crushing on the old Pleasant
Creek goldfield, and his uncle the late J. Mac-
pherson Grant, of Land Act fame. Mr. Grant
has station property in Riverina and Queensland,
and intends to settle with his bride at Bowenville
Darling Downs.
“ Gentlemen - ushers for wedding functions
threaten to come into vogue. At the pairing
of Mr. William Grant, aforesaid, four tall
good - looking Johnnies were a feature of
the show, acting as ushers, and doing the
work of pew - openers. The bride’s family
(some of them) tripped ’Ome recently, and
came back enlightened on all such matters of cere-
mony. But, last year, male guests were ‘ em-
ployed ’ for the first time at the fashionable nuptials
of Miss Ella Staughton with Mr. Seton Williams,
Toorak. A gentleman-usher must have the
qualifications of a first-class coachman, and be
warranted not to lose his temper when on duty.

one better than the Barn-dance—has quite caught
on in Melb. Lady Holled-Sinith had it ‘ starred ’
at her shivoo last week ; and all who hop are sup-
posed to know it, therefore dilatory ones are tak-
mg a private lesson in the ‘ Post ’ (one being suf-
ficient) at half-a-crown a head.
“ The story of Lady Brassey’s private and con-
fidential gathering of 250 ‘friends’ was not to
have appeared in print, and the one penwoman
who took part in the select ‘dissipation’ was
sworn on the Bible, or her best bonnet, or some
other sacred article, to keep the details unspotted
from the Press. However, the wife of a fashion-
able medicine-man gave the dance away to a sas-
siety organ, hence the present gnashing of teeth —
false sets, as a rule —among the ranks of the un-
invited.

“ The craze among the Nicest for beauty-actors
is still raging. Almost the only excitement now
thrilling the souls of the matrons and maids of
Y arratown is struggling with each other to obtain
a favorite leading mummer’s presence at rival ‘ at
homes’ and tea-fights. Invitations to country-
houses also pour in on this idol of the moment,
who seems rather surprised himself at his sudden
burst into favor. On Saturday night a country
train was delayed 10 minutes in starting so that
the actor might be able to catch it after the show
was over!
j wors hip is booming the idol business,
and some deserving, but hitherto slighted, stage
youths are seriously thinking of taking it up as a
profesh. A good-looking juvenile who was doing
small parts with a leading co. was lately promoted
to principal roles vro. tem., and consequently used
to turn up at rehearsals every day with a cool
looming ’ to his old chums, and gloom apart with
folded arms as if the weight of his genius was too
heavy to admit of any frivoling. But the other
youthful pros., so far from being impressed,
chaffed him so unmercifully for ‘ putting on dog’
that Adonis had to take off his dignity with his
stage clothes and relapse into the give-and-take of
every-day.
I ashionable clothes-builders in all the Austra-
lian cities have most reason of all tradesfolks to
be dispirited over the jubilee exodus. Drapery
firms in all places have shown shrunken trade
this winter in satins, silks, and other high-priced
varieties, the usual buyers of such having all taken
their purses to Bond and Regent streets to ex-
change the contents for the latest London styles.
So prices are low in Melb. just now, and the sale-
bargains are really genuine.
A. dreary wedding in St. Hilda’s most toney
church, last week, showed all the guests arrayed
in fresh-looking winter garb. Usually at this
season a swell wedding gives hints of the coming
spring in bonnets prematurely revealed to good
customers as a special favor. But this year, with
a season s stocks on the drapers’ hands, women
have to be still content to outvie each other
in well-established modes. The Jubilee is the
depression of ’97. Yet there were no bigger
flare-ups in the week of gas and grovel than those
displayed by the big drapers—either magnanimity
or a bold ad. on their part.
“Mrs. Harrison Lee, the Melbourne temper-
ance advocate, who has just returned from an
English trip, tells an audience that since she has
been away her husband has gained 91b. in weight.
-There is rather a startling lack of relevance in this
account of the gentleman’s drift to leeward. It
would be interesting if Mrs. H.L. would report
again on her husband’s weight from time to time.
It is evidently an interesting case.
“Gov. Buxton has been trotting about Mel-
bourne a good deal during the past week, and his
rusty-gate-hinge voice has been heard at many
functions. It would be a good thing for Brassey
to keep Bux. for a week or two and show him
about if as to say: ‘You grumble at me, but
how would you like this awful object for a Gover-
nor ? Look at his Nonconformist whiskers, listen
to his grating voice, observe the icy glitter of his
eye, and then thank heaven and the Colonial
Office that you were saved from such a horror.’

Butler’s last message should provide a
chestnutty epitaph :
O, children, recollect my fate,
And fly from Drink’s degrading clutch.
Drink brought me too my present state—
I simply took a drop too much.

ted by the Melb. “ right
thinking ” gang as a noble
and devoted Imperialist for

maintaining the Ruperts-

J. A, Read, who died the other day at
Manly, was remarkable for more things than being
the real inventor of the “ Mackinnon ” reservoir-
pen. He had literary leanings, had been a contri-
butor to London Punch, and was, when chief
clerk of N.S.W. Supreme Court, so noted for
absence of mind that, in order to test the asser-
tion that he would sign any document laid before
him, a bogus warrant for his own execution was
submitted to him. And—“ good, easy, loveable
old fellow he signed it!
C. J. Hodges, Vic. Chinese interpreter,
writes of O’Hara Burke, the ill-fated leader of the
Burke and Wills expedition :
Pen, ink and paper made up an abomination unto him.
I soon discovered this, and humored his foible. The
papers prepared at the end of the month for him to sign
I put in a pile before him in my private room. As he
signed I turned each over. Invariably, some vouchers
and cheques were returned endorsed, “signature illegi-
ble.”
Yet Burke was, according to Hodges, a most
enthusiastic disciplinarian.
GAOL-DRILL.
WARDER CASEY: “ Now sh and, the lot o’ yez, in a loine, wid yeer feet in the forrum of a shquare.
At the worrd WAN, trow yeer roight arrum out to full lingth, as if yez were hitting a
man a clout behind his ear — which, no doubt, most of yez have done whin we wasn’t
luk-king—at the same toime houlding yeer hand flat raid the thoomb at roight angles
to the body. At the worrd TWO, bind yeer elbow, at the same toime catching yeer wind-
poipe wid the thoomb and forefinger. At the worrd THREE, cut away yeer hand to yeer
roight ear, and drop it to yeer soide. This motion, loike all other motions in gaol-dhrill,
has a moral, and it alludes to the pinnelty which awaits yez, if yez don’t mind yeer ways.”
Police - Inspector Latimer to barrister
O’Connor in the middle of a serious case: “ I’ll
not allow you to jeer at the honesty of constables
in general. There are very few constables in
gaol, but plenty of lawyers.” Barrister O’Connor :
“ Impudent officer ! ”
The modest and able B. E. Minns, the
Inverell (N.S. W.) water-color and black-and-white
artist, whose heads of aborigines are among the
most valuable possessions of the Australian sec-
tion of N.S. W. National Gallery, is prospering in
England. When last mail left he had just finished
illustrating a serial by F. C. Phillips, of “ As in
the Looking-glass ” fame, and was starting to
illustrate a series of short stories for Pearson’s
Magazine.
OCULAR PROOF.
PURCHASER : “You guaranteed this cow as a first-
rate milker When I bought her, but you
never told me she had only one eye. ”
AUCTIONEER: “Well, what of that? Were you
going to milk her through the eye ? ”
One can hardly congratulate Canon Stone-
Wigg (a most worthy churchman) on his elevation
to the Bishopric of New Guinea—for acceptance
of that dignity implies residence in the country.
“And,” said a gaunt Papuan missionary to the
writer, “you can’t get acclimatised. The very
natives suffer from fever in the wet seasons, so
there isn’t much hope for a white man.”
John MTntyre, the outcast leader of Vic.
Opposition, lets it be known that he was “ much
disappointed on finding he was not to have any
official recognition in the Jubilee procession.”
Poor man ! The new-made Australian knight
who goes to England to swell round under his
trumpery title is about as sensible as the man
who visits Brummagem to sport his pinchbeck
jewellery.
According to Sydney D. T. ’s special corres-
pondent, Premier Reid, on arriving in England,
“ said he looked forward to enjoying himself im-
mensely in the old country, but was haunted by
the fear that he would be caught taking a nap at
some of the many functions. The Imperial In-
stitute dinner he seemed to fear more especially,
and he expressed a hope that he would be placed
between gentlemen to whom he could confide his
weakness, and ask their kindly aid in stalling off
‘nature’s soft nurse.’” Yet, after all, he did
snatch “ forty winks ” at that dinner.
Yag. : “ Hancock, Yic. M.L.A., is one of
that most unfortunate species—brainy men who
refuse to be taken seriously. Sitting as a member
of the Old-Age Pensions Committee, he will hold
the table silent in admiration of a brilliant list of
suggestions, or a cut of cross-examination good
enough to shine out anywhere; and then—in
stumbles a bald and unconvincing jest, a horse-
laugh, and a patronising smile and reply from the
‘superior ’ witness. Clever as Hancock is, no big
Earty will permanently take him as leader—per-
aps with prudence, for there is too often little
stability in this kind of man. Nevertheless, when
one hears so much of ‘ a saving sense of humor,’ it
is melancholy to reflect on a man being damned
for an excess of it. I have known Hancock begin
a political speech by a long, circus-like disquisi-
tion upon the local beer. Of course, it gained him
the goodwill of his audience; but, after that, what a
plague did any one of them care what he said about
the shipping scandals ? They waited only for more
jokes about beer. On the other side, I have seen
an old-school, ARGUS-fed parson com out of the
Leg. Assembly debates swearing by Hancock’s
talent and temperance. ‘ The breezy Labor-
member ’ is capable of making big impressions ;
but who shall follow a buffoon ? The Argus got
a nasty one home when it wrote: ‘ The people
respect and admire Trenwith, but they love their
’Ancock.’ He is something like the Fool in
Shakespeare’s ‘ Lear ’—brain under bells. And
the bells jangle.”
