Queenslanders will appreciate this notice
of Kinnaird Rose (formerly editor of Brisbane
Courier) clipped from a recent Argonaut :
The best work of the Graeco-Turkish campaign has beer
done by W. Kinnaird Bose, one of the leading correspoa
dents of Reuter’s and the Associated Press. He is »
Scotchman of about 50 years —a tall, sinewy, robust man
of fine physique. Born and educated in Glasgow, he wsi
the special correspondent of the Scotsman during th*
Busso-Turkish war of 1877-78, and distinguished himwlf
by his energy and gallantry. He was with the Russtas
army at Plevna, witnessed the capture of the Gravity
redoubt, was constantly under fire, and was wounded
several times. He also carried off a decoration from th»
Czar for his humane conduct in caring for the wounded
After the war he revisited the Balkan as a special oem>
missioner employed in investigating the condition t> f
the Christian subjects of the Sultan Upon his »
turn Mr. Gladstone expressed high appreciation of h 5»
services, but strongly advised him to abandon journalise
and to study law. He rashly followed this counsel and
became a barrister, and, after an arduous struggle, b*
came convinced that he had no special talent for the law
He went out to Queensland and was editor of the Brisbane
Courier. Recently he returned to Scotland, and, having
suffered from heavy financial losses, was wavering between
a return to the bar and a resumption of work in English
journalism. The outbreak of hostilities in the East re-
kindled his ardor as a war-corresjionden'. and he eageri;
accepted a commission to fpltew’the Greek army. R*
was a witness of the extraordinary stampede of the panic
stricken forces, and made his way from Larissa to Vole
Telegraphic communication with London was denied him.
and he took a small boat and headed down the coast. A
storm came up and he lay off the shore, suffering from ex
posure to cold and being in no little peril. Dayligh*
came, and he landed and rode post haste to Athens,
where he telegraphed to the Associated Press and
Reuter’s the first account of the Greek stampede. It wa»
the best bit of descriptive writing which has been printed
for many a day, full of movement and picturesque detail,
and fairly dramatic in interest. Nothing which has
appeared in the war columns of the press has been so
■eh admired. Mr. Rose subsequently returned to ths
ON FLEM TNG TON. —John Hancock,
Trades Hall's Fat Man.
front, and sent to London an exceedingly graphicaccom
of the closing scenes of demoralisation in the Grert
camp. His description of the inexplicable stampede K
however, the great stroke of the war-correspondejrif*
cam paign. Mr. Rose is the author of a life of General
skobeloff, whom he has described as the modern Bayard;
and he will undoubtedly write a book on the war b>
Thessaly.

WATSON’S WHISKY.
Why Don’t Men Marry ?
Statistics show an increased percentage of
, female over male births in Australia, which, ac-
companied by the increase of single men of
marriageable age, represents a serious question.
The same perplexed question is answered in France
by claims of immorality. Here we must look for
some other cause, for the morality among the
, young men in Australia has never been creator
than at the present time. Nor can it be attri-
buted to “ hard times,” for the census show that the
marriages among men of moderate means have
not diminished, but it is among young men of
leisure and wealth that this important question
is treated with nonchalance and indifference. A
careful observer claims that a woman is, unwit-
tingly, the cause and to blame for this. An
eligible young man receives a note of regret from
the society belle, saying “Kindly relieve me from
my evening’s engagement for the concert. I am
prostrated by a nervous headache.” Or, if she
accepts, she admits the programme entertaining
complains of “ extreme fatigue.” She must
not be exposed to a draught, “which is sure to
bring on an attack of neuralgia,” or at dinner she
demurely refuses this or that article of nourish-
ment, modestly saying, “ I feel it necessary to
be abstemious if I would enjoy good health.”
The acute young man naturally concludes that “-a
weakly bud will make a frail and withered rose,”
and does not care to assume the responsibility of
such a burden for life. There are two wrongs
which ought to be righted in such cases. It is
wrong to nourish imaginary ailments ; and it is
also wrong to ignore marked tendencies to periodi-
cal headaches, melancholy, nervousness, flatu-
lency, or distress, after eating, when it can be
promptly checked by using Warner’s “ Safe ”
Cure. This medicine has become a household
word among the ladies of Great Britain and Aus-
tralia, who have found that a short, safe, timely,
and comparatively inexpensive treatment ensures
health in the fullest acceptation of the term.*

A Privy Councillorship will soon, like the
Order of the Garter, have “ no damned suspicion
of merit about it. ”
A trite saying in the mouth of a leading
statesman may become electric. Thus with Bar-
ton’s remark that Australia is the first power to
launch off with a whole continent.

have him there as a fixture, and our little
iealousies couldn’t stand it 1 ”
A MEASURE OF PRECAUTION.
“ Why do they always sing duets? ”
“It divides the risk.”
Q. Acting-Premier Tozer has sensibly
declared himself in favor of abolishing the attach-
ment of wages on the ground that it is better to
protect wages than to encourage credit.
Dear Bulletin, —You aio
right when you say that the busi-
ness of an Australian Volunteer
is to defend Australia. It is all
that we (I speak as a volunteer
officer) are sworn to do ; and, per-
sonally, I am careful to impress
upon all recruits that their duty
and oath bind them to defend
their native land only in case of
attack. This is an honorable
kind of fighting, which cannot,
as a rule, be said of aggressive
warfare. Australia’s policy should
be one of peace. T say this, though
I fully believe in volunteering,
combined with the encouragement
of gymnastics and manly exercises.
—Yours, &c., Miles.

(Q.), suggests that the Government

platform— to dish the Labor-party !

their supineness at the recent Leg. Council elec-
tions. The Early Closing of Shops Bill, thanks

take their little fad out of the road. The chances
of having any constitution in which to recognise
God or anything else, are not so bright just now
as to be worth talking about.

From Kiama, the Home of the Ghost: —
No. 12, Loyal Orange Lodge, celebrated the Battle of
the Boyne by a banquet There was a large attendance,
five lodges being represented. Bro. Francis Abigail, the
lodge delegate, delivered an address, in the course of
which he announced that he intended offering his ser-
vices to the public at the next general election.
The Kingston Govt, is now a year older
than any other Ministry that ever held office in
S.A. Of the original half-dozen members who
were sworn in on June 15, ’93, only Kingston,
Holder and Cockburn now remain. Playford is
Agent-General, Gillen is dead, and Gordon suc-
cumbed to the Hospital dispute.
Some measures that Q. wants badly and
at once : A workmen’s-lien bill; a bill to prevent
workmen’s wages from being garnisheed (to say
nothing of Friendly Societies’ “ sick pay,” already
here noticed); a bill to bring shearing-sheds under
the Factories’ Act. (By the way, when is the Q.
Factories’ Act going to be really administered ?)
A boom item. In 1896, Westralia imported
goods and specie to the value of £6,400,000, and
exported to the value of £1,600,000 (or just one-
quarter the amount) to pay for them. She owes
most of the balance. And, as it is impossible to
owe a balance like that every year, The Bulletin
still says that the W.A. boom is only a boom, and
the burst must follow.
N.S.W. Minister Carruthers states that he
is personally in favor of not placing the post
offices under Federal control. Carruthers is an
ardent Federationist who wants to create a
Federal Government and give it no control over
anything. Even with the post-offices thrown in,
the number of important matters which the Com-
monwealth Government will have control of, as
things are now arranged, can almost be counted
on the forefinger of one hand.
The omission of the words, “subsequent
provisions of this Act,” which appear in the
Canadian Act, from the Federal section dealing
with date of coming into operation of the Austra-
lian Act, are said, and appear to make, a mull of
the preceding section referring to the proclamation
of the Queen’s assent. The two sections will pro-
bably, as at present, be construed thuslv : “ This
Act may come into operation on any day not more
than six months later than the Greek Kalends.”
TRAVELLING FOR HIS HEALTH, — (By Hop's Understudy.)
According to press cable, Premier Braddon,
of Tasmania, got this notable sentiment off his
chest recently in England: “The time has come
when the colonies must either separate or remain
as they are.” In other words, the hour has arrived
when we must suddenly keep on as we have been
doing all along. Consequently, we continue sitting
still with deafening speed.
Yet another attempt is being made to
establish a Vic. Labor paper. This time it is to
have the official approval of the Trades’ Hall.
The committee contains Bishop and Finley, two
Trades’ Hall ex-presidents; Prendergast, M.P. ;
and Casimir, pedagogue, socialist and orator. The
platform of the paper is gravely set out at length
in 74 items. The Tocsin is to issue weekly at a
penny a time.

Canadian Premier Laurier is bringing in a
novel Alcohol-Prohibition Bill, providing' for the
settlement of the question by vote. The questions
are:—
(1) Do you approve of prohibition as an abstract theory?
(2) Do you approve also of the imposition of direct
taxation to the amount of 10,000,000 dollars a year to
make up the deficiency in revenue which would be caused
by prohibition ?
Naturally, the nocent waterspouts are opposing
the bill vociferously. Laurier has some hundred-
weights of horse-sense and a lot of humor con-
cealed about his person.
The advocates of free admission to the
N.S.W. legal profession are singularly unfortunate
in their choice of parliamentary backers. Prima-
facie, nocent waterspouts Dan O’Connor and J. C.
Neild would damn any cause,, though it must be
admitted that the latter has much useful legisla-
tion at his credit. Why doesn’t the “ higher
power ” Labor-party take up the matter ? Mean-
while, as the present legal practitioners have a
close monopoly, they should be made to pay for it,
like publicans, auctioneers, and pawnbrokers. In
the United Kingdom solicitors pay to the Trea-
sury .£6O on admission and £5 ss. annual license
fee. Why not in Australia ?
“N.” to The Bulletin :
I question whether the Age would have the generosity
of the Argus when it published, in a cable re the rifle-
shooting shivoo, that three cheers were given for Mr.
Syme. For my part, with the retention of these gentle-
manly amenities, I would like to see a healthy antagonism
between the two papers, one square for Labor, the other
for Capital. No matter which. They are equally capi-
talistic. Nor does the Age being Protectionist at all alter
the fact. Both only coquet with Labor, and both about
equally. Fleming and Co., the raddiest Rads., now
barrack for the Argus, and loath the Age, which will not
give them a line, whereas the Argus ostentatiously drags
them out-even from obscurity. As for Hancock’s morn-
ing paper, it never was anything but bunkum, and now
he has patched up with “King David”—his own con-
temptuous expression—again.
Religious announcements in the Sydney-
suburban and N.S.W. country press : “The Hon.
Joseph Cook, Postmaster-General, will preach at
Toongabbie Primitive Methodist Church, morn-
ing and evening, Sunday next.” “The Hon.
Jacob Garrard, Minister for Public Instruction,
will address the Salvation Army.” The Hon.
Brunker trots round with a gospel trumpet, the
Hon Gould shakes a church chancellorship, and
the Hon. Carruthers gets run in as brother of the
Rev. Carruthers. That’s one reason why they’re
called a “ God-fearing ” Ministry. Another
reason, say their enemies, is that if they don’t fear
God more than preceding Ministers have done,
they ought to.

“ H. ” : “It angers me to get such miserable
driblets of news from the United States, by cable
all filtered through jealous Bnlldom, and ‘ faked 5
to keep us in tutelage to Royalty. Twenty years
ago the U.S. ship Tuscarora plumbed the ocean
floor from Maoriland to ’Frisco, and found that
Nature had provided just such another nice cable-
bed as that between Maoriland and Australia. And
all the intrigues of the English will never stop
that lovely wire from being laid, and speaking
with most miraculous organ.”
Newton discovered gravitation. Brunker,
bless him ! has discovered flexibility. Says the
sage of Maitland : “All laws have to be carried
out with a certain degree of flexibility.” Now
one knows why the penalty for theft is eased to
the respectable kleptomaniac and administered
neat to the low prig. Also, why moleskin gets
flogged for evil behaviour and broadcloth for the
same offence is let down light. Flexibility pro-
vides due explanation for all such apparent eccen-
tricities—and to think of Bmotian Brunker having
discovered it 1

Why doesn’t George Rignold promptly revive Joseph
of Canaan ” with a slim fellow in the name-part, and him-
self cast for the wicked priest? Properly produced,
“Joseph” would be a big thing—would draw. Now is
the boomtime for religious costume-dramas. The appetite
of the occasional playgoer who wants to enjoy a “ great
moral lesson” has been whetted. He has seen the
amorous Marcus chasing Mercia when the lights go out,
and is just in the cue for shuddering at Mrs. Potiphar’s
attack upon holy Joe.
Pinero’s clever comedy, “ The Hobby Horse,” was to be
revived in London on May 16, after a ten years’ retire-
ment. “ The Hobby Horse ” was not sufficiently strong
in plot and dramatio orthodoxy for the public of 1887,
but in the light of modern dialogue plays it is now re-
garded as quite up to date.

Latest coster song.
“ I lost £5OOO with a company,” says Martin Simonsen,
who came out to assist at his son’s recent benefit in
Sydney. “ It was half English, half Italian. The mixture
disagreed. There was Almo, Lilian Tree (now intending
to enter a convent), Lablache, Dimetresco, Walshe, St.
Cliir, Pimazzoni, &c. The two countries fought one
night in the last act of ‘Carmen,’ at the Opera House.
The English bulldogs won, I believe. I had gone home
before the sorimmage.”
Ethel Haydon, it seems, went to London under the
guarantee of an engagement from Irving, with whom her
family was acquainted. This was an open sesame for
her anywhere.
The Melb. Jubilee Fever Hospital has been the excuse
for a good many benefit performances which didn’t result
in a pen’orth of benefit for the fund. The waste of
“charity," in connection with amateur theatrical per-
formances, fetes, &c., is quite painful to witness.
Max O’Rell, even as did Christie Murray, has taken to
the stage, acting in his own play, “On the Continong,”
in the English provinces.
If it be true that George Musgrove couldn’t arrange
terms for an Australian tour of the Haverley Minstrels,
the Firm may congratulate itself on a lucky escape, Thu
Bulletin fancies. This country is distinctly Hinglish in
its moderate appetite for minstrel business, and the sight
of half-a-dozen blackened performers executing a com-
plicated step-dance with awful precision wouldn’t rouse
very much enthusiasm. Haverley’s Minstrels tried to take
London by storm in 1880, and proved a phenomenal
“ frost.” The town was billed as it had never been billed
before, also the vast Her Majesty’s Theatre was “papered”
on a scale never previously attempted, but the paying
public remained calm and unmoved.
According to a recent interviewer’s account, Melba
seems to have developed quite as much “ frill ” as she can
carry. “In her magnificent bed Marie Antoinette had
slept; upon the very same sofa had she reclined.” Which
raises the question—from whom did Melba acquire that
bed? Hey!
The dreaded rack in “The Sign of the Cross’ is an
old-time “Charley’s” rattle.
S.S. : “Melb. Alexandra, alias the Queen’s and Her
Majesty’s, has a most wonderful drop-soene. The Exhibi-
tion Building, elevated and condensed, occupies the
back-ground. On some stone steps in the front a browny
black-gin is squatted, nursing a pieaninny like a fat ex-
hibit from the waxworks, whilst a nondescript nigger on
the left is anxiously inquiring the road to the nearest
pub. But the gem of the whole thing is a female wallaby
with a pouch-vent like a post-hole, out of which is thrust
the head of an adult kangaroo. What becomes of the
rest of the kangaroo is not explained. But the picture
serves a noble purpose, since audiences sit stupidly through
the intervals fascinated by the phenomenon and forget
to go out for drinks.”
At the close of a recent University Extension lecture on
“Macbeth,” at a Melb. suburban Mechanics’lnstitute, a
well-known barrister who has claims to some critical
knowledge of Shakespeare, summed up the business of
the evening thusly : “Macbeth murdered sleep, the lec-
turer murdered Macbeth.”
Dear Bulletin, —As one of vhose “ young Australians”
whose want of enthusiasm regarding that hard, dry,
British person, Harry Paulton, has proved a red rag to
the “Melbourne” Bull, may I speak a piece? We say
Paulton is like Toole, and that displays guile and hatred
for England! Well, the fact remains, Paulton is a second,
small, revised edition of Johnny Toole, and the resem-
blance when the former appears in “My Friend from
India ”is glaring. Johnny the antique would have played
this part much as Paulton plays it, even if the latter does
not jerk his head and make mugs—played it in a fat,
slow, gouty way, with a certain tired appreciation of the
humor of the thing, despite the obvious fact that the
persecuted barber should be a spry young man. As for
Goodwin in “ Mizzoura ” being praised for playing the
sheriff, leaning up against things and drawling scant
sentences, it is enough to say that Goodwin was repre-
senting a man who spoke in a slow, drawling way, and
who habitually leaned up against things, and who was
one of a type that drawls, leans, and chews meditatively,
and yet “ Melbourne ” would have that kind of character
played at a hand-gallop. Re the last lines of “ Mel-
bourne’s” letter, I have observed that the Britisher,
fatuously appreciative of all things English, squares his
prejudices with an intense hatred for aught that comes
from America.—S.S.

Ed. B., —One of your correspondents says: “ As original
as Ohirgwin’s dance with the churchwarden pipes.” This
was a bar-room trick ages before Chirgwin was born.
Another says :—“ Pattie Brown is as slim as ever.” Now
I saw Pattie in a “ Night Out ” last Oct. in London. She
is now almost the image of Miss Watt-Tanner.—X.

Melb. Age is wincing at the persistent
references to its policy of hush with regard to
movements it doesn’t patronise. It had a long
and lame paragraph in the other day explaining
why it refused to insert an advt. announcing a
meeting of unemployed for the purpose of peti-
tioning Gov. Brassey. Its reasons were almost as
undemocratic and time-serving as anything of the
sort that has yet appeared in the Age.

purpose of working up the Imperial sentiment .”
And that’s how the milk gets into the cocoanut—
with the “ loyal ” Australian press to boom the
transfer.
Dear Bulletin, —You speak of W. N.
Willis plotting to dethrone Lyne and reinstate
Dibbs. Rats ! Even a conspirator must be pos-
sible. Willis is not. This scheme to bluff Reid
into making Dibbs Agent-General won’t hold
water, anyhow. Who imagines that Reid wants
to get rid of Damn Chicago? An army with two
leaders personally hostile to each other is easier to
lick than the same force with only one—and well
does Reid know it. Abbott may have the billet if
he likes, but never the man with “ the sprawling
intellect. ’’—Corny.
Fitz Gibbon, of Melbourne’s Board of
Works, was made quite happy by his Jubilee
“honor,” and he now signs himself officially—
“E. G. Fitz Gibbon, Esq., J.P., Commander of
Michael and George.” If a deputation of citizens
were to present him with a brass helmet, he
would wear it with enthusiasm. As a humorless
Irishman, this rare old bird is incapable of feeling
ridiculous. He takes even his own poems seri-
ously. “There is no fun in me,” said Fitz. at a
recent Vic. artists’ smoke-night gathering. He
said it neither proudly nor regretfully, but just as
a solid statement of himperial fact for the infor-
mation of any among Her Majesty’s liege subjects
who might have supposed that he was apt to joke
on his birthday, or other historical occasions.
There is no fun in Fitz Gibbon.
Early in the Federal debate N.S.W.
Assembly affirmed, by an overwhelming majority,
that it believed in two Houses. Two nights later,
by an even bigger majority, it practically gave the
Government of the day power to dissolve the
Senate at its pleasure, thus stripping the second
Chamber of every vestige of power and dignity,
and to all intents and purposes declaring its abso-
lute belief in one House only. Three days later it
again decided that the Senate was to . have a life
of six years. And yet some members wonder why
the present Parliament is, in the eyes of its
creators, an utterly discredited body ! As a
result, Federation is still in the air so far .as
N.S.W. politics are concerned. The broader in-
telligence of the Convention will probably bring it
down to earth again, and then the people will
probably grab at it, if only to smother their im-
becile provincial representatives for ever.
N.S.W. Acting-Premier Brunker’s refusal
to assist in extending hospitality to the French
troops per Tanais is a very ugly circumstance and
will need a great deal of explanation. As the
daily papers have reported, the Tanais, a French
mail-steamer, broke down on her way from Noumea
and was towed into Sydney about a fortnight
behind time. On board her were some 65 French
soldiers who should have left Sydney to return
home on 14th July, but are now detained till the
next steamer leaves on 11th Aug. The Tanais is
going into dock for repairs; and it will be very in-
convenient to keep the men aboard her, and even
more inconvenient to billet them round town in
detachments. French soldiers are decent fellows,
but they are not saints ; and, especially when
they are in a foreign city, it is desirable that
they be kept under reasonable discipline. Yet it
is obviously impossible for two or three officers to
maintain discipline over a dozen scattered knots of
men. So the French Consul-General, M. Biard
d’Aunet, applied to Acting-Premier Brunker to
know if the Government could aid him in his
difficulty by finding quarters at Paddington
barracks or elsewhere to keep the men together
till their boat leaves ; and (though the local mili-
tary authorities were ready and even eager to afford
the Frenchmen every hospitality) Acting-Premier
Brunker replied that the Government could not.
Of course, payment would have been forthcoming
for any expense entailed. But, in any case,
the Consul’s request was so small, and the courtesy
involved was so important, that one would think
Govt, would have hurried to place all its re-
sources at his disposal. Acting-Premier Brunker’s
action looks like a stupid and unnecessary slight
to a friendly nation ; whether he can explain it in
any other way remains to be seen. The French
Consul-General has thanked Mr. Brunker for his
courteous reply to the Consul-General’s letter, and
regretted that there should have been a refusal of
hospitality—especially as not long ago the French
authorities and people gave to the ship-wrecked
crew of H.M.S. Sultan a welcome befitting the
flag of their country.

Doing a Day’s Washing.
Of all the duties that fall to the lot of the house-
wife whose means do not permit of her indulging
in the luxury of a paid “ help,” perhaps the most
cordially detested is “washing.” But there are
some people who make light of even this laborious
task, as will be seen by the perusal of a reporter a
interview with Mrs. Mary Brown, of 71 Simmons-
street, Enmore
“ I have called, Mrs. Brown,’ said our reporter,
“to ascertain the truth of the rumor that has
reached us of your having recently suffered from a
severe attack of rheumatism.’
“It is quite true, but I am now happy to relate
that I feel as well as I ever did. I have been
suffering on and off from rheumatism for the last
five years, and the slightest change in the weather
used to give me most awful twinges. It chiefly
affected my arms, and I can assure you, sir, that
it took me all my time to do my hair. As to lilt-
ing this tub, that was a matter of sheer nu possi-
bility. If it had not been for some of my friends
I could never have gotten through my household
duties. This complaint appeared to affect my
entire system, and as to the money I have spent
in doctors’ advice and so-called remedies, why it
makes me positively disgusted to think of. 1
began to think that if my sufferings continued life
was not worth living. I rememb 1 r one day, as I
was sitting by the fire and endeavoring to employ
my mind by reading the ‘ Evening News that an
advertisement caught my eye headed Ur. Wil-
liams’ Pink Pills for Pale People cures Rheu-
matism, Sciatica, &c.’ Now, having wasted so
much money already, I was at first loth to try any
more advertised medicines, but I thought in for a
penny in for a pound, so I invested in three boxes
of this specific. , , wi
“ I hope you were careful to ask for Ur. wil-
liams’ Pink Pills ? ” _ T v a
“ I took particular notice to do that, for I had
read of so many worthless imitations that I was
careful to ask for, and obtain, the genuine article.
“We think it highly probable, Mrs. Brown,
that before long some of the compounders of these
worthless imitations will be spending time in
seclusion, with the accompanying eccentricities of
diet, official garb, and escort.” .
“And indeed it would only serve them right.
Brown (my husband) laughed at me when I told
him I was going in for a course of Dr. Williams
Pink Pills, and said, ‘ Oh, you are going to chuck
away some more money, are you ? ’
“ I said ‘ Yes, I am,’ and right glad am I that I
ran the risk of ‘ chucking away some money, for I
am happy to tell you that they have done me all
the good in the world. Talk about the new
woman, why I feel now a brand new woman, and
my husband only this morning said to me ‘Well,
you certainly look the “ pink ’ of perfection now.
I suppose it is your Pink Pills that have done it.
And so it is. Not only have I lost my rheumatic
pains, but my appetite has returned, and I can
now enjoy my food. In fact, my appetite is so
good that Brown says I will eat him out of house
and home.”
“How many boxes have you taken, Mm.
Brown?” . , _ ,
“ I have had seven boxes m all, and 1 have so
much belief in them that I intend always to keep
a box by me, and I shall recommend them to all
my friends. You are quite at liberty to publish
this statement, as I believe in giving credit to
whom credit is due.”
The reporter then withdrew, and reflected as he
walked slowly away that here at least was another
case in which the many virtues of this marvellous
preparation had been exhaustivelyand conclusively
illustrated.
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills are not like other
medicines, and their effects are permanent.
Nothing else is so prompt in pulling up the system
when, from some temporary depression or other-
wise, tone is needed. They have cured more than
fifty-five hundred cases of diseases arising from
impoverished blood, such as anaemia, pale and sal-
low complexion, muscular weakness, depression of
spirits, loss of appetite, palpitation of the heart,
shortness of breath, pains in the back, nervous
headache, loss of memory, early decay, all forms
of female weakness, hysteria, paralysis, locomotor
ataxy, rheumatism and sciatica, scrofula, rickets,
hip-joint diseases, chronic erysipelas, consumption
of the bowels and lungs.
The genuine pills are sold only in wooden boxes,
about two inches in length, in a white wrapper
with the full name, Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for
Pale People, printed in red. They are never sold
in bulk, or from glass jars, and any dealer who
offers substitutes in this form should be avoided.
In case of doubt it is better to send direct to the
Dr. Williams’ Medicine Company, Queen’s Cham-
bers, Queen’s Place, Sydney, enclosing the price,
2s. 9d. a box, or six boxes for 14s. 9d.
“These pills are not a purgative, and they con-
tain nothing that could injure the most delicate.*

There are now 14 millions of Singer sew-
ing machines in use throughout the world. This
immense demand is solid evidence of the world-
wide popularity obtained by these machines, as-
sisted by the easy terms of payment on which
they are sold. Since the Singer Manufacturing
Company inaugurated its system of hire with
option of purchase, it has been taken advantage of
by the public to an extraordinary extent. This
system has been the means of enabling an immense
number of all classes to become possessed of these
most reliable sewing-machines, and it always offers
peculiar advantages to parties in want of machines
for domestic use by affording a thorough trial
before purchase. From the numerous inquiries
received by the company, it would appear that
there are yet many who are anxious to procure a
Singer’s machine under this system, but who lack
information as to its details. The co. invites
every one in want of a sewing-machine to write to
the head office, 99 Clarence-street, Sydney (or the
co. ’s agents located in every large town in Aus-
tralia). Price-lists and full particulars will be
supplied. *
Now that Ranjitsinjhi is coming for cer-
tain, the question is: how will he shape on the
strange, fast, Australian wickets ? The cricket
axiom is that a true cricketer is a cricketer any-
where, and there seems to be nothing in Australian
conditions to detract from the established bril-
liance of the first batsman in England. The quick-
ness of his eye will not be affected by the glare of
the Austral sun, for Ranjitsinjhi was bom and
bred under the equatorial glare of India. True,
the Indian Prince learnt all his cricket on English
wickets, and developed his cunning of eye and
hand there. But his eye is so exceptionally cun-
ning, and his arms and wrists so perfectly
supple, that there appears to be no reason
why he shouldn’t glance Jones off his nose
in Australia as he did when scoring his
famous 153 in England. Moreover, being a
mere youth and an enthusiastic cricketer as well,
he should speedily adapt himself to new sur-
roundings. Iredale, Gregory, Trott, Donnan,

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Rosebery Park (Sydney) racecourse added to its
list of slain and maimed, last week : Thomas Rooke was
killed outright, and Ernest Crockett seriously injured.
Those who have never seen a field of horses turn into the
straight on a soup-plate course, each jockey fighting for
position, can have no conception of the terrible risk run
by the poor devils—many of them, like Rooke, married
men with families—who ride on pony-courses for bread.
It certainly seems invidious to howl over a jockey killed
on a pony-course, and receive in silence the death or
maiming of horse and man on aFlemington or Randwick ;
in Melbourne they have been killing their steeplechase
horses and maiming their jockeys with clocklike regu-
larity for weeks past. The surroundings of the average
soup-plate course are such that it would be a distinct
gain to humanity to improve it off the face of the earth,
but it takes a fine perception to distinguish between a
dangerous and unfashionable small course and a large,
safe and fashionable course made dangerous with
obstacles.
* * * *
Yet another jockey butchered to make a holiday
for pony thieves and mugs. By the way, why don’t the
pony-course people hold a benefit meeting or two in aid
of the hospitals on which they cast so many maimed and
bleeding burthens ?
* « * »
Melbourne is capable of providing some
pretty low-down forms of thievery. On the local race-
courses it is no longer safe for a jockey to get a spill and
knock in his skull, because he is no sooner down than the
good Samaritans rush in and rob him of everything
detachable right down to his socks. One jockey, stunned
in a fall at a suburban course, was robbed, amongst other
things, of a glass-eye. Glennon fell at Caulfield the other
day, and, when he recovered his senses, found that that
was about all he could expect to recover, the Samaritans
having left nothing else belonging to him worth men-
tioning. The V.R.C. has promised to take steps, but
meanwhile no jockey with horse-sense wears his false
teeth in a steeplechase on a Melb. course.
• « » *

off-day meetings, brought big dividends: £lO5 Is., £l9
175., £6 195., £l5 75., £2 Bs., and £3l2s. for the six races
gave the tote another big ad.
The div. of <£los Is. was on Maid of the Mist, a
rank outsider, the total invested on the mare being £7
10s. Which recalls the sensational div. paid on D.O.D. in
S.A. Goodwood Handicap years ago. A fanner named
O’Dea went to Adelaide for the Cup meeting, knowing
little about racing. The similarity in initials to his own,
and the fact that the jockey’s colors were green, decided
him, and he put a sov. on D.0.D., a rank outsider, who
was not seriously considered. But “they run in all
shapes.” D.O.D. won. The farmer’s sovereign was the
only money invested on the horse, and he scooped over
* * * *
On last Steeple day, at Flemington, a poorly-
dressed, old-looking young man was trying to sell six-
penny tips. His now beery face brought back memories
of the days, a fevv years ago, when he owned some of the
best cattle, rode in his carriage, lived in a mansion, swam
in gold-top, and was as tender a pigeon as was ever
plucked by Ikey Mo.
* * * «
Merman, now in England, has been given some
trials and does not seem to have acquitted himself very
creditably. Not much notice can be taken of this, how-
ever. Merman never was a great success as a track-
horse, being one of the “ white-when-he’s-wanted” variety
When acclimatised he should account for something good.
* » » »
Ronda, with which “Black Bill” Forrester
attempted his second coup in Melb. Cup, has never yet
won a race, and has so often been donkevlicked in small
handicaps around Sydney that many now believe him
incapable of winning the smallest event at the minimum
weight. Yet, but for Glenloth he would have won a Cup.
Horses are kittle cattle. Newman’s win reminds one that
he has proved a disappointing and deceiving horse for
even so acute a judge as Kelso.
» * * *
Jockey Jim Hayes seems a source of constant
worry to the V.R.C. The facts of the latest : the V.R.C.
committee recently decided that all disqualified jockeys
should, at the expiration of their term, be required to
apply for a license before riding again. Hayes was offered
the mount at Caulfield on Legs for the Welter, and, his
six months’ “ dis.” having just expired, he asked the
Caulfield stewards for leave to ride. He got it, rode, and
won ; in good style, too. Now the V.R.C. committee is
annoyed at what it considers a snub on the part of the
V.A.T.C. However, as its recommendation had never
been passed by the general body of members, it doesn’t
seem to have a leg to stand on.
♦ * * *
Canterbury Park Cup, N.S.W., run to-day
(Sat.) will doubtless bring out a big field. Kelso,
Blue Vest, Silvertone and Admonition are a likely
quartette to furnish the winner. Beauvoir should win
Parkes Stakes, whilst Bathilda, with a run, reads the
winner of Two-year-old Handicap. The July Handicap
may fall to The Meddler or Lady Trenville, and Temple-
stowe or Juvenile should be good enough to account for
the Welter.
* * « *
Acceptances for Caulfield G.N. Hurdles (four
miles) promise a big field, and Hive, His Grace and First
King appear to possess good winning chances, whilst
Vigil, Britomarte and Peveril are sure to figure promi-
nently in the G.N. Steeple (four miles). Tagus is first
favorite for the Hurdles, but will likely run much more
forward in the Steeplechase if he only stands up.

Jumpers’ flat races, at present greatly in vogue
in Victoria, are to be introduced into N.S. W. This class of
event is akin to the hunters’ flat races at ’Ome, where
they are made the medium of some of the most out-
rageous ramps.
* « * *
While racehorses, taken all in all, are deteriorat-
ing, that more useful animal, the trotter, is improving.
Robert Bonner says that, when he bought his first trot-
ting horse in 1856, only 19 horses, living and dead, had
trotted a mile in 2:30. Now there are more than 13,000
in the list.

Gray’s starting-machine is to be given a trial at
the Newmarket (Eng.) second October meeting.

The huge sum of £30,000 was given away in
stakes at the recent Ascot meeting (Eng.).
The V.R.C. has had a successful season, as after
clearing: off £3555 overdraft, it is left with a credit
o* £4os3 —mainly due to the splendid weather it has
had. A reserve-fund is to be started to provide against
wet seasons.
* * * *
The eycle-on-ferry-steamers problem will attract
much attention in the near future. As matters stand, on
the Manly ferry for instance, scores of cycles are often run
aboard a boat and stacked loose in the alley-ways, &c. If
“anything” happened to cause a panic on board, the
loose cycles would be a terrible element of danger.
• * * •
The Lady, a very high-toned English fashion-
paper, describing a “society” cycle-meet One girl
rode a very smart-looking tandem, painted white, and
behind her sat a negro groom, dressed in neat dark green
livery, with a cockaded hat, doing his share of pedalling.
This is a good idea for ladies who have discarded their
horses for ‘ironmongery,’ yet wish to find employment
for their men-servants, and a man in attendance to take
charge of one’s machine and undertake trifling repairs is
certainly an advantage, to say nothing of his value, when
lonely roads are traversed at dusk.” Just think of it—a
buck nigger riding behind a lady on a lonely road in the
dusk ! How they would smile in Yankee-land !
* * * *
Recent road-racing accidents have had their
warning effect, and Vic. League of Wheelmen has now
prohibited machines of a lighter weight than 231 b. being
used in road-races, and has also debarred racing-tyres.
The latter alteration is quite as important as the other, for
a small-diameter tyre, when pumped up hard, is almost
absolutely unyielding to inequalities of the road, conse-
quently an undue strain is put on the machine. Such
tyres, though safe enough on a smooth track, are simply
suicidal on a rough road.
* * * *
Vic. League of Wheelmen proposes to institute
the French Brassard, or Badge system, during the coming
season. This is a scheme whereby the champion at three
distances—one, five, and ten miles—becomes the possessor
of a badge, which entitles him to a sum of £5 per week
during the racing season. He must, however, defend his
title, if called on to do so, the challenger putting up £lO
as bona-fides. This should prove a good spec, for the
League, as the matches will be run under its control, and
will provide a great draw for little expense.
* * * »
“Jock” Green, the Newcastle (Eng.) rider,
owes his victory in the 25-mile Vic. road championship to
the fine pacing which Kellow has been giving him in
training. One of the top-notchers of England, Green was
bound to distinguish himself here as soon as he became
acclimatised, and his recent successes, both on the track
and the road, prove that he is not far off his best form
now. On Saturday he negotiated the Campbellfield out
and back course in lh. 13min., more than 3min. less than
A. A. Middleton’s time last year. Middleton rode splen-
didjy this time, but an accident to his brother broke up
their pacing arrangements somewhat. Nevertheless, he
was able to battle with Green until 100yds. from home,
when the thick-set Britisher forged ahead, and an un-
known man named Rogers dashed out and finished in
front of the plucky Dunolly miner. Johnstone, the No. 1
of last season, who used to act as rear-guard to every
field in long sc. races, rode splendidly for one half the
distance, when a fall put him out of it. On Saturday’s
form, it certainly looks as though Green would win both
the 50 and 100 miles championships of the Vic. League,
while the E. Melb. Club, which scored most points for
position, will secure the chief premiership with its com-
bination of the two Middletons, Geo. Carpenter, and Kiel.
» * » *
Jack Hellings’ win of the world’s 100yds.
swimming championship, from scratch, at Manchester,
England, with its 117 entries —■ he has since won the
Belgian championship was a performance in itself
justifying the little ’un’s trip. The first time the present
writer saw Hellings was at Holdsworth s boat-shed, Wool-
loomooloo Bay, one Sunday morning, when the youngster,
scarcely able to walk, toddled out to the end of the jetty
and flopped into the water just as a voung seal might do,
and dog-paddled in to the bit of sandy beach. And it
may safely be said that young Hellings made the water
as much his element as earth during the succeeding best
part of a couple of decades. He comes of a briny family,
his father, “ Dick” Hellings, being the crack yacht-sailer
of his day, vvhen the Xarifa and Mistral were fighting out
their historic battles. If the writer’s memory serves
him, old Dick was skipper of the Xarifa when she went
to Melbourne to meet the Hobson Bay cracks, Mr
“Billy” Smythe, late of the Sydney Customs, making one
of the crew.
* * * *
Hellings’ win shows the calibre of Sydney
amateurs as compared with the othersiders. It is acknow-
ledged that Sid. Cavill and “ Mud” Bishop are very little
behind the little chain, ion, whilst there is probably not
more than 3sec. differei ce in the following nine swim-
mers C. Proudfoot, A. Folm ■*, J. Canning, F. Lane,
J. Taylor, Coulsen, C. Wii.n, J. Davidson and Perov
Cavill.
* * - » *
The recent doings of his sons reminds us that it
is now almost 20 years since old-man Cavill swam across
the English Channel—a feat for which he did not obtain
due credit, because the late Captain Matt Webb’s faction,
then omnipotent in the press, raised the objection that,
while taking some light refreshment, Ca\ ill had clung to
a rope. Cavill would have beaten Webb in any kind of
match —long or short.

“ Peter Jackson is by no means the wreck that
American and other papers would make him out to be,”
writes The Bulletin’s London correspondent. “ There is
a calm air of dignity about the battered face with two
purple scars across the depressed nose-bridge and ‘ betune
the eyes,’ and the old warrior looks fit enough to make
the best of the ring and paper-fighters more inclined to
eat thistles than taste his stiff, straight left. Apropos of
the Fitz.-Corbett attack of indigestion, provoked by
Fitz.’s right-timed, right-placed poke, he said : ‘ln the
18th round of my fight with the Pompadour I hit him
hard over the pit, and he never came near me again. My
ankle was useless, from a buggy accident two weeks
beforehand, and I could not go after him, and that is why
our fight lasted 61 rounds and ended as it did. From his
biggest backer in that match I heard long afterwards that
Corbett, urged by his seconds to mix it a bit, refused,
saying : “ That fellow has hit me a terrible blow.’’ Then
I knew his weakness, and also knew that he would never
meet me again. Fitzsimmons says straight out I’m too
good for him.’ ”

From a footballer’s point of view, last Satur-
day's match on Sydney Cricket Ground, between the
Kanawicks and Pirates, was the best game of the season,
although played in the mud and slush. Rain suited the
Ri rates, the lighter team, and they nearly got over no less
than five times, but Randwicks’ defence was simply un-
breakable, and the match ended in a draw.

* * * *
It needs but a few matches like that between
Collingwood and Fitzroy, last Saturday, to renovate foot-
ball to the position it held in Melb. six years ago, and to
transform the half-million inhabitants of the smellful
citj r into a dozen bands of bloodthirsty and bigoted par-
tisans once more. The game was the “ willingest ” seen
this season ; not a man on the ground seemed to know
what fear was, and the whole 40 rolled in with such vigor
that layings-out were plentiful and squarings-up the
accompaniment of every rally. At half-time, “ Darkey ’’
Ranks, the’Roy captain, was carried off, and Proudfoot, the
giant policeman who defends .the “Woodsey** goal was
chucking about the maroon men like mere drunks. The
10,000 spectators screeched in excitement and leaned over
the fence till they had pains in their stomachs. The
battle ended in the Collingwoods winning bv St points to
21, goals bc'ng even.

* * * *
In addition to being an army man himself, Capt.
Wynyard, one of Stoddart’s probables, comes of a military
family, not unknown here. Wynyard Square, Sydney,
the site of the old barracks, was named after one of his
forbears. In 1847, General E. B. Wynyard, com-
manded the troops of N.S.W., V.D.L., and M.L.; and,
later, in 1854-55, Lieut.-Col. H. K. Wynyard, was Gov.
of Maonland.
* * * *
Hayward, who played so well in last year’s test
matches, and who, it is cabled, comes with Stoddart, is of
delicate physique, and possibly unequal to the wear and
tear which a travelling- team must expect. Both as
bowler and batsman he is at the top of the tree, and his
fielding is phenomenal.

* * * *
Ned Triokett, ex-champion sculler, is, it is
plain, thoroughly Saved, judging from his autobiography
now being published in the War Cry. Describing his
race with Rush, he says, inter alia :—" At the first'part
of the course my opponent’s trainer, who was in a boat
pulled by seven men, was shouting to him all the time,
saying, That’s it, Michael! Good boy, Michael! You’ve
got him, Michael ! It’s all over with him, Michael! He’s
done, Michael ! You have him beat, Michael ! ’ and such-
like cheering expressions. But I knew better, and when
I shot past my opponent it took all the Michael out of
his trainer. It seems strange to look on it all now How
easy it is to get a crowd roaring with excitement over a
race, nearly wild to stake their money on the favorite
They think, poor souls, that such a life is far better than
one of joyful service to Christ. But we cannot blame
them so much, for we have been as bad. Lord, open their
eyes ! Be his experiences as a publican, the saved Ned
remarks “As soon as it became known that I wanted a
hotel I was besieged on all hands by unscrupulous agents
one of whom persuaded me to take an hotel and deposit
£BO on it. This I did, and repented of my action next
day, and thereby lost my £BO. At last I satisfied myself
and took premises which let me in for four or five hun-
“r®, pounds to fit up, but it was a good business stroke,
and Trickett s Hotel became the leading hotel of the
town, and for a time I did well. There is very little peace
however, for the worldling, and I soon found that to win
the championship was one thing, and to keep it another.”
“ At this time, if I had been well up in my
business I might have made my fortune selling drink ;
but I could not stand the confinement, and the tempta-
Don to drink was very great, so many wanting me to
drink with them. The fact was that Trickett failed as a
publican because the successful publican is born not
made. The typical successful publican is fat and broad
red and muscular; Ned Trickett was long, thin, sinewy
and painfully pale. When the boys who worshipped him
and who had travelled 1500 miles to see him and had
paid him the idolatrous compliment of naming’after him
their kangaroo dogs and the little blackboy whom thev
had captured on the Hellfire river, entered his bar and
asked him to have a drink, and held out their brown
hands in greeting, poor Ned looked black and sour at
them, and slowly, and with much evident reluctance ex-
tended to them a large, clammy hand, which felt like a
dead fish or a cold shoulder of mutton. And then the
boys didn’t come to see publican Ned Trickett any more
Ned was born for the Harmy, and he has got there
* * * *
The übiquitous bike has at last found its level
A couple of Prahran (Melb.) men have started a bike
merry-go-round, and are temporarily coining money
borne 30 bikes with wooden—in place of rubber—tyres are
fixed to frames, and run in grooves on a wooden staging
No driving-engine is needed, as the 3d. a time riders do
their own driving for about a mile at a ten-mile gait
when the proprietor puts on a brake. Girls seem to be
the best customers.

N.S. Wales has been laboring under a
delusion in imaginingthatit possessed Responsible
Government—v.e one answerable to the people.
cting-Premier Brunker has denied the impeach-
ment, and has raised the standard of Theocracy,
acknowledging no one but God as his master!
Mow will it be when the people actually endorse
this policy, and relinquish their claim to being
the principal source of power in the State by
transferring it to God, or, rather, to theindividual
conscience of the members of the God-fearing
Government ?
When Griffith’s Free Education Bill was
counted out in N.S.W. Assembly, the other night,
there were but eight Labor-men present, though
I'lee Education is a plank of the Labor platform 1
Ihe case stands this way. Absolutely free educa-
tion—the abolition of school-fees—would cost the
State A lO,OOO a year, and (as the S.M. Herald
has thoughtlessly pointed out) the Treasury can’t
afford this. Cos why? Because the Govt, has to
buy the support of the daily press by giving free-
postage to the newspapers at an expense of tens of
thousands of pounds annually.
One especial fool of an amendment on the
lederation Bill, carried by S.A. Council, reads
that Ihe senators shall be directly chosen by
the people of the State as each State shall deter-
mine. The theory is that it is each State’s own
funeral, and no one else’s funeral whatsoever, how
it elects its own representatives, and if they only
governed the State which elected them it would
be right enough. But each State will be governed
by the senators of every other State as much as
by its own, and therefore every State has as much
right to a say in fixing the franchise of the other
States as in fixing its own. A senate elected on
six distinct franchises ranging from aggressively
Tory to strongly Democratic, would be a fearful
example of the legislative Kilkenny cat.


Re N.S.W. Sheriff Maybury’s favored
dozen execution-witnesses, oughtn’t some M.P.
to ask for names and reasons why ? If the law
refuses to pander to the morbid curiosity of the
many, the sheriff should have no power to gratify
that of the few, especially as certain press repre-
sentatives were refused admission to Butler’s
hanging on the pretence that the function was to
be absolutely private.
£35, £34, £33, and so on, was the way
unbroken draught horses sold at Camperdown last
Wednesday. It’s a long time since these prices
have been seen before, and it looks like a revival
in the horse-raising industry. Certainly, as usual,
the auctioneer’s enthusiastic eloquence made the
most of the opportunity, Pitt, Son and Badgery
Ltd. being the salesmen.*
Hard to conceive anything more grotesquely
hideous than the hope expressed by Sheehan—
recently hanged in Maoriland that he would
“ meet in Heaven ” the girl he had ravished and
murdered —when, presumably, she would be de-
lighted to renew the acquaintance ! And this,
too, in a letter to “Dear Madam,” the victim’s
wretched mother. Correspondence-manuals will
hardly now be complete without “ Admonitory
epistle of saved murderer to unregenerate relatives
of murdered,” or “ Consolatory reply of parent to
destroyer of child.” The born snuffler and the
born criminal come together in their ghastly lack
of all sense of humor.

WONDERFUL
Blood-Purifying Effect
OF
Ayer’s Sarsaparilla
Mr. Charles Stephenson, a well-known
Railway Employe at Kalapoi, New
Zealand, writes:
“About ten years ago, while en-
gaged in shunting, my loot caught
between the rails, and my leg was
fractured below the knee. It healed
in time, but I have been troubled
ever since with swollen veins, and
have been obliged, at times, to wear
a bandage. About a year ago it be-
came much worse, and I feared I
should he obliged to give up my
work. A friend advised me to try
Ayer’s Sarsaparilla. I did so, and
after taking four bottles the swell-
ing disappeared, and I have not
been troubled with it since.”

The damages recently awarded (as pe r
cable) to an English war-correspondent for an inr
putation on his courage, will help further to swell
the heads of the high-falutin fraternity. The old-
fashioned war-scribe’s sole ambition was to get the
latest news earliest home, whereas his Jin-de-siecle
representative appears to look upon his chronicle j
as primarily a mere setting for his testimonials to
his own superhuman courage, and cleverness and 1
all the rest of it. Also, when he happens to be his
own artist, he’s pretty certain to figure extensively
in his own foregrounds, in an aggravated state of
beard, and boot, and belt, and pistol.
Dunedin (M.L.) had a real good Jubilee.
The little town didn’t make adequate preparation
for the crowd that swarmed into it, and after
every hotel and lodging-house was full, and every-
body who could be persuaded to take in a
boarder had taken him in, and all the people who
could shake down conveniently in railway and
tram-cars and arcades had shaken down, over
200 tired, cold jubilators dragged themselves and
their wives and little Johnny up and down the
streets all night with a bun in one hand and an
umbrella in the other, and had a good time.
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to j
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.*
A comic incident in Acting-Premier
Isaacs’s recent historic interview with the Melb.
unemployed concerned the ehueking-out of the
aged Anarchist, White. White had announced
that all Crown Ministers were thieves, and, as
Isaacs dramatically said, “ Constable Appleby, do
your duty,” a stalwart young man in plain clothes
seized the veteran “by the scruff.’’ A hat lay
on a table beside him, and this the officer jammed
on White’s head as he gently, but firmly, ejected
him from the building. Then the official chucker-
out returned to the Ministerial sanctum to get his
own hat, but a search of the whole place only re-
sulted in the finding of a battered billycock, which
Isaacs identified as belonging to White. Then
the fearful truth burst upon Appleby, and he
rushed into the street just in time to see the
white-haired revolutionary dancing on his hat, to
the plaudits of a couple of hundred proletarians
on the pavement.
The coolest cheek has been found at last—
and, of course, in Melbourne. Last Sunday week,
as the collection was progressing to slow music in
a fashionable suburban church, a well-dressed man,
with a pious complexion and holy whiskers, sig-
nalled to the official with the trencher. “ I wish
to donate 5s.,” he whispered, “but find I have
nothing less than .£1 on me ; would you mind
giving change?” “Certainly not; the Church
rarely gives change, but rather than lose 5s. —
why, of course.” The man took 15s., dropped a
pound-note into the plate, and the organ gave
thanks. Five minutes later it was found, when
the takings were counted behind the scenes, that
that pound note was a bad one, and quite unfit for
publication. And the pious stranger—where, oh
where was he?

The recent trouble in Greece, like the
French overthrow of 1870, proves the rottenness
of royalty. A dynasty, to save its own position
and emoluments, will do anything—regardless of
life, property and national intei-ests.
My wife : “ No, I’ll never sail again in a
boat where they carry a stewardess.” “Why
not ? ” “A steward is ever so much kinder, more
sympathetic. A steward always seems gentle, no
matter how sick I am.”

On Dunlop tyres F. W. Chinn has won
the English professional quarter and mile cham-
pionships, while T. Summergills and E. H. Ains-
worth have respectively won the amateur mile and
five miles. Cyclists should read the interesting
and instructive booklet, “All about Dunlop
Tyres.”
The Australasian is strong in natural
history. Speaking of wild turkeys, it says:
“Owing to the drought in N.S.W. a good num-
ber of the buzzard species have found their way
into Victoria, and they are being ruthlessly
destroyed. Steps are bring taken to prosecute
offenders.” Turkey buzzards ? Ye gods, what a
feast!
The locking of the Darling at Bourke
robbed the lower river of any advantage from the
June and early July rains, and the townships
along the stream, from Brewarrina down to
Wentworth, have been on short commons. Wil-
cannia, the biggest of these townships, was for a
week without such every-day articles as potatoes
and kerosene, and a sale of chaff was made at Alo
per ton. Momba, the biggest station in IN. o. W.,
on the river just out of Wilcannia, ran so short of
supplies that, in order to get them, a tram of oO
camels was sent into Broken Hill (the track being
too bad to allow of economical transport by bul-
lock-teams) with an out-loading of skins of sheep
slaughtered for the sake of their pelts during the
drought.
A Boon to Subt’eheks. —A well-known stock-
dealer and storekeeper announces that, if written
to confidentially, he will reply and state the plan
he pursued by which lie was restored to health
and vigor after years of suffering from Nervous
and Physical Weakness, Loss of Vigor, etc. He
writes : “ I have no scheme to extort money from
anyone whomsoever. I was victimised by quacks
and empirics until I lost faith in mankind ; but,
thank heaven, I am now well, vigorous, and
strong, and anxious to make this certain means of
cure known to all. Having nothing to sell, I
want no money.” Address—J. KILIAN, Store-
keeper, Daveyston, S.A. #

enhanced value of those left#
I have much pleasure in calling your attention to a paragraph in the Report informing you of our
worthy General Manager having completed fifty years of continuous service m the Bank. Your cor-
currenee will be asked to a minute passed by the Board on the subject, and a special resolution will be
Pro Pjt'will be the duty of the meeting to elect a Director, consequent upon the retirement, by rotation,
of Mr. Edward Knox, and to appoint Auditors for the ensuing year.
Mr. G. J. Cohen, Deputy-Chairman, having taken the chair, ,
Sir William Manning moved that Mr. Edward Knox be re-elected a Director of the Bank. Me
said he felt sure it was unnecessary to add any words to the reading of the resolution, and he though#
it would be carried unanimously.
Mr. L. P. Bain seconded the motion. . . , . 1r
The Deputy-Chairman having put the resolution to the meeting, it was carried unanimously.
Mr Knox said he had to express his thanks to the shareholders for having elected him to the
directorate for the tenth time. The first time he was elected there was so to speak, » different com
stituency, for the election took place in 1845, and it was in connection with the old_Bank. t hat
time it had been his good fortune to serve the Bank in different capacities, as Manager, Managing
Director, and Director. On this occasion he had some doubts as to whether he should come | or w a ,
for re-election. He did not think it might be fair to himself or to the Bank that at 79 years of age he
should take upon himself the responsibility of a further term of office, but he had come to the con-
clusion that he should do so ; still he wished to let them all know that if, from infirmity °™ny other
reason, he could not do the work he had hitherto done he would at once resign his <
to last there had been varying changes in the institution, but he thought that on the whole the share-
holders would admit that few establishments had been more satisfactorily conducted. (Hear, hear.;
Upon the motion of Mr. R. Binnie, seconded by Mr. L. P. Bain, Messrs J. De Ve Lamb and
E S Cape were elected Auditors. At this stage the General Manager was asked to retire.
Mr F H Dangar then said he had a resolution to-move which, he ventured to say, would meet
with the general approval of the meetiug. The motion was “ That this meeting entirely concurs in
the minute of the Board on the subject of the General Manager’s faithful and mmiose
Bank for the past 50 years, and places at the disposal of the Directors the sum of £:250 for the’ Pu r P° 8 e
of presenting Mr. Dibbs with a suitable memento of his jubilee. (Applause.) Fortunately this
resolution did not require many words from him to ensure its hearty approval by the meeting. He
considered that it was a great privilege that he should have been called upon to move it. His acquaint-
ance with Mr. Dibbs extended over 44 years, and for most of that time he had , , kn^ V* '.'®
business capacity. They all knew that he had always enjoyed the confidence of the Bank and teat
he was largely responsible for the present position of the institution. There was no doubt that t e
Directors were quite right in embodying the minute with reference to Mr. Dibbs m the leport. he
Directors were the best judges of what Mr. Dibbs had done, and it was now for the meeting toendoise
in a very emphatic manner the opinion which the Board had expressed. He did not suppose that any
of those present would care to offer a money contribution to Mr. Dibbs, and therefore they should
empower the directors to purchase some present which would mark their appreciation or the taithtui
services which he had rendered to the Bank. (Applause.) ,
Mr. James Stedman said it gave him very much pleasure to second the resolution. He hacl al ways
looked upon Mr. Dibbs as a perfect manager, and he thought they should all be thankful to Almighty
God that he had been spared for so many years. Apart from the fact that it was Mr. Dibbs s Jubilee
he thought he thoroughly deserved the recognition of his services which the motion implied. He
thought that if it were possible for every shareholder in the Bank to be present at the meeting, there
would not be one dissentient voice against the motion. (Applause.)
Sir William Manning said it was undesirable that this vote should be passed without iurther com-
ment and he wished to say a few words in accentuation of the minute which the Direetors had
embodied in their report with regard to Mr. Dibbs’s long and faithful services. Mr. Dibbs had served
the institution for a lifetime, and the great institution had grown and developed with the growth and
development of the colony. It must be patent to everyone present, and, indeed, to everyone who was
an observer of the financial affairs of Australia, that for any gentleman to be connected with an insti-
tution of such magnitude as the Commercial Banking Company of Sydney, in the capacity of manager
for such a length of time as Mr. Dibbs had filled that office, was positive proof that he possessed great
ability in matters of finance. They all knew' that the word “ banking ’’ had in these days a very wide
significance ; but unless they had capable men at the head of banking institutions in this young coun-
try their resources would not be developed as they should be. Further than that, Mr. Dibbs pos-
sessed that marvellous thing called tact, and, aided by the Directors, he had shown singular ability in
conducting the affairs of the institution. Not only did Mr. Dibbs possess great probity of character,
but he had that other great quality which English people called backbone. He had been connected
with the Bank when the colony had gone through great trials, and, though he had been known to
stand in fear and trembling, he had never lost his head, but had always had a thorough grasp of the
position and known what to do. He thought that the occasion was one calling for a very emphatic
expression of opinion, not only from shareholders, but from those who, like himself, represented
financial people both here and in England. ,
Mr. Josiah Mullens said that as an old friend of Mr. Dibbs he would like to endorse what had
been said with regard to his work in connection with the institution, and also to concur in the pro-
priety of the resolution which had been submitted for their approval. Mr. Dibbs was the
model managers of great institutions. He not only had plenty of backbone, but he could say ‘ No ”
when the occasion required him to do so. He could recall instances wherein Mr. Dibbs’s exercise of
this faculty had saved the Bank many thousands of pounds. He had also used a great deal of
discretion in the management of the general affairs of the Bank, particularly with respect to the
collecting of a staff of officers who were a credit to the institution. He not only enjoyed the affection
of his officers, but he could rely upon their zealously serving the interests of the Bank. He wished, of
conclusion, to say that he had watched the results 'which had followed from Mr. Dibbs s exercise in
the faculties he had mentioned, and had been filled with admiration for him.
The resolution, having been put to the meeting, was carried with acclamation.
Mr. Dibbs having entered the room, the Chairman said he had much pleasure in communicating
the words of the resolution to him.
Mr. Dibbs, in reply, said— , .. , .
Chairman and Gentleman,—l thank you very much for the congratulatory minute embodied m
the Report referring to my-fifty years’ service in the Bank, and also for the special resolution which
you have been good enough to pass so unanimously. It is very gratifying for me to know that in the
management of the Bank I have won the approval of the Shareholders and Directors. Fifty years of
continuous service in such an institution as the Commercial Banking Company of Sydney is indeed a
thing to be proud of. I can truly say that the service has been a pleasant one, and has been
made so by the consideration at all times shown to me by the Chairman and Directors of the Bank,
and by the valuable services freely given to me of a zealous and efficient staff of officers. I can only
say when the time arrived, as it would some day, when I could not cope with the affairs of the Bank
successfully, I would be the first to ask to be retired. I can assure you that nothing could have given
me greater pleasure than the vote that the shareholders have passed. I thank you. (Cheers.)
Mr. Josiah Mullens said he thought they should not close their meeting on this auspicious occasion
without passing a vote of thanks to the Directors. He thought he expressed the sense of the meeting
when he said that they always held them in grateful remembrance.
The motion having been seconded was carried by acclamation.
Tbe Chairman returned thanks for the vote. /

ITS RAVAGES ARE APPALLING-ONLY

THE SUFFERER TO HEALTH.
The people of this country have good cause to
view with alarm an outbreak of influenza, as it
leaves behind it more shattered constitutions than
any other known disease. Mr. Edward Botting,
for ten years a councillor of the united townships
of Bedford, Olden, and Palmerston, in Fontenac
country Ontario, Canada, is one who nearly fell a
victim to the scourge. To a Whig correspondent
Mr. Botting said :—“ About two years ago I had
a bad attack of influenza, and the after-effects of
that malignant trouble brought me so low that my
fri nds despaired of my recovery. I was troubled
with severe and constant pains in the back, sensa-
tions of extreme dizziness, weakness, and was in
fact in a generally-used-up condition. I had read
frequently of Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills, and felt
they must have some special virtues else they
could not obtain such strong endorsations in all
parts of the country. I determined to try them,
and I bless the day that I came to that conclusion.
Before the first boxwas finished Ifeltbenefited, and
I continued their use till I was as strong as ever.
I consider Dr. W illiams’ Pink Pills the best medi-
cine sold, and I would not be without them in the
house if they cost five dollars a box.”*

Jubilee item from an English paper :
Elizabeth R. Kenneally, the daughter of John Maloney,
the man who saved the Queen’s life prior to her accession
to the throne, is in very poor circumstances. Last year
when ill she wrote to the Queen asking for some assist-
ance ; but received a reply to the effect that the Queen
could do nothing for her. Those persons who are so
anxious that Victoria’s Diamond Jubilee should be fit-
tingly celebrated have thus an opportunity afforded them
of relieving the wants of the daughter of the man who
made that event possible. Her address is 63, Watson-
street, Deptford, London, S.E. Maloney, it may be men-
tioned, lies in a pauper’s grave in Brockley Cemetery.
Minister Peacock, of Victoria, is invited to pub-
lish the above in that beautiful concatenation of
ignorance and loyal slush, the School Paper.
George Westinghouse tried to sell his patent
railway brake for .£6OO, but could not find a pur-
chaser. In the subsequent ten years it brought
him in three millions of money. There are many
other things to be invented just as valuable as
a railway brake. But many inventors have lost
their patents through defective specifications,
claims, etc. This risk can be avoided by inventors
consulting Mr. Arthur Griffith (M.L.A.), Patent
Agent, Sydney, who will not only do the work
with exactitude, but at rates within the reach of
every inventor. For instance, £2O will secure
Provisional Protection in the whole of the seven
colonies of Australia and Great Britain, and
Foreign Patents can be obtained through him at
similarly reasonable rates.*
Madden, M.L.A. (Vic.), as a Tory of the
most infuriated type, is bitterly opposed to all
“tinkering with small reforms” and “grand-
motherly policy,” and so on. But Madden bought
some land and went in for farming as a hobby,
and being presumably swindled with bad seed he
came down to Parliament with an indignant Seed
Bill, dealing with the practice of selling stuff that
won’t grow. Now he seems to have been further
defrauded over some guano, and he has a bill
before the House providing that “ Where any
manure submitted for analysis as aforesaid is
found to contain less nitrogen, phosphoric acid or
potash than the proportions thereof respectively
declared in the invoice certificate, and such defi-
ciency as to nitrogen amounts to 1| per cent, of
the whole sample in manures declared to contain
less than 10 per cent, of nitrogen, or 15 per centum
of the total nitrogen declared to be present in
manures declared to contain more than 10 per
cent, of nitrogen, or as to phosphoric acid 3 per
cent.,” &c. t &c., then the consequences are to be
too dreadful for description.

The Horrors of Fat .
[For The Bulletin.]
“ An elderly man of independent means, named Thos.
Winnell, a retired contractor, was charged before Haw-
thorn court yesterday with attempting to commit suicide.
Winnell was so very corpulent that he could not stand
up. He had consequently grown tired of life, and cut his
throat almost from ear to ear with a razor. As his friends
undertook to provide a professional nurse to take care of
him, he was discharged. —Melbourne Paper.
Old Thomas Winnell blew his nose
And prayed, in weak despair—
“ O Lord, this tissue adipose
Is more than one can bear.
The less I eat the more I bloat,
Too fat am I to stand ;
Forgive me if I cut my throat
To reach the Better Land.
“ On earth I merely sit and sulk ;
But up above the skies
An angel may reduce his bulk
By flying exercise.
I cannot mix with mortals lean,
My solitude is drear ”...
The speaker seized his razor keen
And cut from ear to ear.
Tom Winnell, since you he not dead,
Repent! in pious haste.
How many brothers underfed
Would like to have your waist 1
This is a world of want and woe,
Where men loaf round about,
Craving, in vain, a chance to kno\V
'The joy of getting stout—
The joy of sitting like a toff
Upon a padded chair,
With lots of filling food to “ scoff,"
And woollen clothes to wear.
Your lazy, fat existence drags I
You dunno what he feels
Who lives and dies in draughty rags,
Devoid of mid-day meals.
Tom Winnell, place a podgy palm
Against your bulging vest,
And think with what internal calm
Obesity is blest.
Bid Nursey load the festive board
With dainties roast and fried,
And say this grace—“ I thank Thee, Lord—
I have no rats inside."


One of the Salvation Army’s trading con-
cerns—the tea-shop lately started in Prahran
(Melb.)—has closed up for want of custom.
N.S.W. railway authorities are adopting
stringent measures in re the holiday passes issued
to employes, which have of late been much abused.
A loco, employe who had jubilee-journeyed from a
far Western town to Sydney with his family
posted his pass to another resident of the town,
who, with his family, travelled as far as Strath-
field before the trick was discovered. Result, em-
ploye dismissed. Another case was that of a fire-
man, unmarried, who obtained pass for self and
wife, and (M.P.-fashion) travelled to Queensland
with a “lydy.” That fireman, it is ukased, will
never receive another pass while in Govt, employ-
ment. Other instances have occurred of employes
selling their passes.

The London Referee philosopher attri-
butes the Greek stampede at Larissa to the general
keenness of Greek intellect. Rare mental endow-
ments are not required of Tommy Atkins. He
isn’t even asked for moral courage. As the Ref.
man writes: “Let us not delude ourselves with
the idea that there is only one kind of courage.
What I have been describing is the courage of the
common soldier—-animal courage, the basis of
which I believe to be want of perception.” The
courage that may come to a passionate fool at any
time, though it can never animate a wise man
until he gets drunk, and is not likely to get hold
of him then. The courage that carries a plain
commercial person into the middle of a quarrel
before he has begun to remember that a pair of
black eyes may blight his career in life. Blind
courage !

Bananaland monthly statistics now include
a leper column. During April, the leper-stations
at Dunwich and Peel Island increased in popula-
tion by three, viz., one European, one Chinese and
one Kanaka. A cosmopolitan disease, evidently.

Teaching a Farmer .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I was up at Glen Innes last summer and called on
a farmer whose crops had been injured by rain
and frost. And, in order to hearten the poor
fellow, I pointed out that it was only a question
of time before his occupation would be gone.
“ You see,” I explained, “we already know all
the constituent parts of a cereal. When chemical
science can form a synthesis of these, food-pro-
ducts will be directly derived from the soil in
laboratories.”
il Now, just you clear ! ” said he, I won t have
none of you blanky book-agents about my place.
Git, you broody German ! ”
A book-agent! I was hurt.
“My dear sir,” I said, “you misapprehend
me.” , . , , , (T
“Ye’re a blooming liar ! ” cried Wapstraw, I
never laid a finger on yer !„ But if yer’ll put up
your blessed ’ands I’ll .”
I, with some presence of mind, hastened to
soothe the sore part, by applying whisky to the
patient, and when he had relapsed into his normal
state of tranquil idiocy I placed the fence between
us and bored in again. I wrestled with the matter,
put on the strangle-hold, and threw it repeatedly.
I appealed to him as a man of intelligence and
enterprise (the poor devil had never tried to grow
anything but wneat and spuds) to try and realise
the imminence of the danger that menaced the
back-bone of this great country. In the course of
lialf-an-hour I used up enough bram-tissue to over-
stock the N.S. W. junior Bar for 18 months.
“What will become of you,” I cried, quiver-
ingly, “ when we get a week’s supply of bread by
dropping into the chemist’s for a penn orth of
wheat-extract; or when a beef pellet swallowed
too quickly gives a swagman acute indigestion for
a fortnight ? ”
The cocky regarded me with a sadly critical look
in his little grey eyes, and sniffed despairingly at
intervals. , , , . ,
I pushed the question. I -showed that agricul-
tural lands would lose all value; that, if crops
grew of themselves, they wouldn’t pay to reap,
and that wheat would be down to a half-penny a
bushel. And all the time I rang “ synthesis
into him. I don’t quite know what it means, but,
if we would only speak of things we understood,
they’d never discuss Federation in our Parliament.
I’d slung “ synthesis ” at him 30 or 40 times and
was just getting through again when the cocky
spoke : ,
“ That’s all blanky fine,” he said. But s pose
you sows your gory sinisis, and s’pose we gets no
blooming rain and she don’t come up, what •
It was nearly sundown, and I pushed on towards
Glen limes.

steamer needs 30,000 h.p. engines and a daily
500 tons of coal to drive at 22 miles an hour
a mass of 20,000 tons’ displacement—carrying 450
of a crew, and accommodating 1600 passengers.
The leviathan has 24 boats, 68 subsidiary
engines, and breaks every record but that of
speed, which has now been wrested from the
Star by the just-la nched Turbinia—loo feet
