Land of the Crocodile.
[For Tub Bulletin.]
An ancient colonist told me the following
anecdote of Dr. Perry, first Bishop of Melbourne.
The A.C. was present on the occasion. Bishop P.
was on a visitation tour, and called at Redesdale,
near Beechworth. The usual procession of “ lead-
ing citizens ” acted as escort Curtis Reid and
John Reid were the owners of the station, and
John was acting as honorary protector of abori-
gines. When the bishop and party arrived, John
Reid was busy down at the washpool. Curtis
sent a blackfellow to bring him up. “ Mitha
Curtith want ’im you ’long houth; big fella
bithop come up,” said the Myall messenger.
John was covered with sweat, and grease, and
wool; and the temper of fiery Caledonian ances-
tors was at boiling-point as he replied “ Oh
the bishop ! ” The messenger returned and
delivered the reply verbatim to Curtis Reid in the
presence of the bishop and a goodly company ! ! !
A few smiled and the others felt ill.
* * * *
The thought of holy men recalls the departed
Rev. Peter Campbell, once bush-missionary of
Queensland. Peter stayed one night at Aramac
station. He arrived late, and ownerForsyth made a
shake-down on the dining-room floor. Before retir-
ing, he asked the Lord’s anointed if he would “take
a little whisky?” “Oh, dear me, no,” said the
abstemious apostle, clasping his paws and raising
his eyes ratterwards. “Oh, well, there’s the
decanter and some water if you should feel in-
clined for a little during the night.” Later on,
Forsyth went into Peter’s room to get the water-
jug, and lie found the jug and the whisky-decanter
both empty ! Likewise he found Peter sound
asleep, breathing and snoring like a pleuro-stricken
bullock, and the atmosphere of the room resembled
that of a distillery !
* * * *
Peter, in liis early, unregenerate days, had
won some fame as a boxer. On the way from
Aramac to Clermont his coach companion was
Edmund Rouse, manager of Culloden station.
Rouse was never remarkable for piety, or venera-
tion for “ Devil-dodgers.” He made various sar-
castic observations to Peter during the journey,
and, on arrival at a wayside pub., the warlike
evangelist invited him to “come down and have it
out.” This was a new phase of Christianity for
E.R., and after contemplating Peter’s broad
shoulders and the wild look in Peter’s eye, he
decided to adopt some Religion requiring less risk
in the process of conversion. Peter smiled a grim
smile, and remounted the coach, with a “Scots
wha hae ” expression that commanded respect for
the rest of the trip.
• * * *
Parry-Okeden, the present Q. Commis-
sioner of Police, is one of the first of the Men
from Snowy River.” He was born there on Mara-
numbla station in ’4l. The only available Lucina
was a faithful aboriginal woman. At three years
of age, he was carried on horseback to Rosevale
station, in Cippsland, where his father was the
first squatter to establish friendship with the wild
tribes. Young Okeuen was also taken on a visit
to Flinders Island, where he saw the last of the
Tasmanians—a forlorn group, seated on the sea-
shore, gazing, heartsick and homesick, at the far-
Off blue hills of that beloved native land which
they were never more to inhabit. #
Ex-Chief Justice Lilley, in his political
davs was present at a grand ball in Toowoomba.
One of the richest of the Darling Downs squatters
was standing near Lilley, as his wife went career-
ing past them in a waltz. Gazing at his fair part-
ner’s gorgeous and expensive costume, the jum-
buck magnate observed, “ Look there, Lilley (hie),
there gofs my Sal, with 20 (hie, blanky bullocks on
her back! ” , *
On another occasion, “Sal” was en g a S e^
at night in her dressing-room, when her maid had
only supplied one candle. Mi, tneie, rviaiy .
bring me six candles ! I cannot dress with less !
which earned her the name of 'Six-candle Sally,
still well-remembered by the old pioneers. But as
the lady’s descendants are swell society people
with an extensive bank-account, I may as well
stop here and avoid the risk of a blasphemy-pro-
secution. , ,
* * *
Another prominent Northern family de-
rived most of its coin from an eccentric bachelor
brother-in-law of the head of the house. We shall
call the aforesaid head “ old Mac, for the sake o
brevity. The brother-in-law we shall call Joe.
Joe came to reside with Mac, and fell ill with
some “curmurrin” in his epigastric region.
Thinking Joe was about to enter the Land ot
Shadows, Mac called on a local undertaker, and
bargained for a cheap coffin. The undertaker put
on all his store clothes and called at Mac s house
to measure the corpse. Mac met him at the door,
and remarked : “ Oh, he’s no dead yet; but he II
no be lang!” 0 „
At the Queensland Benevolent Asylum for
the aged, at Dunwich, on Stradbroke Island, a
humorist named Agnew is telegraph-master. Ho
was once, as “ Captain Agnew,’ commanding a
bloodthirsty squad of grammar-school boys in a
sanguinary battle where the opposing forces were
under drill-sergeant Grimes, a Hibernian warrior
with a rich and sonorous brogue. Agnew s Ber-
serkers got in a position where they were exposed
to a devastating fire from the enemy—a position in
which no mortal man could have lived for three
minutes. Agnew took all his men into the
trenches and told them to lie down and stop there
until further advised. Then he advanced alone,
in the form of a hollow-square, to meet the hostile
commander. Grimes, a strict disciplinarian,
called out in a voice of Kerry thunder : Captain
Agnew! I must have discipline, sorr ! Where
the divil are all your min ? ” Agnew saluted, and
in a sorrowful voice replied : “ I regret to say they
have all perished ! ” The battle being over, Grimes
rode proudly home leading the victorious troops,
followed a short time afterwards by Agnew at the
head of the dead. „
Agnew’s father was a well-known clergy-
man in Brisbane, and, about a month after his
arrival from England, while preaching in a local
church, he observed an old blackfellow stroll in,
sit on one of the front stools, and fix him with his
glittering eye all through the sermon— apparently
a most attentive listener. Agnew was delighted
with the effect of his eloquence! It had capti-
vated even the wild ’possum son of the primeval
past l The sermon ended, and the audience left
all but the blackfellow. Agnew was even more
delighted. He advanced to the artless child ot
the Stone Age, and observed, “You pleased with
my talk to-day?” “Yowi! My wud! you
yabba maroomba ! ” Maroomba indicated super-
lative excellence. Agnew continued “Next Sun-
day, you come back and hear some more ?
the noble savage, with great energy, replied, iNo
blanky fear! ” Agnew never afterwards enter-
tained the slightest idea of starting a mission to
the aboriginals.

The late Christopher Gorry was the ‘ ‘ father
of Ipswich,” and a genial Irishman with the soul
of honor, but he had not been educated at Dublin
University. Rising once to deliver an oration to
a public meeting he led off by saying, “ Gintle-
min ! Oim goin’ to undress the lot of yez on a very
sayrious subject ”! One morning he met O’Malley,
the C.P.S., now one of Queensland’s Civil Service
Commissioners. “ You know that little dog o
mine, O’Malley ? He got his tail jammed in the
gate to-day, and it’s broke ! Do you think it 11
ignite ? ” O’Malley expressed a serious belief
that it would if Gorry soaked it in kerosene. His
own girls could not resist a little fun with the old
man. They told him not to say “ put out that
light,” but “extinguish it.” He bad
called Tiny, and one day at dinner he said Will
one of you girrils extinguish Tiny ! ” But Gorry s
masterpiece was during a meeting between him-
self, P. O’Sullivan, and Father Horan. Ihe
latter had not been well for some time, hut was
unable to go for a holiday on account of the diffi-
culty of finding a substitute. He stated this in
reply to O’Sullivan’s advice to “take a change
somewhere.’’ Gorry, in a sudden burst of sym-
pathy, “ Bedad, your riverince must go and get
better somehow ! What the divil is to prevent
yez? Sliure Bishop O’Quinn can get a prostitute
to take yer place at any minnit ”! That is
authentic.

With Coal to Callao .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
They slewed her in to dump her load,
And cleaned her aft and fore ;
They turned her out to take the road
She’d taken oft before ;
All geared aloft, all free aloft, all tight and trim
below,
To take the road and make the road, the road to
Callao.
He kissed the girl ashore he’d found,
And said, “ You’ll never miss me;
You won’t start weepin’ if I’m drowned,
But kiss me, sweetheart, kiss me !
’Tis miles to go, long miles to go, eight thousand
miles or so,
With seas about and seas abeam, and coal to
Callao ! ”
Around his neck she twined her arms,
“ Luck speed you, Jack ! ” cried she,
“ And from the sea and all its harms
Come back some day to me.
I’ll wait for you, I’ll watch for you
Though well, dear lad, I know
There’s other girls and fairer girls—the girls of
Callao.”
He took his sheath-knife from his belt
And said, “ ’Fore God, my beauty,
Yon sun from out the sky may melt
But I won’t turn from dooty.
This lock o’ yourn, this curl o’ yourn,
Goes with me where I go—
Across the world, around the world, to Hell—or
Callao.”
Her eyes were like two shining stars
That sparkle through the rain.
All sail was bent upon the spars,
He kissed his love again.
’Twas “come aboard,” and “all aboard, and let
her shore lines go,
And take the road and make the road— the road
to Callao.”
They swung across Newcastle bar
And sou’ by east away ;
They saw the Cross hung out afar
Below the Milky Way,
They saw the land go down a-lee, and heard the
rollers go
Across the road, along the road, the road to Callao.
The sun came up on sixty days
And set on sixty nights ;
Beneath the star-lit heaven’s maze
She kept her course to rights,
And while the cool winds kissed her wings as
white as driven snow,
She drove the dancing spray ahead—laid down for
Callao.
They heaved her log for sixty days,
But on the sixty-first
Her greasy cargo went ablaze,
, And then the hatches burst!
Twas Man the pumps! All hands to pumps ;
and curse her as ye go ;
A broken ship, a burning ship, ten days from
Callao ! ”
They tied the air-pipes throat and neok
With canvas triple-fold,
Then passed the wet hose down the deck
To flood her flaming hold ;
All cursing hard, all praying Christ to heed them
in their woe
To bring their feared and sinful souls alive to
Callao !
The yellow smoke that trailed a-lee
It clouded in her wake,
The steam that tore the lashings free
Hissed like a scalded snake,
And, blinded, beaten, driven back, they watched
the Fire-Fiend grow,
And cursed the hour and damned the day they
sailed for Callao.
Death’s Angel bared his flaming sword
And smote her hip and thigh ;
Her foremast splintered by the board
Like twig three seasons dry ;
But when the mainmast crashed to port they
sweltered in their woe,
To see her useless boats drift by—ten days to
Callao !
The prisoned gas shot out aflame
And licked her mizzen yard ;
Her broken bones against her frame
Jammed home again and jarred ;
They flung the hurried scrawl adrift to let their
fellows know
What fate was theirs who’d fought with Death,
bound out for Callao !
She lurched abeam until the brine
Began to lap her rail,
Till Doom and she with level twine
Were reeving neck and tail,
l’hey dragged the rum-keg aft at that, and let the
liquor flow,
To die the death they had to die, ten days from
Callao.
But when she gulfed the water in,
And when her stern heaved clear,
With God’s good grace to shrive their sin,
They rose a British cheer—
Then choked like men who pay the debt all men
to Nature owe,
On either road, on every road ’tween this and
Callao.
They’re swinging coal aboard The Star
’Longside Newcastle quay,
And out across Newcastle bar
Far spreads the lonely sea ;
And Jack’s fond lass has found a friend to love
her ere he go
Along the road, the level road, the road to Callao.
E. J. Brady.
A hysterical Vic. parson, on Jubilee Sun-
day, suggested to his choir the intensely “ loyal ”
idea of holding flags (at ninepence each) in their
hands during divine service. The good shepherd
was crushed by a unanimous refusal.
F. P. M‘Cabe, the ex-manager of Mt.
Keira colliery, who died the other day, at Bowral,
unsuccessfully contested, in the early days, two
N.S W. Parliamentary elections with the learned
J. I. Hargrave. Hargrave became Solicitor-
General in the Forster Government and Attorney-
General under Cowper, afterwards going on to the
Supreme Court Bench as Equity and Divorce
Judge.

On May Ist, a law came into force in Ten-
nessee, U.S.A., absolutely prohibiting the sale of
cigarettes and cigarette-paper within the State.

warned Southern pastoralists against the use of

ago a large mob of cattle travelled from Norman-’

without losing a single hoof. Evidently all had
become tick-proof.
